Philosophers' Denial of Bodily Resurrection: The Decomposition Argument
Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument against bodily resurrection via decomposition and the first alternative of reassembly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' rational objection to bodily resurrection based on the decomposition and mixing of body parts, and their first alternative of gathering only the parts at death.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that biological decay makes physical resurrection rationally impossible.

In this passage from the Tahafut, al-Ghazali reconstructs the philosophers' argument against bodily resurrection. He first states their claim that descriptions of paradise are mere metaphors for spiritual rewards, then presents their logical proof: the body decomposes and its parts become irrecoverably scattered. He then examines the first of two theoretical responses to this problem, showing its absurd consequences. Understanding this argument is essential to grasping the core dispute between philosophy and Islamic theology on the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that Ghazali is not stating his own view but presenting the philosophers' reasoning.
The passage starts with Ghazali's note about the title of the 20th issue.
2
Identify the philosophers' core claim
Locate the sentence beginning with 'وأما تقدير بقاء النفس...' which states the impossibility of the soul returning to the same body due to decomposition and mixing.
This is the central premise of their argument.
3
Examine the first alternative
After mentioning that even if God's power could reassemble the body, the philosophers consider two possibilities. This passage only gives the first: gathering only the parts present at death, which leads to a defective body, unsuitable for paradise.
The second alternative (gathering new parts) is not covered in this segment.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why the philosophers present this as an objection: they aim to show that bodily resurrection leads to absurdities, making spiritual interpretation necessary.
This sets the stage for Ghazali's own refutation later in the Tahafut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاك أيضاً مثلاً آخر يتضح منه موقف الغزالى تجاه خصومه . لقد وجدت الغزالى - وأنا أقرأ التهافت لإعداده للنشر فى المرة الأولى - يقرر عنوان المسألة العشرين هكذا : [مسألة في إبطال إنكارهم لبعث الأجساد ، ورد الأرواح إلى الأبدان ووجود النار الجسمانية ، ووجود الجنة والحور العين ، وسائر ما وعد به الناس؛ إن كل ذلك أمثلة ضربت لعوام الخلق ، لتفهيم ثواب وعقاب روحانيين ، هما أعلى رتبة من الجسمانيين ] ثم يسوق الدليل التالى على لسان الفلاسفة ، تبريراً لإنكارهم البعث الجسماني : وأما تقدير بقاء النفس وردها إلى ذلك البدن بعينه ، فهو لو تصور لكان معاداً ؛ أى عوداً إلى تدبير البدن بعد مفارقته ؛ لكنه محال ؛ إذ بدن الميت يستحيل تراباً ، أو تأكله الديدان والطيور ، ويستحيل ماء و بخاراً وهواء ، ويمتزج بهواء العالم و بخاره ومائه ، امتزاجاً يبعد انتزاعه واستخلاصه. ولكن إن فرض إمكان ذلك ، اتكالا على قدرة الله تعالى فلا يخلو : إما أن يجمع الأجزاء التي مات عليها فقط ، فينبغي أن يعاد الأقطع الأنف والأذن ، وناقص الأعضاء ، كما كان ؛ وهذا مستقبح ، لا سيما في أهل الجنة ، وهم الذين خلقوا ناقصين في ابتداء الفطرة ، فإعادتهم إلى ما كانوا عليه من الهزال عند الموت ، في غاية النكال
Here is another example that clarifies al-Ghazali's stance toward his opponents. I found al-Ghazali—while I was reading the Tahafut to prepare it for publication the first time—setting down the title of the twentieth issue thus: 'Issue on refuting their denial of the resurrection of bodies, the return of souls to bodies, the existence of bodily fire, the existence of paradise and the wide-eyed houris, and all that people have been promised; [they claim] that all these are examples struck for the common folk to make them understand spiritual reward and punishment, which are of a higher rank than bodily ones.' Then he adduces the following argument on behalf of the philosophers as a justification for their denial of bodily resurrection: 'As for supposing the survival of the soul and its return to that same body, if it were conceivable it would be a return; i.e., a return to administering the body after separation; but it is impossible because the dead body turns into dust, or is eaten by worms and birds, and turns into water, vapor, and air, and mixes with the air, vapor, and water of the world in a mixture that makes its extraction and recovery improbable. But if its possibility is assumed, relying on God's power, then it would either: gather only the parts that existed at death—then the one with a cut nose and ear and missing limbs should be restored as he was; this is ugly, especially for the people of paradise, who were created deficient at the beginning of nature, so restoring them to what they were at death in emaciation is extreme punishment.'
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage begins with Ghazali's note that he is presenting another example illustrating his method. He states that he found Ghazali (himself) setting the title of the twentieth issue, which is about refuting the philosophers' denial of bodily resurrection. The title mentions that the philosophers claim that these are only examples for the common people. Then Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument: the return of the soul to the same body is impossible because the body decomposes and mixes with the world. Assuming it is possible by God's power, there are two alternatives. The first alternative is gathering only the parts present at death, which would result in a defective body, which is unbecoming especially for paradise. This is the first objection.

Key Terms
بعث الأجساد
إعادة الأرواح
معاد
استحالة
أجزاء
مستقبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is often read as al-Ghazali's own view, but he is presenting the philosophers' argument.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly says 'then he adduces the following argument on behalf of the philosophers' indicating he is reporting their reasoning, not endorsing it.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why is returning the soul to the same body impossible?
A) Souls are eternal and do not need bodies
B) The body decomposes and its parts become mixed with the world
C) God's power is limited
D) The soul cannot exist without the body

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that the decomposition of the body makes physical resurrection impossible, and even if God could reassemble it, restoring only the parts at death would lead to defective bodies unfit for paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever allowed rational difficulty—like the impossibility of a decomposed body being restored—to weaken your conviction in Allah's promise of bodily resurrection? What does this reveal about your trust in His power?
?
The philosophers claimed that the descriptions of paradise are merely examples for common people. In your heart, do you truly believe in the physical realities of Heaven and Hell, or have you reduced them to spiritual symbols that demand nothing of your daily choices?
?
If you were to stand before Allah with a body restored exactly as it was at the moment of your death (with all its flaws and weaknesses), would that feel like reward or punishment? How does this question shape your urgency to seek forgiveness and transformation now?

The Cannibalism Paradox
Ghazali explains the first impossibility of the second alternative: the cannibalism paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the cannibalism scenario poses a logical impossibility for bodily resurrection if all parts from a lifetime are gathered.

Core Idea
Cannibalism creates an irresolvable conflict: one body's matter cannot host two souls.

Ghazali presents the second alternative considered by the philosophers: gathering all body parts that ever existed in a person's lifetime. This leads to two impossibilities. The first involves the case where a person eats another person — the same matter becomes part of both bodies, making resurrection of both impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how Ghazali introduces the second alternative and then gives the first impossibility.
2
Identify the cannibalism scenario
Find the description of one person eating another. Understand that the same matter becomes part of two bodies.
The text mentions this is customary in some countries and during famines.
3
Understand the impossibility
Why can't both be resurrected? Because one matter cannot return two souls to one body. That would require two souls sharing a single body, which is impossible.
4
Connect to the larger argument
This is one of two impossibilities for the second alternative. The philosophers argue that gathering all parts from a lifetime leads to absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إن اقتصر على جمع الأجزاء الموجودة عند الموت وإن جمع جميع أجزائه التي كانت موجودة في جميع عمره ، فهو محال من وجهين : أحدهما : أن الإنسان إذا تغذى بلحم إنسان – وقد جرت العادة به في بعض البلاد ، و يكثر وقوعه فى أوقات القحط - فيتعذر حشرهما جميعاً ؛ لأن مادة واحدة ، كانت بدناً للمأكول ، وصارت بالغذاء بدناً للآكل ، ولا يمكن رد نفسين إلى بدن واحد. والثانى : أنه يجب أن يعاد جزء واحد ، كبداً ، وقلباً ، ويداً ، ورجلا ؛ فإنه ثبت بالصناعة الطبية أن الأجزاء العضوية يتغذى بعضها بفضلة غذاء البعض ، فيتغذى الكبد بأجزاء القلب ، وكذلك سائر الأعضاء . فنفرض أجزاء معينة قد كانت مادة الجملة من الأعضاء ، فإلى أي عضو تعاد ؟.
If he restricts himself to gathering the parts existing at death, [that's one case]. And if he gathers all his parts that existed throughout his entire life, it is impossible for two reasons: First, if a person ate human flesh—as is customary in some countries and often occurs during famines—then the resurrection of both becomes impossible because a single substance was the body of the eaten and became through nutrition the body of the eater, and it is impossible to return two souls to one body. Second, a single part must be restored as a liver, heart, hand, and foot; for it is established by medical science that organic parts are nourished by the surplus food of other parts; the liver is nourished by parts of the heart, and likewise all organs. So if we assume specific parts that were the material for the whole of the organs, to which organ would they be returned?
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage continues the philosophers' argument. After discussing the first alternative (gathering only parts at death), they consider the second alternative: gathering all parts that ever belonged to the person from his entire life. This leads to two impossibilities. The first is the famous 'cannibalism' problem: if one person eats another, the same matter becomes part of both bodies. Since each person must have a soul, resurrecting both with the same matter would require either that one body is incomplete or that two souls share one body, which is impossible. The second impossibility is based on medical theory: the idea that different organs consume parts of each other through nutrition. So a specific part could have been part of multiple organs over a lifetime; to which organ should it be returned? This creates a logical impossibility.

Key Terms
أجزاء
حشر
بدنة
تغذى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that resurrection is impossible altogether.
Correction: Ghazali is not proving resurrection impossible; he is showing a logical difficulty if one insists on gathering every atom that ever belonged to a person. The philosophers themselves are pointing out this difficulty in their own framework.

Checkpoint
Why does cannibalism make resurrection of both individuals impossible according to the passage?
A) The eaten person no longer exists
B) The same matter cannot belong to two bodies each with its own soul
C) The eater does not have a soul
D) The body of the eater decays

Key Takeaway
The cannibalism paradox shows that if matter is strictly recycled, the same matter cannot serve as the body for two different souls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have consumed many things in your life. If your resurrection depended on gathering every molecule that ever passed through your body, how would you face the impossible? Does this paradox force you to trust in a resurrection beyond human logic?
?
Consider the soul that defines you. Is it truly attached to your physical matter, or is it something deeper? How does this passage challenge you to see your identity as more than just your body?
?
If you were the eater in this paradox, would you be content to be resurrected without the matter you consumed? What does that tell you about the nature of resurrection and your reliance on Allah's perfect justice?

The Medical Paradox: Organs Consuming Each Other
Ghazali explains the second impossibility: the medical paradox of organ interdependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the medical theory of organ nutrition leads to a second impossibility for gathering all lifetime body parts.

Core Idea
If organs feed on each other's surplus, a single part could have been part of multiple organs, making reassignment impossible.

The philosophers present a second impossibility: based on medical science, organs are nourished by the surplus of other organs. This means a single particle could have been part of the liver, heart, hand, etc., at different times. When resurrecting all parts, to which organ should that particle be returned?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second impossibility
Read the second part of the passage, which starts with 'والثانى'. Notice how Ghazali draws on medical theory.
2
Understand the medical theory
The text claims that organic parts are nourished by the surplus food of other parts. For example, the liver is nourished by parts of the heart.
This was a common medical theory at the time.
3
Identify the problem
If a specific particle was the material for multiple organs over a lifetime, to which organ should it be returned when reassembling all parts?
The particle cannot be in two organs simultaneously.
4
Grasp the impossibility
This creates a logical contradiction: the same part would need to be assigned to different organs, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إن اقتصر على جمع الأجزاء الموجودة عند الموت وإن جمع جميع أجزائه التي كانت موجودة في جميع عمره ، فهو محال من وجهين : أحدهما : أن الإنسان إذا تغذى بلحم إنسان – وقد جرت العادة به في بعض البلاد ، و يكثر وقوعه فى أوقات القحط - فيتعذر حشرهما جميعاً ؛ لأن مادة واحدة ، كانت بدناً للمأكول ، وصارت بالغذاء بدناً للآكل ، ولا يمكن رد نفسين إلى بدن واحد. والثانى : أنه يجب أن يعاد جزء واحد ، كبداً ، وقلباً ، ويداً ، ورجلا ؛ فإنه ثبت بالصناعة الطبية أن الأجزاء العضوية يتغذى بعضها بفضلة غذاء البعض ، فيتغذى الكبد بأجزاء القلب ، وكذلك سائر الأعضاء . فنفرض أجزاء معينة قد كانت مادة الجملة من الأعضاء ، فإلى أي عضو تعاد ؟.
If he restricts himself to gathering the parts existing at death, [that's one case]. And if he gathers all his parts that existed throughout his entire life, it is impossible for two reasons: First, if a person ate human flesh—as is customary in some countries and often occurs during famines—then the resurrection of both becomes impossible because a single substance was the body of the eaten and became through nutrition the body of the eater, and it is impossible to return two souls to one body. Second, a single part must be restored as a liver, heart, hand, and foot; for it is established by medical science that organic parts are nourished by the surplus food of other parts; the liver is nourished by parts of the heart, and likewise all organs. So if we assume specific parts that were the material for the whole of the organs, to which organ would they be returned?
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage continues the philosophers' argument. After discussing the first alternative (gathering only parts at death), they consider the second alternative: gathering all parts that ever belonged to the person from his entire life. This leads to two impossibilities. The first is the famous 'cannibalism' problem: if one person eats another, the same matter becomes part of both bodies. Since each person must have a soul, resurrecting both with the same matter would require either that one body is incomplete or that two souls share one body, which is impossible. The second impossibility is based on medical theory: the idea that different organs consume parts of each other through nutrition. So a specific part could have been part of multiple organs over a lifetime; to which organ should it be returned? This creates a logical impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the medical theory mentioned, how are organs nourished?
A) Each organ produces its own food
B) Organs are nourished by the surplus of other organs
C) Organs do not need nourishment
D) The heart nourishes all organs directly

Key Takeaway
The medical paradox shows that if a single particle has been part of multiple organs, its reassignment becomes logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that your body is constantly exchanging particles. If you believe that God will resurrect you, what part of 'you' do you expect to be restored? Does this challenge your understanding of your own identity? Will you search the Quran for God's answer or simply trust His promise?
?
Ghazali uses medical science to reveal a logical contradiction in naive materialism. Are you willing to let your intellect be humbled by such paradoxes, or do you insist on a fully rational explanation for the resurrection? Commit to one position today.
?
If you cannot logically resolve how a particle that was part of many organs can be returned to one, does that weaken your faith in the resurrection? Or does it drive you to rely more on God's power beyond human logic? Choose a response and act on it—either by increasing your study of Islamic theology or by prostrating in humility.

The Recycling of Matter and the Impossibility of Infinite Souls
Ghazali argues that natural matter recycling leads to a third impossibility: infinite souls require finite matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the finite matter of the world cannot accommodate an infinite number of souls, leading to a third impossibility for bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
If matter is finite and souls are infinite, resurrection is impossible.

Ghazali extends his critique of the philosophers' view on resurrection by showing that the problem is not limited to cannibalism. Even the normal cycle of matter—soil to plants to animals to humans—means that the same physical matter has been the body of countless people. This leads to a logical contradiction when we consider the infinite number of souls. Let's examine how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage and the accompanying translation carefully. Notice how Ghazali describes the cycle of matter.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the conclusion: that the materials are insufficient for all people because souls are infinite and matter is finite.
3
Consider the philosophical implications
Think about why this is a third impossibility. How does it build on the earlier arguments?
This shows the depth of Ghazali's critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل لا يحتاج فى تقرير الاستحالة الأولى إلى أكل الناس الناس ؛ فإنك إذا تأملت ظاهر التربة المعمورة ، علمت بعد طول الزمان ، أن ترابها جثث الموتى قد تتربت وزرع فيها وغرس ، وصارت حبا وفاكهة ، وتناولتها الدواب ، فصارت لحماً ، وتناولناها فصارت أبداناً لنا . فما من مادة يشار إليها إلا وقد كانت بدناً لأناس كثيرين ، فاستحالت وصارت تراباً ، ثم نباتاً ، ثم لحماً ثم حيواناً بل يلزم منه محال ثالث ، وهو أن النفوس المفارقة للأبدان غير متناهية والأبدان أجسام متناهية ، فلا تفي المواد – التي كانت مواد الناس – بأنفس الناس كلهم ، بل تضيق بهم
Moreover, one does not need the eating of people by people to establish the first impossibility; if you contemplate the visible soil of the inhabited earth, you will know after a long time that its dust consists of the corpses of the dead that have become earth, been sown and planted, become grain and fruit, consumed by animals, become meat, then we consume them and they become our bodies. Thus there is no material substance that can be pointed to except that it has been a body for many people, then it changed and became dust, then plants, then meat, then animals. Rather, a third impossibility follows from this: that the souls separate from bodies are infinite, while bodies are finite substances, so the materials that were the materials of people are insufficient for all the people themselves; rather they are too narrow for them.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage extends the previous argument by showing that the problem is not limited to cannibalism. Even without it, the natural cycle of matter (soil, plants, animals, humans) means that the same physical matter has served as the body of countless people over time. Since each person has a soul, and souls are infinite in number (due to the eternity of the world or continuous creation), yet the matter is finite, it is impossible to assign unique matter to each soul. This is a third impossibility, adding to the earlier two. It shows the logical inconsistency in the idea of bodily resurrection with material continuity.

Key Terms
تربة
مادة
نفس
غير متناهية
متناهية
تضيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is invalid because it assumes an infinite number of souls.
Correction: Ghazali is not asserting that souls are infinite; he is showing that if one holds the philosophers' view that the world is eternal or that creation is continuous, then souls would be infinite, leading to a contradiction. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum against the philosophers' premises.

Checkpoint
Why does the recycling of matter through soil make bodily resurrection impossible according to the philosophers?
A) The matter is too scattered
B) The matter is impure
C) Finite matter cannot accommodate infinite souls
D) The bodies are not preserved

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's third impossibility shows that the finite material world cannot provide unique bodies for an infinite number of souls, thus undermining the philosophers' resurrection theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every atom in your body has been part of someone else's body, and will be again. Are you willing to let go of your attachment to your physical self and see it as a temporary loan from God? What keeps you from embracing that humility?
?
If your soul is unique and infinite, but the world of matter is finite and recycled, what does that say about where your true identity lies? When you stand before God, will you cling to the matter that is not yours, or to the soul He created?
?
Ghazali uses the cycle of matter to remind you that your body is not ultimately your own. How does that change the way you use your body today—in worship, in consumption, in pride or shame? Commit to one concrete act today that treats your body as a borrowed vessel.

Three Impossibilities in the Philosophers' Requirement for Bodily Resurrection
Ghazali presents three impossibilities that follow from the philosophers' requirement for bodily resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the philosophers' demand to gather all original parts for bodily resurrection leads to three impossibilities, and how this refutes their objection to bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
The philosophers' condition for bodily resurrection is self-defeating—it leads to logical absurdities.

Imam al-Ghazali here engages with the philosophers' objection that bodily resurrection is impossible because it would require gathering all the original parts of a person that existed throughout their life. Rather than disputing the need for original parts, Ghazali shows that the philosophers' own requirement entails three impossibilities, thus proving that the requirement itself is invalid. This passage is a masterful use of reductio ad absurdum in theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the impossibilities
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Try to note the three impossibilities Ghazali presents.
The passage begins with two clear impossibilities, then later introduces a third.
2
Examine the first impossibility: cannibalism
Focus on the first argument: if a person eats another human, the same material was the body of two different people. The philosophers' requirement would force two souls into one body, which is impossible.
Ghazali notes this occurs in famines.
3
Examine the second impossibility: organ nutrition
Medical science shows that organs feed on surplus from other organs. Thus a single part could have been material for multiple organs. The philosophers must decide which organ it belongs to, which is impossible.
This shows the requirement leads to contradiction even without cannibalism.
4
Understand the broader third impossibility
Ghazali extends the argument: over time, all soil contains decomposed bodies, which become plants, then animals, then human bodies. Every material has been part of countless people. Since souls are infinite and bodies finite, the materials cannot suffice for all souls. This third impossibility seals the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إن اقتصر على جمع الأجزاء الموجودة عند الموت وإن جمع جميع أجزائه التي كانت موجودة في جميع عمره ، فهو محال من وجهين : أحدهما : أن الإنسان إذا تغذى بلحم إنسان – وقد جرت العادة به في بعض البلاد ، و يكثر وقوعه فى أوقات القحط - فيتعذر حشرهما جميعاً ؛ لأن مادة واحدة ، كانت بدناً للمأكول ، وصارت بالغذاء بدناً للآكل ، ولا يمكن رد نفسين إلى بدن واحد . والثانى : أنه يجب أن يعاد جزء واحد ، كبداً ، وقلباً ، ويداً ، ورجلا ؛ فإنه ثبت بالصناعة الطبية أن الأجزاء العضوية يتغذى بعضها بفضلة غذاء البعض ، فيتغذى الكبد بأجزاء القلب ، وكذلك سائر الأعضاء . فنفرض أجزاء معينة قد كانت مادة الجملة من الأعضاء ، فإلى أي عضو تعاد ؟ بل لا يحتاج فى تقرير الاستحالة الأولى إلى أكل الناس الناس ؛ فإنك إذا تأملت ظاهر التربة المعمورة ، علمت بعد طول الزمان ، أن ترابها جثث الموتى قد تتربت وزرع فيها وغرس ، وصارت حبا وفاكهة ، وتناولتها الدواب ، فصارت لحماً ، وتناولناها فصارت أبداناً لنا . فما من مادة يشار إليها إلا وقد كانت بدناً لأناس كثيرين ، فاستحالت وصارت تراباً ، ثم نباتاً ، ثم لحماً ثم حيواناً بل يلزم منه محال ثالث ، وهو أن النفوس المفارقة للأبدان غير متناهية والأبدان أجسام متناهية ، فلا تفي المواد – التي كانت مواد الناس – بأنفس الناس كلهم ، بل تضيق بهم
This is if he restricts himself to gathering the parts present at death, but if he gathers all his parts that were present throughout his entire life, then it is impossible from two aspects: One of them: that if a person feeds on the flesh of a human – and it is customary in some countries, and its occurrence is frequent in times of famine – then the resurrection of both of them together becomes impossible; because one single material substance was the body of the eaten person, and then through nutrition became the body of the eater, and it is not possible to return two souls to one body. The second: that it would be necessary to return a single part as a liver, a heart, a hand, and a foot; for it is established by medical science that the organic parts feed on the surplus nutrition of some [other] parts, so the liver feeds on parts of the heart, and likewise the rest of the organs. So let us assume specific parts that were the material substance of the entirety of the organs, then to which organ are they returned? Rather, there is no need in establishing the first impossibility for people to eat people; for if you contemplate the apparent soil of the cultivated land, you would know after a long stretch of time that its soil is the corpses of the dead that have become dust, and were sown and planted in it, and became grain and fruit, and beasts consumed them, and so they became flesh, and we consumed them and so they became our bodies. For there is no material substance indicated except that it has been the body of many people, so it transformed and became dust, then plants, then flesh, then an animal. Rather, from it follows a third impossibility, which is that souls separating from bodies are infinite, while bodies are finite bodies, so the material substances – which were the materials of people – do not suffice for all people themselves, but rather fall short of them.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that bodily resurrection is impossible because it would require gathering all the original parts of a person that existed throughout their life. Ghazali presents three impossibilities that actually follow from the philosophers' own requirement, showing that their demand leads to absurdity, not resurrection itself.

First impossibility: If a person has eaten human flesh (as may happen in famine), the same material substance once belonged to two different persons. Since the philosophers require that each person's original parts be gathered, it becomes impossible to assign that material to both simultaneously. Two souls cannot be returned to one body.

Second impossibility: Even if we ignore cannibalism, medical science shows that organs feed on the surplus from other organs; for example, the liver feeds on parts of the heart. Thus, a specific part of the body may have been material for multiple organs. If we try to assign it to one organ, we must decide which one, which is impossible.

Ghazali then shows that the first impossibility does not even require cannibalism. Over time, the earth's soil itself consists of decomposed bodies; plants grow from it, are eaten by animals, and then we eat the animals. Thus, every material substance has been part of countless bodies. This leads to the third impossibility: since souls are infinite (in the sense of an endless succession of human souls) and bodies are finite, the available material substances cannot accommodate all the souls that have existed. Therefore, the philosophers' requirement that all original parts be gathered leads to an absurd conclusion, thus the requirement itself is invalid and bodily resurrection is not disproven.

Key Terms
بعث جسماني
أجزاء
مادة
نفس
استحالة
حشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali is arguing that bodily resurrection itself is impossible.
Correction: Ghazali is not arguing against resurrection; he is showing that the philosophers' requirement for gathering all original parts leads to impossibilities, thus the requirement itself is invalid and does not disprove resurrection.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how many impossibilities arise from the philosophers' requirement to gather all original parts for bodily resurrection?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the philosophers' condition for bodily resurrection is self-refuting, as it leads to logical impossibilities that do not undermine resurrection itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the philosophers' condition for resurrection is logically impossible, yet Allah promises to raise you exactly as you are, what does that say about your trust in His power over the most complex details? Do you truly believe He can resurrect you, or do you secretly doubt because of materialistic thinking?
?
The passage shows that material continuity is not necessary for identity. What does that mean for your hope of being reunited with loved ones who have died? How does this reshape the way you grieve and the way you live in anticipation of the next life?

The First Criticism: Ghazali's Unwarranted Certainty
The author raises the first criticism against Ghazali's accusation, highlighting Ibn Sina's contradictory statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first criticism against Ghazali's claim that Ibn Sina denied bodily resurrection, and why Ibn Sina's contradictory texts make such a definitive accusation problematic.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's texts affirm bodily resurrection—Ghazali chose to ignore that.

This passage presents a scholarly critique of Ghazali's handling of Ibn Sina's position on bodily resurrection. The author argues that Ibn Sina's texts are contradictory, but Ghazali arbitrarily picked the side that denies bodily resurrection while ignoring the clear affirmation. This lesson examines the first criticism: how can Ghazali be certain of Ibn Sina's denial when Ibn Sina both affirms and denies it in the same work?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author sets the context by describing Ibn Sina's division of the discussion on resurrection.
Pay attention to the words 'صرحاً واضحاً' and 'غموض والتواء' which indicate the contrast.
2
Identify Ibn Sina's two-part treatment
The author states that Ibn Sina split the inquiry into two parts: one on bodily resurrection (clear and affirming) and one on spiritual resurrection (vague and denying). Understand the significance of this division.
The first part is 'على اختصاره وقصره، صريحاً واضحاً' while the second is 'غموض والتواء'.
3
Locate the author's conclusion about contradiction
The author says the overall inquiry is 'متضارباً' (contradictory). Recognize that this contradiction is the basis for the criticism.
The word 'تضارب' is a key term meaning inconsistency.
4
Understand the first criticism
The author asks: if Ibn Sina is contradictory, how can Ghazali be certain that he denies bodily resurrection? Why does Ghazali take only one side and discard the other? This is the first criticism.
The criticism is that Ghazali's certainty is unwarranted given the evidence.

Source Text

كتاب التهافت التي يصرح الغزالى فيها بأن الفلاسفة أنكروا البعث الجسماني والتي يروى فيها دليلا، على لسانهم، يجعل وقوع البعث الجسماني أمراً مستحيلاً النص الكامل الوارد في النجاة بخصوص هذه المسألة. وفيه قسم ابن سينا البحث قسمين: خصص واحداً منهما للحديث عن البعث الجسماني، وقد جاء على اختصاره وقصره، صريحاً واضحاً، دالاً على أن صاحبه يؤمن بالبعث الجسماني إيماناً عميقاً، ويوقن به إيقاناً شديداً. أما ثانيهما: فقد خصصه لشرح البعث الروحاني وقد جاء مستفيضاً مطولاً، وفيه غموض والتواء، بل فيه عبارات تشير إلى نفي البعث الجسماني وهكذا ظهر البحث في جملته متضارباً، لكن تضارب ابن سينا وتعا رض موقفه بالنسبة للبعث الجسماني في النص الواحد، لم يصح - عندي - شفيعاً للغزالي في أمرين أخذتهما عليه: أولهما: أنه إذا كان ابن سينا متعارضاً يثبت البعث الجسماني وينفيه، فمن أين للغزالي القطع بأن ابن سينا ينكر البعث الجسماني؟ ولماذا يأخذ طرفاً معيناً من الطرفين اللذين يتردد بينهما ابن سينا ويسجله عليه، ويضرب بالآخر عرض الحائط؟
The book of Tahafut in which Ghazali declares that the philosophers denied bodily resurrection and in which he narrates a proof, on their tongue, that makes the occurrence of bodily resurrection impossible—the complete text found in al-Najat concerning this issue. And in it Ibn Sina divided the inquiry into two parts: he devoted one of them to discussing bodily resurrection, and it came, despite its brevity and shortness, explicit and clear, indicating that its author believes in bodily resurrection with deep faith and is strongly certain of it. As for the second: he devoted it to explaining spiritual resurrection, and it came extensive and lengthy, and in it there is obscurity and twisting, rather in it are expressions that indicate the denial of bodily resurrection. Thus the inquiry appeared in its entirety contradictory. But Ibn Sina's contradiction and inconsistency regarding bodily resurrection in the same text did not, in my view, serve as an advocate for Ghazali in two matters I hold against him: First: That if Ibn Sina is contradictory, affirming bodily resurrection and denying it, how can Ghazali be certain that Ibn Sina denies bodily resurrection? And why does he take a particular side from the two sides between which Ibn Sina wavers, and record it against him, and cast the other aside?
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly critique of Ghazali's handling of Ibn Sina's position on bodily resurrection. The author begins by summarizing Ibn Sina's treatment of the issue in his works: Ibn Sina split the discussion into two parts—one on bodily resurrection, which is clear and affirms it, and another on spiritual resurrection, which is vague and suggests denial of bodily resurrection. Thus Ibn Sina's overall position is contradictory. The author then states that this contradiction does not justify Ghazali's accusation; rather, it raises two problems. The first problem is that if Ibn Sina is contradictory, Ghazali cannot definitively claim that Ibn Sina denies bodily resurrection—he arbitrarily picks one side of Ibn Sina's statements. The author implies that Ghazali was unfair by ignoring the part where Ibn Sina affirms bodily resurrection. The second problem (mentioned but not elaborated here) is introduced as a separate point. The passage follows the author's own argumentative order: first setting up Ibn Sina's contradictory statements, then presenting the first criticism. The language is analytical and reflective, urging the reader to consider the evidence Ghazali used.

?

Ibn Sina's Two Accounts of Resurrection
Presentation of the two parts of Ibn Sina's resurrection view and their conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two conflicting accounts of resurrection found in Ibn Sina's works.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's resurrection doctrine splits between revealed body and rational soul.

This passage presents the two parts of Ibn Sina's view on resurrection. The first part adheres to Islamic revelation, affirming bodily resurrection. The second part follows reason, describing a spiritual resurrection where happiness depends on liberation from the body. The author then shows that the second part undermines the first.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two parts
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the word 'شطران' and the two explicit parts introduced by 'أحدهما' and 'ثانيهما'.
The first part relies on revelation, the second on reason.
2
Understand the second part's mechanism
Focus on how the second part explains happiness and misery: they depend on liberation from the body. The body prevents souls from feeling their true state.
This is a key point: souls feel their bliss or pain only when free from the body.
3
Reflect on the implication
The passage states that both kinds of souls leave their bodies without return. Ask yourself: does this leave room for bodily resurrection?
The author concludes this means denial of bodily resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا رأى ابن سينا في البعث، وهو - كما ترى - شطران: هو أحدهما: يرجع فيه إلى الشريعة الإسلامية، وما جاء فيها عن بعث البدن ونعيمه وعذابه، وقد آمن بكل ذلك وأذعن له. وثانيهما: يرجع فيه إلى العقل وما تأدى إليه من بعث الروح ونعيمها وعذابها وقد حكى كل ذلك أيضاً حكاية المذعن المؤمن والذي لا يستطيع المنصف أن يمارى فيه، أن ما جاء في الشطر الثاني يكاد يودى بما جاء في الشطر الأول، إذ قد جعل مناط السعادة والشقاوة في الخلاص من البدن، فالنفوس التي توافرت لديها أسباب السعادة، إنما كان يمنعها من الشعور بها البدن، فإذا خلعته وتخلصت منه، استذوقت سعادتها واستكملتها والنفوس التي توافرت لديها أسباب الشقاوة، إنما كان يحول بينها وبين الشعور بها، البدن وشواغله، فإذا ألقته جانباً، تأذت وتألمت ولقد ورد في عبارته ما يفيد أن كلا الصنفين من النفوس سيفارق بدنه إلى غير رجعة، ومعنى هذا إنكار البعث الجسماني، وما يترتب عليه من نعيم البدن وعذابه فهل كان (ابن سينا) يعنى ما جاء في الشطر الثاني وإنما ذكر الأول تقية؟ هذا محتمل، أم هو الاضطراب الذي كان ظاهرة شائعة في الفلسفة الإسلامية، من جراء إيمان أصحابها بمصدرين مختلفين، واعتقادهم فيهما العصمة والنزاهة عن الخطأ؟ إن كان الأول فلماذا لم يستشعر ابن سينا التقية في غير هذه المسألة، مما لا يقل خطره في نظر خصومه عن خطرها، كالقول بقدم العالم؟ وإن كان الثاني، فكيف غاب عنه هذا التناقض الواضح بين الجانبين، فإن كلا منهما ينفي ما يثبته الآخر؟ في الحق أن موقف ابن سينا في هذه المسألة غامض، ورأيه فيها مضطرب ولكن هل يحق للناقد المنصف أن يسجل عليه أحد الجانبين ويضرب بالآخر عرض الحائط؟ وإن حق له ذلك، فهل هو بالخيار بين أن يغفل أي الجانبين يشاء؟
This is Ibn Sina's view on resurrection, and it—as you see—has two parts: One of them: he refers in it to Islamic law, and what came in it regarding the resurrection of the body, its bliss and its punishment, and he believed in all that and submitted to it. And the second: he refers in it to reason and what it led to regarding the resurrection of the soul, its bliss and its punishment. And he narrated all that as well with the narration of a submissive believer—concerning which the fair-minded cannot argue—that what came in the second part almost destroys what came in the first part, because he made the criterion of happiness and misery in liberation from the body. So the souls that possess the causes of happiness, the body prevents them from feeling it; when they shed it and free themselves from it, they taste their happiness and complete it. And the souls that possess the causes of misery, the body and its preoccupations come between them and feeling it; when they cast it aside, they suffer and feel pain. And indeed it occurred in his expression that indicates that both kinds of souls will leave their body without return, and the meaning of this is the denial of bodily resurrection and what follows from it of bodily bliss and punishment. So was Ibn Sina meaning what came in the second part and only mentioned the first out of dissimulation? This is possible. Or is it the confusion that was a common phenomenon in Islamic philosophy, due to its adherents' belief in two different sources and their conviction that both are infallible and free from error? If the first, then why did Ibn Sina not practice dissimulation in other issues, no less dangerous in the eyes of his opponents than this, like the statement of the pre-eternity of the world? And if the second, then how did this clear contradiction between the two sides escape him, since each denies what the other affirms? In truth, Ibn Sina's position on this issue is ambiguous, and his view in it is confused. But is it permissible for a fair-minded critic to record one of the two sides against him and cast the other aside? And if that is permissible for him, does he have the choice to ignore whichever of the two sides he wishes?
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the two accounts of resurrection found in Ibn Sina's works. The author identifies two 'shatr' (parts): the first conforms to Islamic revelation, affirming bodily resurrection, bliss, and punishment; the second is based on reason and discusses the resurrection of the soul, making happiness and misery dependent on liberation from the body. The author then argues that the second account effectively undermines the first, because it states that souls never return to their bodies—implying a denial of bodily resurrection. This leads to a question: Did Ibn Sina intend the second account and only mention the first out of dissimulation (taqiyya)? Or was he genuinely confused due to relying on two supposedly infallible sources (revelation and reason) that seem to conflict? The author probes this dilemma by asking: if it was dissimulation, why did Ibn Sina not practice it on other issues like the pre-eternity of the world? If it was confusion, how did he miss such an obvious contradiction? The passage concludes by questioning whether a critic is justified in recording only one side of Ibn Sina's view and discarding the other. The commentary follows the author's sequential reasoning: first presenting the two parts, then showing the conflict, then raising the two hypotheses (dissimulation or confusion), then interrogating each hypothesis, and finally reflecting on the critic's fairness.

Key Terms
شطران
البعث الجسماني
البعث الروحاني

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina presents two incompatible accounts of resurrection: one affirming bodily resurrection, the other denying it by positing eternal separation of soul from body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
He relied on reason to define happiness as liberation from the body, contradicting revealed bodily resurrection. When your reason clashes with a clear teaching from Allah, which one do you ultimately trust? Are you ready to submit your intellect to the Qur'an and Sunnah even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
The passage says the fair-minded cannot argue that Ibn Sina's rational account undermines his revealed one. Have you ever been guilty of trying to harmonize incompatible ideas just to avoid inner conflict? What step will you take this week to honestly confront and correct any such contradiction in your beliefs?

Interpreting Ibn Sina's Contradiction: Dissimulation or Confusion?
Analysis of the possible reasons for Ibn Sina's contradictory accounts and reflection on the critic's role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's analysis of the possible reasons behind Ibn Sina's contradictory positions on resurrection.

Core Idea
The author questions whether Ibn Sina's contradiction stems from dissimulation or genuine confusion.

In this passage, the author probes deeper into Ibn Sina's inconsistency. He proposes two explanations: either Ibn Sina practiced dissimulation (taqiyya) by outwardly affirming bodily resurrection while secretly holding the rational view, or he was genuinely confused due to relying on two infallible sources. The author then challenges each explanation and reflects on the critic's responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two hypotheses
Read the Arabic passage. Look for the two possible explanations: dissimulation (تقية) and confusion (اضطراب).
The author asks 'فهل كان (ابن سينا) يعنى ما جاء في الشطر الثاني وإنما ذكر الأول تقية؟ أم هو الاضطراب...'
2
Examine the criticism of each hypothesis
The author challenges the dissimulation hypothesis by asking why Ibn Sina did not practice dissimulation on other issues like the pre-eternity of the world. He challenges the confusion hypothesis by asking how such a clear contradiction could escape him.
These criticisms suggest both possibilities are problematic.
3
Consider the author's conclusion about ambiguity
The author concludes that Ibn Sina's position is ambiguous and his view confused. This is not a definitive judgment but an acknowledgment of uncertainty.
The author says 'في الحق أن موقف ابن سينا في هذه المسألة غامض، ورأيه فيها مضطرب'.
4
Reflect on the final question about the critic's fairness
The passage ends with a question: can a fair critic record one side of Ibn Sina's view and discard the other? And if so, is the critic free to choose which side to ignore?
This challenges the reader to consider the ethics of interpreting ambiguous thinkers.

هذا رأى ابن سينا في البعث، وهو - كما ترى - شطران: هو أحدهما: يرجع فيه إلى الشريعة الإسلامية، وما جاء فيها عن بعث البدن ونعيمه وعذابه، وقد آمن بكل ذلك وأذعن له. وثانيهما: يرجع فيه إلى العقل وما تأدى إليه من بعث الروح ونعيمها وعذابها وقد حكى كل ذلك أيضاً حكاية المذعن المؤمن والذي لا يستطيع المنصف أن يمارى فيه، أن ما جاء في الشطر الثاني يكاد يودى بما جاء في الشطر الأول، إذ قد جعل مناط السعادة والشقاوة في الخلاص من البدن، فالنفوس التي توافرت لديها أسباب السعادة، إنما كان يمنعها من الشعور بها البدن، فإذا خلعته وتخلصت منه، استذوقت سعادتها واستكملتها والنفوس التي توافرت لديها أسباب الشقاوة، إنما كان يحول بينها وبين الشعور بها، البدن وشواغله، فإذا ألقته جانباً، تأذت وتألمت ولقد ورد في عبارته ما يفيد أن كلا الصنفين من النفوس سيفارق بدنه إلى غير رجعة، ومعنى هذا إنكار البعث الجسماني، وما يترتب عليه من نعيم البدن وعذابه فهل كان (ابن سينا) يعنى ما جاء في الشطر الثاني وإنما ذكر الأول تقية؟ هذا محتمل، أم هو الاضطراب الذي كان ظاهرة شائعة في الفلسفة الإسلامية، من جراء إيمان أصحابها بمصدرين مختلفين، واعتقادهم فيهما العصمة والنزاهة عن الخطأ؟ إن كان الأول فلماذا لم يستشعر ابن سينا التقية في غير هذه المسألة، مما لا يقل خطره في نظر خصومه عن خطرها، كالقول بقدم العالم؟ وإن كان الثاني، فكيف غاب عنه هذا التناقض الواضح بين الجانبين، فإن كلا منهما ينفي ما يثبته الآخر؟ في الحق أن موقف ابن سينا في هذه المسألة غامض، ورأيه فيها مضطرب ولكن هل يحق للناقد المنصف أن يسجل عليه أحد الجانبين ويضرب بالآخر عرض الحائط؟ وإن حق له ذلك، فهل هو بالخيار بين أن يغفل أي الجانبين يشاء؟
This is Ibn Sina's view on resurrection, and it—as you see—has two parts: One of them: he refers in it to Islamic law, and what came in it regarding the resurrection of the body, its bliss and its punishment, and he believed in all that and submitted to it. And the second: he refers in it to reason and what it led to regarding the resurrection of the soul, its bliss and its punishment. And he narrated all that as well with the narration of a submissive believer—concerning which the fair-minded cannot argue—that what came in the second part almost destroys what came in the first part, because he made the criterion of happiness and misery in liberation from the body. So the souls that possess the causes of happiness, the body prevents them from feeling it; when they shed it and free themselves from it, they taste their happiness and complete it. And the souls that possess the causes of misery, the body and its preoccupations come between them and feeling it; when they cast it aside, they suffer and feel pain. And indeed it occurred in his expression that indicates that both kinds of souls will leave their body without return, and the meaning of this is the denial of bodily resurrection and what follows from it of bodily bliss and punishment. So was Ibn Sina meaning what came in the second part and only mentioned the first out of dissimulation? This is possible. Or is it the confusion that was a common phenomenon in Islamic philosophy, due to its adherents' belief in two different sources and their conviction that both are infallible and free from error? If the first, then why did Ibn Sina not practice dissimulation in other issues, no less dangerous in the eyes of his opponents than this, like the statement of the pre-eternity of the world? And if the second, then how did this clear contradiction between the two sides escape him, since each denies what the other affirms? In truth, Ibn Sina's position on this issue is ambiguous, and his view in it is confused. But is it permissible for a fair-minded critic to record one of the two sides against him and cast the other aside? And if that is permissible for him, does he have the choice to ignore whichever of the two sides he wishes?
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the two accounts of resurrection found in Ibn Sina's works. The author identifies two 'shatr' (parts): the first conforms to Islamic revelation, affirming bodily resurrection, bliss, and punishment; the second is based on reason and discusses the resurrection of the soul, making happiness and misery dependent on liberation from the body. The author then argues that the second account effectively undermines the first, because it states that souls never return to their bodies—implying a denial of bodily resurrection. This leads to a question: Did Ibn Sina intend the second account and only mention the first out of dissimulation (taqiyya)? Or was he genuinely confused due to relying on two supposedly infallible sources (revelation and reason) that seem to conflict? The author probes this dilemma by asking: if it was dissimulation, why did Ibn Sina not practice it on other issues like the pre-eternity of the world? If it was confusion, how did he miss such an obvious contradiction? The passage concludes by questioning whether a critic is justified in recording only one side of Ibn Sina's view and discarding the other. The commentary follows the author's sequential reasoning: first presenting the two parts, then showing the conflict, then raising the two hypotheses (dissimulation or confusion), then interrogating each hypothesis, and finally reflecting on the critic's fairness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ibn Sina's position on resurrection is straightforwardly either for or against bodily resurrection.
Correction: The passage shows that Ibn Sina intentionally or unintentionally provides two contradictory accounts, making his position ambiguous and requiring careful interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one possible explanation for Ibn Sina's contradictory statements on resurrection?
A) He was a skeptic.
B) He was practicing dissimulation (taqiyya).
C) He changed his mind over time.
D) He never wrote about resurrection.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that Ibn Sina's position on resurrection is ambiguous and possibly contradictory, and it is not straightforward for a critic to judge which side to accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the author's question about whether a critic can choose which side to ignore, did you feel convicted of times you have dismissed evidence that contradicted your preferred narrative? How will you actively guard against that bias in your own study of conflicting opinions?
?
The author suggests that believing two different sources to be infallible can lead to confusion. In your own life, are there authorities—teachers, scholars, or even your own desires—that you treat as infallible, causing you to hold contradictory beliefs? What would it cost you to honestly confront that contradiction?
?
Ibn Sina's possible dissimulation raises the question: would you rather be known for a false consistency that pleases others, or for an honest confusion that seeks truth? Which have you chosen in the past, and which will you choose starting today?

Ibn Sina's Contradiction on Resurrection: Sharia vs Reason
Editor analyzes Ibn Sina's twofold view on resurrection and its apparent contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the apparent contradiction in Ibn Sina's treatment of resurrection and the two possible explanations offered by critics.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's two accounts of resurrection seem to contradict each other — was it dissimulation or confusion?

The editor of Ibn Sina's works presents a critical analysis of his view on resurrection. He shows that Ibn Sina offered two separate accounts: one based on Islamic law affirming bodily resurrection, and another based on reason describing resurrection of the soul. But these accounts appear mutually contradictory, raising questions about Ibn Sina's true belief and intellectual consistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of Ibn Sina's view
Read the passage to distinguish the first part (based on sharia, affirming bodily resurrection) from the second part (based on reason, focusing on the soul).
Notice that Ibn Sina narrates both as if he submits to them.
2
Understand the contradiction between them
The editor points out that the second part undermines the first by making happiness dependent on liberation from the body, implying eternal separation from the body.
This amounts to denial of bodily resurrection.
3
Consider the two possible explanations
The editor suggests either taqiyya (dissimulation) or confusion due to dual sources. Weigh the plausibility of each.
The editor questions why taqiyya would be limited to this issue if it were the case.
4
Evaluate the critic's dilemma
The passage ends by questioning whether a critic has the right to choose one side over the other, and if so, which side.
This highlights the ambiguity in Ibn Sina's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا رأى ابن سينا في البعث ، وهو - كما ترى - شطران : هو أحدهما : يرجع فيه إلى الشريعة الإسلامية ، وما جاء فيها عن بعث البدن ونعيمه وعذابه ، وقد آمن بكل ذلك وأذعن له . وثانيهما : يرجع فيه إلى العقل وما تأدى إليه من بعث الروح ونعيمها وعذابها وقد حكى كل ذلك أيضاً حكاية المذعن المؤمن والذى لا يستطيع المنصف أن يمارى فيه ، أن ما جاء في الشطر الثاني يكاد بودى بما جاء في الشطر الأول ، إذ قد جعل مناط السعادة والشقاوة في الخلاص من البدن ، فالنفوس التى توافرت لديها أسباب السعادة ، إنما كان يمنعها من الشعور بها البدن ، فإذا خلعته وتخلصت منه ، استذوقت سعادتها واستكملتها والنفوس التى توافرت لديها أسباب الشقاوة ، إنما كان يحول بينها وبين الشعور بها ، البدن وشواغله ، فإذا ألقته جانباً ، تأذت وتألمت ولقد ورد في عبارته ما يفيد أن كلا الصنفين من النفوس سيفارق بدنه إلى غير رجعة ، ومعنى هذا إنكار البعث الجسماني ، وما يترتب عليه من نعيم البدن وعذابه فهل كان ( ابن سينا ( يعنى ما جاء فى الشطر الثاني وإنما ذكر الأول تقية ؟ هذا محتمل ، أم هو الاضطراب الذى كان ظاهرة شائعة في الفلسفة الإسلامية ، من جراء إيمان أصحابها بمصدرين مختلفين ، واعتقادهم فيهما العصمة والنزاهة عن الخطأ ؟ إن كان الأول فلماذا لم يستشعر ابن سينا التقية في غير هذه المسألة ، مما لا يقل خطره فى نظر خصومه عن خطرها ، كالقول بقدم العالم ؟ وإن كان الثاني ، فكيف غاب عنه هذا التناقض الواضح بين الجانبين ، فإن كلا منهما ينفي ما يثبته الآخر ؟ في الحق أن موقف ابن سينا في هذه المسألة غامض ، ورأيه فيها مضطرب ولكن هل يحق للناقد المنصف أن يسجل عليه أحد الجانبين ويضرب بالآخر عرض الحائط ؟ و إن حق له ذلك ، فهل هو بالخيار بين أن يغفل أى الجانبين يشاء ؟
This is Ibn Sina's view on resurrection, and it is - as you see - two parts: one of them: it refers to Islamic law (shari'a) and what has come in it concerning the resurrection of the body and its bliss and torment, and he believed in all of that and submitted to it. And the second of them: it refers to reason ('aql) and what it led to regarding the resurrection of the soul and its bliss and torment, and he narrated all of that also as a narration of a submitting believer. And the impartial person cannot dispute that what came in the second part almost destroys what came in the first part, since he made the criterion of happiness and misery in liberation from the body. So the souls that have gathered in themselves the causes of happiness, what prevented them from feeling it was the body; so when they shed it and free themselves from it, they taste their happiness and complete it. And the souls that have gathered in themselves the causes of misery, what prevented them from feeling it was the body and its preoccupations; so when they cast it aside, they are harmed and pained. And in his expression there occurred what indicates that both classes of souls will depart from their bodies never to return, and the meaning of this is the denial of bodily resurrection and what follows from it of bodily bliss and torment. So was Ibn Sina meaning what came in the second part and only mentioned the first out of dissimulation (taqiyya)? This is possible. Or is it the confusion that was a common phenomenon in Islamic philosophy, due to its adherents' belief in two different sources and their conviction in their infallibility and freedom from error? If it is the first, then why did Ibn Sina not feel the need for dissimulation in other matters no less serious in the view of his opponents than this one, such as the statement of the pre-eternity of the world? And if it is the second, then how did this obvious contradiction between the two sides escape him, for each of them negates what the other affirms? In truth, Ibn Sina's position on this matter is ambiguous, and his opinion on it is confused. But does the impartial critic have the right to record one of the two sides against him and discard the other? And if he has that right, does he have the choice to ignore whichever side he wishes?
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by the editor of Ibn Sina's twofold treatment of resurrection. The editor begins by identifying two distinct parts in Ibn Sina's view: the first relies on Islamic law (shari'a) and affirms bodily resurrection, bliss, and torment; the second relies on reason (al-'aql) and discusses the resurrection, bliss, and torment of the soul. Ibn Sina narrates both as if he submits to them. However, the editor points out a clear contradiction: the second part effectively undermines the first because it makes happiness and misery dependent on liberation from the body, implying that souls depart from their bodies forever—denying bodily resurrection. The editor then raises two possibilities: either Ibn Sina was practicing taqiyya (dissimulation) by publicly stating the sharia-based view while secretly holding the rationalist view, or he was genuinely confused due to his commitment to two different authoritative sources (revelation and reason). The editor questions why taqiyya would be limited to this issue if it were the case, and if confusion, why the contradiction went unnoticed. He concludes that Ibn Sina's position is ambiguous and confused, and questions the critic's right to choose one side over the other.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's two accounts of resurrection are compatible and consistent.
Correction: The rational account undermines the sharia account by making happiness depend on liberation from the body, implying denial of bodily resurrection, creating a contradiction.

Checkpoint

A) It affirms bodily resurrection more strongly than sharia.
B) It denies bodily resurrection by making happiness depend on liberation from the body.
C) It reconciles reason and revelation perfectly.
D) It is identical to the sharia account.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's view on resurrection presents a contradiction between affirming bodily resurrection in the sharia account and denying it in the rational account, leaving his true position ambiguous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an Islamic truth you profess with your lips but act against with your life? What would it cost you to align your actions with that truth today?
?
When you encounter a contradiction between what revelation says and what your reason suggests, do you dismiss the tension or wrestle with it until you find clarity? What one step will you take this week to confront such a conflict in your own heart?
?
Ibn Sina may have hidden his belief to avoid persecution. Are there situations where you downplay your faith to fit in or avoid discomfort? How does that compromise your integrity before Allah?

Ibn Sina's Dilemma on Bodily Resurrection
Ibn Sina presents a dilemma on the impossibility of bodily resurrection from the same matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Ibn Sina uses to argue against the possibility of bodily resurrection from the same matter.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's dilemma: returning to death-matter mutilates; returning to life-matter contradictions.

This lesson examines a key argument from Ibn Sina against bodily resurrection. He presents a dilemma: if souls return to the same matter, then either that matter is only from the moment of death (leading to resurrecting mutilated bodies) or it is all matter from the whole life (leading to one body having many contradictory parts). Both options are absurd, so bodily resurrection is impossible—at least from the same matter. This passage is part of Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa, where he reports and then refutes the philosophers' arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the logical structure: the author presents a series of conditional statements.
The proof is cut off at the page break, but the main dilemma is clear.
2
Identify the first dichotomy
Find where Ibn Sina states the first choice: souls return to the same matter or to another matter. Understand that the addressees (those who affirm bodily resurrection) choose the first option.
3
Unpack the second dichotomy
Within 'same matter,' there are two possibilities: only the matter at death, or all matter from the entire lifespan. Ibn Sina argues both lead to unacceptable consequences.
4
Analyze the consequences
For the first sub-option: a mutilated person would be resurrected mutilated (ugly). For the second: the same body would need to be multiple contradictory parts at once (impossible).
5
Consider the refutation context
Remember that Imam Ghazali is narrating this argument before refuting it. This lesson only covers the argument itself, not Ghazali's response.
In the Tahafut, Ghazali goes on to challenge the premises of this proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إما أن تكون النفوس تعود إلى المادة التي فارقتها . أو إلى مادة أخرى . وقيل من حكاية مذهب المخاطبين بهذه الفصول : إنهم يرون عودها إلى تلك المادة بعينها ، فحينئذ لا يخلو : إما أن تكون تلك المادة هي المادة التى كانت حاضرة عند الموت . أو جميع المادة التي قارنته جميع أيام العمر . فعلى الأول : أى إن كانت المادة الحاضرة حالة الموت فقط ، وجب أن يبعث المجدوع ، والمقطوع يده فى سبيل الله ، على صورته تلك ، وهذا قبيح عندهم . و إن بعث جميع ذلك أجزائه ، التى كانت أجزاء له مدة عمره ، وجب من أن يكون جسد واحد بعينه يبعث يداً ، ورأساً ، وكبداً ، وقلباً . وذلك لا يصح ;
Either the souls return to the matter they left, or to another matter. And it was said from the narration of the doctrine of those addressed by these chapters: they hold that they return to that same matter. Then there is no alternative: either that matter is the matter that was present at death, or all the matter that accompanied it all the days of life. On the first: i.e., if it is only the matter present at the time of death, then it would be necessary that the mutilated one and the one whose hand was cut off in the path of God should be resurrected in that very form, and this is ugly in their view. And if he is resurrected from all those parts, which were parts of him for the duration of his life, then it would be necessary that one and the same body should be resurrected as a hand, a head, a liver, and a heart. And that is not valid.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof presented by Ibn Sina against bodily resurrection, as quoted from his manuscript. The proof is structured as a dilemma. First, he presents a dichotomy: either souls return to the same matter they left, or to different matter. Those who affirm bodily resurrection (the addressees) believe they return to the same matter. Then another dichotomy: either that same matter is just the matter present at the moment of death, or it is all the matter that constituted the body throughout its entire life. If it is only the matter at death, then a person who was mutilated (e.g., lost a hand) would be resurrected with that mutilation, which is considered ugly/improper (qabih) by the opponents. If it is all matter from the whole life, then one and the same body would simultaneously be resurrected as a hand, head, liver, heart, etc. (since those parts were part of the body at different times)—an absurd impossibility. Therefore, bodily resurrection is impossible. The proof is cut off here at the page break, but it likely concludes that since both options are unacceptable, bodily resurrection cannot occur.

Key Terms
المادة
النفوس
المعاد
البعث
المجدوع
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that this snippet fully disproves bodily resurrection on its own.
Correction: This argument is part of a larger debate; Ghazali will later refute it. The passage only presents Ibn Sina's first set of objections.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma Ibn Sina presents regarding the return of the soul to the same matter?
A) The soul returns to matter present at death, which would require resurrecting mutilated bodies as they are.
B) The soul returns to matter from all of life, leading to one body being multiple parts at once.
C) The soul returns to a different matter entirely.
D) The soul does not return at all.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's argument uses a dilemma to show that bodily resurrection from the same matter leads to absurd consequences, either resurrecting mutilated bodies or contradictory body parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina argues that being resurrected with a mutilated body would be 'ugly' (قبيح). Can you honestly say that you have never felt ashamed or disgusted by your own physical appearance or limitations? If you were resurrected exactly as you are now—flaws, sins, and all—would that be a source of hope or terror? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in God's mercy?
?
The dilemma shows that clinging to the identical particles of your body leads to logical absurdities. Yet, you often cling to your identity, your past, and your physical self as if they define you. Are you willing to let go of your attachment to this material world and trust that God can recreate you in a way that is better and more just? What would that require from you today?
?
Imagine a loved one who died after a long illness that disfigured them. If they were resurrected in that same state, would you question God's wisdom? How does your answer to this hypothetical challenge the strength of your faith? Write down one concrete step you can take to deepen your reliance on God's power over the resurrection.

The Dilemma of Bodily Resurrection
Ibn Sina poses the basic dilemma and the first horn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's initial dilemma about whether souls return to the same matter or another, and the problem with returning only to the matter present at death.

Core Idea
Resurrecting only the matter at death leaves amputees permanently deformed.

Ibn Sina begins his critique of bodily resurrection by setting up a logical dilemma. He forces the defender of bodily resurrection to choose between two options, each leading to a problematic conclusion. This first part examines the horn where only the matter present at death is returned.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation together. Notice how Ibn Sina structures the dilemma with clear alternatives.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يخلو' (it is not free from) which signals a mandatory choice.
2
Identify the first horn
Locate the condition where the matter is only the matter present at death. What consequence does Ibn Sina draw?
The consequence is an ugly resurrection for amputees.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this would be considered 'ugly' in their view. What does this tell us about their assumptions about bodily resurrection?

.
و وجب أن يكون الإنسان المغتذى من الإنسان - في البلاد التي يحكى أن غذاء الناس فيها الناس - إذا نشأ من الغذاء الإنساني ، أن لا يبعث ؛ لأن جوهره من أجزاء جوهر غيره .
وتلك الأجزاء تبعث في غيره ، أو يبعث هو وتضيع أجزاء غيره ، فلا يبعث ؟ فإن أجبت بأن المعاد ، إنما هو بالأجزاء الأصلية ، وهى الباقية من أول العمر إلى آخره ، لا الأجزاء على الإطلاق ، وهذا الجزء فضلة في جميع الإنسان ، إن أكله فلا يجب إعادة فواضل المكلف ، ثم إن كان من الأجزاء الأصلية للمأكول أعيد فيه وإلا فلا
و إن قالوا إن المبعوث من أجزائه أجزاؤه التى تصلح بها حياته ، فلا خلاص فيه ؛ لأنها قد تربت ، وتساوت فى أن يكون بعضها مقوماً للحياة ، وبعضها نافعاً غير مقوم، وصار البعث عن ذلك التراب وعن تراب غيره سواء لا فرق فيه ؛ فقد رفعوا حكم العدل الذي يراعونه في بعث أعضاء البدن ، إلا أن يجعلوا للأجزاء المخصوصة بالبعث خصوصية المعنى زائد عليها ، وهو أنها في حال الحياة الأولى كانت مادة للأجزاء المقومة للحياة ، فيكون القول بذلك هو تحكم ، لا فائدة فيه ولا جدوى ، بوجه من الوجوه : أعنى تخصيص بعض أجزاء الأعضاء المتشابهة ، بالبعث ، دون بعض ، هو القول بتصيير عدم معنى كان سبباً في استحقاق شيء لمعنى دون غيره ، وحال العدم الكائن ، والممكن الكون ، الغير الكائن في المادة القابلة لها ، واحدة وأنت إذا تأملت وتدبرت ، ظهر لك أن الغالب على ظاهر التربة المعمورة جثث الموتى المتربة ، وقد حرث فيها وزرع ، وتكون منها الأغذية ، وتغذى بالأغذية جثث أخرى ، فأنى يمكن بعث مادة ، كانت حاصلة لصور في إنسانين في وقتين ، لهما جميعاً فى وقت واحد بلا قسمة (١) ؟]
(۱) رسالة أضحوية في أمر المعاد ، ص ٥٥، ٦ ، ۷ طبع دار الفكر العربي سنة ١٩٤٩ .
[It is not free from:
Either the souls return to the matter they separated from,
Or to another matter.
And it was said from a narration of the doctrine of those addressed by these chapters: they hold that they return to that same matter. Then it is not free from:
Either that matter is the matter that was present at death,
Or all the matter that accompanied it all the days of life.
On the first: i.e., if it is the matter present at death only, then it would be necessary that the amputee and the one whose hand was cut off in the way of God be resurrected in that same image, and this is ugly in their view.
And if all its parts that were parts for it during its lifetime are resurrected, then it would be necessary that one and the same body be resurrected as a hand, a head, a liver, and a heart. That is not valid;
Because it is established that organic parts always transfer into one another in nutrition; some are nourished from the surplus nourishment of others.
And it would be necessary that a human nourished from a human - in the lands where it is told that the food of people is people - if he grows from human nourishment, that he not be resurrected; because his substance is from parts of the substance of another.
And those parts are resurrected in another, or he is resurrected and the parts of another are lost, so he is not resurrected? For if you answer that the return is only by the original parts, which remain from the beginning of life to its end, not parts absolutely, and this part is surplus in all of a human; if he eats it, it is not necessary to return the surpluses of the morally responsible person. Then if it is from the original parts of the eaten, it is restored in him, otherwise not.
And if they say that what is resurrected of his parts are his parts which are suitable for his life, there is no escape in it; because they have become dust, and become equal in that some are life-sustaining and some are beneficial but not sustaining, and the resurrection from that dust and from dust of another becomes the same with no difference; so they have removed the rule of justice that they observe in resurrecting the organs of the body, unless they assign to the parts specifically for resurrection a specificity of meaning additional to them, which is that in the first life they were matter for the life-sustaining parts. Then such a statement is arbitrary, with no benefit or usefulness in any way: i.e., specifying some parts of the similar organs for resurrection over others is a statement making the non-existence of a meaning a cause for something to deserve a meaning over another, and the state of non-existent being, possible existence, and non-existent in the matter receptive to it is one. And if you reflect and ponder, it becomes apparent to you that most of the inhabited earth's surface is the bodies of dusty dead, and it has been plowed and sown, and from them come nutrients, and other bodies are nourished by the nutrients. So how can matter that was attained by forms in two humans at two times be resurrected for both of them at one time without division? (1)
(1) Risala Adhawiyya on the Matter of the Afterlife, pp. 55, 6, 7, Dar al-Fikr al-Arabi printing, 1949.]
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof by Ibn Sina against the possibility of bodily resurrection. It is structured as a dilemma. First, souls either return to the same matter or another matter. The opponent claims return to the same matter. Then that matter is either only what was present at death or all matter over the lifetime. If only the matter at death, then a person missing limbs would be resurrected missing limbs, which is considered ugly. If all matter over the lifetime, then because organic parts constantly change through nutrition, the same body would have to be multiple organs simultaneously, which is impossible. Furthermore, cannibalism leads to entanglement of parts. If one human eats another, the parts are shared. Even if one says return is by 'original parts' that persist, that is vague. The argument ends with a practical observation: the earth is filled with mixed remains, making resurrection of a specific matter for multiple individuals impossible.

Key Terms
بعث جسماني
المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina denies all resurrection entirely.
Correction: Ibn Sina is only criticizing a specific naive version of bodily resurrection, not resurrection per se.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what is problematic about returning only the matter present at death?
A) It leads to multiple bodies
B) It would cause amputees to be resurrected with missing limbs
C) The matter is too old
D) It contradicts divine power

Key Takeaway
The first horn of Ibn Sina's dilemma shows that returning only the matter at death leads to an imperfect resurrection, which is unacceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina's dilemma forces you to examine what 'your body' really means. If you were resurrected with only the matter from your deathbed, you might be incomplete. Have you ever considered that your sense of self might survive even if your current physical form is not restored? How does this challenge your attachment to your body?
?
The passage calls a deformed resurrection 'ugly'. Does that align with your concept of divine justice? Could an all-powerful God still create a perfect world even if some bodies are imperfect? What does this say about your expectations of the afterlife?
?
This argument uses logic to question a theological belief. Are you willing to let reason challenge your deeply held assumptions about resurrection? If Ibn Sina's pointer shows a flaw, what is your intellectual duty in response?
