The Problem of Changing Matter
Ibn Sina argues against resurrecting all matter from life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina uses the constant change of body parts through nutrition to argue that returning all matter from one's lifetime is impossible.

Core Idea
Body parts constantly change; resurrecting all matter would create a single body with multiple identities.

Having rejected the first horn, Ibn Sina now turns to the second: the return of all matter that composed the person over their entire life. He shows that because of nutrition and the transfer of parts, this leads to contradictions, especially in cases like cannibalism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Ibn Sina uses the example of nutrition to show parts transfer.
The phrase 'الأجزاء العضوية دائماً ينتقل بعضها إلى بعض في الاغتذاء' is key.
2
Understand the cannibalism example
Consider the scenario where one human eats another. How does this complicate resurrection?
The parts of the eaten become part of the eater, creating a dilemma: whose body do they belong to in resurrection?
3
Trace the logical conclusion
If all matter from life is resurrected, the same body would have to be multiple organs simultaneously, which is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ إنه لا يخلو :
إما أن تكون النفوس تعود إلى المادة التي فارقتها .
أو إلى مادة أخرى .
وقيل من حكاية مذهب المخاطبين بهذه الفصول : إنهم يرون عودها إلى
تلك المادة بعينها ، فحينئذ لا يخلو :
إما أن تكون تلك المادة هي المادة التى كانت حاضرة عند الموت .
أو جميع المادة التي قارنته جميع أيام العمر
·
فعلى الأول : أى إن كانت المادة الحاضرة حالة الموت فقط ، وجب أن يبعث المجدوع ، والمقطوع يده فى سبيل الله ، على صورته تلك ، وهذا قبيح
عندهم .
ذلك أجزائه ، التى كانت أجزاء له مدة عمره ، وجب من
و إن بعث جميع أن يكون جسد واحد بعينه يبعث يداً ، ورأساً ، وكبداً ، وقلباً . وذلك لا يصح ؛

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof by Ibn Sina against the possibility of bodily resurrection. It is structured as a dilemma. First, souls either return to the same matter or another matter. The opponent claims return to the same matter. Then that matter is either only what was present at death or all matter over the lifetime. If only the matter at death, then a person missing limbs would be resurrected missing limbs, which is considered ugly. If all matter over the lifetime, then because organic parts constantly change through nutrition, the same body would have to be multiple organs simultaneously, which is impossible. Furthermore, cannibalism leads to entanglement of parts. If one human eats another, the parts are shared. Even if one says return is by 'original parts' that persist, that is vague. The argument ends with a practical observation: the earth is filled with mixed remains, making resurrection of a specific matter for multiple individuals impossible.

Why does Ibn Sina think resurrecting all matter from one's life is impossible?
A) Because matter is corrupted

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina shows that the constant flux of body parts through nutrition makes the resurrection of the complete lifetime matter impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that your physical body is in constant flux. If your identity is not tied to the matter you're made of, what does that mean for how you view your own body? Will you still cling to it as 'you'?
?
Imagine that a part of your body once belonged to someone else through the food chain. How does that challenge your sense of ownership and uniqueness? Can you still say 'my body' with the same certainty?
?
The impossibility of resurrecting all matter forces us to think about what truly defines us. If not the physical particles, then what? Are you ready to accept that your true self is something beyond the material?

The Incoherence of Original Parts
Ibn Sina refutes the 'original parts' response and gives the dust argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's criticism of the 'original parts' solution and his final argument from the mixing of remains in the earth.

Core Idea
The notion of 'original parts' is arbitrary and the mixing of remains in the earth makes bodily resurrection impossible.

After raising the problem of changing parts, Ibn Sina anticipates a possible response: that resurrection is by 'original parts' that persist from birth to death. He shows this is an arbitrary stipulation. Then he delivers a final blow: the earth is filled with mixed remains, making it impossible to assign specific matter to each soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the flow from the 'original parts' response to the dust argument.
The passage ends with a rhetorical question emphasizing impossibility.
2
Deconstruct the 'original parts' response
Ibn Sina dismisses this as arbitrary (تحكم). Why does he consider it arbitrary?
Because it assigns special status to some parts without basis.
3
Examine the dust argument
The final argument observes that dead bodies mix with the earth and become part of other bodies. How does this make resurrection of specific matter impossible?
The same matter may have been part of multiple individuals, so it cannot be uniquely assigned to one.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if they say that what is resurrected of his parts are his parts which are suitable for his life, there is no escape in it; because they have become dust, and become equal in that some are life-sustaining and some are beneficial but not sustaining, and the resurrection from that dust and from dust of another becomes the same with no difference; so they have removed the rule of justice that they observe in resurrecting the organs of the body, unless they assign to the parts specifically for resurrection a specificity of meaning additional to them, which is that in the first life they were matter for the life-sustaining parts. Then such a statement is arbitrary, with no benefit or usefulness in any way: i.e., specifying some parts of the similar organs for resurrection over others is a statement making the non-existence of a meaning a cause for something to deserve a meaning over another, and the state of non-existent being, possible existence, and non-existent in the matter receptive to it is one. And if you reflect and ponder, it becomes apparent to you that most of the inhabited earth's surface is the bodies of dusty dead, and it has been plowed and sown, and from them come nutrients, and other bodies are nourished by the nutrients. So how can matter that was attained by forms in two humans at two times be resurrected for both of them at one time without division? (1)
(1) Risala Adhawiyya on the Matter of the Afterlife, pp. 55, 6, 7, Dar al-Fikr al-Arabi printing, 1949.]
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof by Ibn Sina against the possibility of bodily resurrection. It is structured as a dilemma. First, souls either return to the same matter or another matter. The opponent claims return to the same matter. Then that matter is either only what was present at death or all matter over the lifetime. If only the matter at death, then a person missing limbs would be resurrected missing limbs, which is considered ugly. If all matter over the lifetime, then because organic parts constantly change through nutrition, the same body would have to be multiple organs simultaneously, which is impossible. Furthermore, cannibalism leads to entanglement of parts. If one human eats another, the parts are shared. Even if one says return is by 'original parts' that persist, that is vague. The argument ends with a practical observation: the earth is filled with mixed remains, making resurrection of a specific matter for multiple individuals impossible.

Key Terms
المعاد
الأجزاء الأصلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Sina consider the 'original parts' solution arbitrary?
A) Because original parts are not identifiable
B) Because it assigns special significance to some parts without justification
C) Because God cannot resurrect original parts
D) Because original parts change over time

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina concludes that no coherent notion of bodily resurrection can escape the problems of part identity and mixing of remains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that the dust of your body has been part of countless others before you, does it shake your confidence that Allah can resurrect you uniquely? What would it take for you to trust His power over matter completely?
?
Ibn Sina's argument forces you to choose between logical coherence and divine omnipotence. Are you willing to affirm resurrection as a miracle beyond human reasoning, or do you still seek a logical explanation? How does this tension affect your daily conviction?
?
If the matter of your body is not exclusively 'yours,' what does that mean for your sense of self before Allah? Will you live today as though your identity is defined by your soul's relationship with Him, not your physical parts?

Reason and Revelation: Ghazali and Descartes
Compares Ghazali and Descartes on reason and revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the parallel between Ghazali and Descartes in restricting reason's role in matters of revelation.

Core Idea
Ghazali and Descartes both placed revelation beyond reason's reach.

This brief passage draws a striking parallel between two great thinkers from different traditions. Despite their different contexts, both Ghazali and Descartes agreed that the truths of revelation cannot be accessed by human reason alone but require divine grace. Here we explore their shared conviction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then its English translation. Let the author’s comparison sink in before analyzing.
The Arabic is short but dense.
2
Identify the central claim
The text reports that Descartes removed revelation from reason’s domain. Note the reason: truths of revelation are only known through divine grace (‘imdad’).
This mirrors Ghazali’s own stance on samiyyat.
3
Connect to Ghazali’s view
Recall that Ghazali also distinguished between rational knowledge and revealed knowledge. This passage claims the two thinkers resembled each other in this respect.
Both limit reason, but do not reject it.
4
Reflect on the term ‘imdad’
The Arabic word ‘imdad’ means divine assistance or grace. Consider why revelation would require this rather than rational proof.
This highlights the epistemological boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ب ) وكما تشابه الغزالى وديكارت فى أمر المعرفة ، فقد تشابها أيضاً في أمر آخر هو موقف العقل من الوحى أما ديكارت فقد روى عنه أنه : 1 نحى حقائق الوحى عن مجال العقل لأنها - فى رأيه - لا تدرك إلا بمدد Lectures on metaphysics and Logic. Vol. IV. p. 67-80 Published in 1861. (1)
(B) And just as Ghazali and Descartes resembled each other in epistemology, they also resembled each other in another matter: the position of reason regarding revelation. As for Descartes, it is reported of him that: 'He removed the truths of revelation from the domain of reason because – in his opinion – they are only known through divine grace.' Lectures on Metaphysics and Logic. Vol. IV. p. 67-80 Published in 1861. (1)
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This brief passage draws a comparison between Ghazali and Descartes. Both held that revelation is not subject to rational evaluation; rather, its truths are known only through divine grace (or 'imdad' meaning assistance). The text reports that Descartes set aside the truths of revelation from the realm of reason. This parallels Ghazali's distinction between rational knowledge and revealed knowledge.

Key Terms
الوحى
العقل
ديكارت
الغزالى
المدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ghazali and Descartes rejected reason entirely.
Correction: They only limited its scope in matters of revelation, affirming reason in other domains.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what did Descartes believe about the truths of revelation?
A) They can be known through reason.
B) They are known only through divine grace.
C) They are illusory.
D) They are the highest truths of reason.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali and Descartes both held that revelation is beyond rational evaluation and requires divine grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own spiritual life, do you tend to treat the truths of revelation as open to your rational judgment, or do you accept them as beyond your reason? Which tendency does this lesson challenge you to adopt?
?
If divine grace is required to know the truths of revelation, have you ever humbly asked God for that grace? Or have you relied on your own intellect to understand matters of faith?
?
Are there any commands or beliefs in your faith that you have struggled to submit to because they seem 'irrational' to you? How does the example of Ghazali and Descartes push you to trust revelation over your own reasoning?

Refutation of Plato's View on the Unity of Souls
Ghazali refutes Plato's claim that the soul is one and divides through bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two-pronged refutation of Plato's claim that all souls are one in essence and only divide through bodies.

Core Idea
If souls were identical, self-awareness and knowledge differences refute it.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a subtle theological error: the idea that all human souls are essentially one, only becoming many through bodies. The objection raises Plato's authority, but Ghazali responds with two devastating arguments from rational necessity. Let's see how he dismantles this view using self-awareness and the impossibility of dividing a non-physical entity.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the Platonic objection
The objector presents Plato's view: the soul is eternal and one, but divides in bodies, reuniting after death.
This is a common philosophical position Ghazali aims to refute.
2
First refutation: identity vs self-awareness
Ghazali asks: are Zayd's soul and 'Amr's soul identical? If yes, each person would not feel distinct; but each knows they are not the other. Also, they would share knowledge, which is false.
This appeals to direct human experience.
3
Second refutation: division requires physical quantity
If they say souls are distinct but divided from one, Ghazali argues that division of something without size or quantity is impossible by reason. Only physical objects can divide and reunite.
This shows the logical incoherence of the Platonic position.
4
Synthesize the refutation
Ghazali concludes that Plato's view is contrary to rational necessity on both counts: self-awareness and the nature of division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فالصحيح رأى أفلاطن وهو أن النفس قديمة وهي واحدة وإنما تنقسم في الأبدان ، فإذا فارقتها عادت إلى أصلها واتحدت . قلنا : فهذا أقبح وأشنع ، وأولى بأن يعتقد مخالفاً لضرورة العقل ، فإنا نقول : نفس زيد عين نفس عمر و أو غيره ؟ ! فإن كان عينه فهو باطل بالضرورة ، فإن كل واحد يشعر بنفسه ، ويعلم أنه ليس هو نفس غيره ، ولو كان هو عينه لتساويا في العلوم التي هي صفات ذاتية للنفوس داخلة مع النفوس في كل إضافة وإن قلتم : إنه غيره ، وإنما انقسم بالتعلق بالأبدان، قلنا : وانقسام الواحد الذى ليس له عظم فى الحجم وكمية مقدارية ، محال بضرورة العقل ، فكيف يصير الواحد اثنين ، بل ألفاً ثم يعود ويصير واحداً ، بل هذا يعقل فيما له عظم وكمية
For if it is said: The correct view is that of Plato, which is that the soul is eternal and is one, and it only divides in bodies, and when it departs from them it returns to its origin and becomes united. We say: This is uglier and more hideous, and more deserving of being considered contrary to the necessity of reason. For we say: Is the soul of Zaid the very same as the soul of 'Amr or another? For if it is the very same, that is false by necessity, because each one is conscious of himself and knows that he is not the soul of another. And if it were the very same, they would be equal in the sciences which are essential attributes of souls, entering with the souls in every relation. And if you say: It is other, and it only divided by attachment to bodies. We say: The division of one that has no size in volume and quantitative magnitude is impossible by the necessity of reason. How can one become two, indeed a thousand, then return and become one? Rather, this is conceivable only in what has size and quantity.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objector raises Plato's view as an alternative: that the soul is eternal and one, but it divides through attachment to bodies, and upon separation it reunites. Ghazali immediately declares this 'uglier and more hideous' and even more opposed to rational necessity. He then presents two refutations. First, if all individual souls are the same soul, then Zayd's soul would be identical to 'Amr's soul. But this is false by necessity because each person is self-aware and knows he is not another. If they were identical, they would share all knowledge, which is not the case. Second, if the objector says the soul is other (distinct) but became many by dividing through bodies, Ghazali argues that division of a non-physical entity (having no size or quantity) is impossible by rational necessity. How can one become two or a thousand and then reunite? Such division and reunion only makes sense for physical objects with size and quantity. Thus Plato's view is untenable.

Key Terms
نفس (soul)
قديم (eternal)
انقسام (division)
عظم (size)
كمية (quantity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Plato's view that souls are one and divide through bodies is plausible.
Correction: Ghazali shows it contradicts self-awareness (each person knows they are distinct) and requires physical properties for division, which a non-physical soul lacks.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the consequence if the soul of Zaid is identical to the soul of 'Amr?
A) They would have different bodies but the same soul.
B) They would have equal knowledge and self-awareness would be shared.
C) The soul would become divisible.
D) The soul would cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes Platonic unity of souls by showing identity contradicts self-awareness, and division of a non-physical entity is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own self-awareness, do you truly believe your soul is distinct from every other soul? How does that conviction affect your sense of personal responsibility before Allah, knowing that no one else can bear your burdens?
?
Ghazali argues that if souls were identical, then knowledge would be the same in all people. In what ways do you take your own learning and understanding for granted? How does this argument motivate you to seek knowledge as a unique, personal obligation?
?
The impossibility of dividing a non-physical soul shows that each soul is an indivisible whole. Examine your life: Do you divide your spiritual attention among many worldly pursuits as if your soul could be fragmented? How can you strive to keep your soul whole and focused on Allah?

The Philosophers' Objection: Infinite Souls Cannot Exist
Ghazali presents the philosophers' objection and their two positions on souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that an infinite number of souls is impossible, and the two solutions they proposed to avoid this consequence.

Core Idea
Philosophers denied infinite souls by making souls either one eternal or dependent on bodies.

The philosophers raised a powerful objection: if souls are many and persist after death, given their belief in infinite cycles of world generation, there would have to be an infinite number of souls. This they considered impossible. To salvage their cosmology, they proposed two alternative views on the nature of the soul. In this passage, Ghazali presents their objection and these two positions, setting the stage for his response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core objection
Read the passage carefully and identify what problem the philosophers see in infinite souls. Note that they distinguish between mental supposition and real existence.
The objection is based on the combination of many souls persisting and infinite cycles.
2
Understand the first solution
Some philosophers said all souls were one eternal entity before bodies and reunite after death, thus no multiplicity exists.
This avoids infinite number by denying plurality of souls entirely.
3
Understand the second solution
Other philosophers said the soul is dependent on the body's temperament and perishes with it, so souls only exist for the living, who are finite.
This avoids infinite number by denying that souls persist after death.
4
Reflect on the philosophers' motive
Consider why the philosophers felt the need to propose these solutions. What assumption about cycles do they hold that creates the problem?
They believe in infinite cycles of generation and corruption of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانت المقدرات أيضاً بعضها علل لبعض ، فالإنسان قد يفرض ذلك في وهمه ، وإنما الكلام في الموجود في الأعيان ، لا في الأذهان . فلا يبقى إلا نفوس الأموات وقد ذهب بعض الفلاسفة إلى أنها كانت واحدة أزلية قبل التعلق بالأبدان وعند مفارقة الأبدان تتحد ، فلا يكون فيها عدد ، فضلا أن توصف بأنها لا نهاية لها ، وقال آخرون : النفس تابعة لمزاج البدن ، وإنما معنى الموت عدمها ، ولا قوام لها بجوهرها دون الجسم ، فإذن لا وجود للنفوس ، إلا في حق الأحياء ، والأحياء الموجودون محصورون ، ولا تنتفى النهاية عنهم ، والمعدومون لا يوصفون أصلاً ، لا بوجود النهاية ولا بعدمها إلا في الوهم ، إذا فرضوا موجودين . والجواب : أن هذا الإشكال في النفوس ، أوردناه على ( ابن سينا ) و « الفارابي » والمحققين منهم ، إذ حكموا بأن النفس جوهر قائم بنفسه ، وهو اختيار أرسطاليس الأوائل ؛ ومن عدل عن المسلك فنقول له : هل يتصور أن يحدث شيء يبقى أم لا ؟ ! فإن قالوا : لا ، فهو محال ، وإن قالوا : نعم ، قلنا : فإذا قدرنا كل يوم حدوث شيء وبقاءه ، اجتمع إلى الآن لا محالة موجودات لا نهاية لها ، فالدورة وإن كانت منقضية ، فحصول موجود فيها يبقى ولا ينقضى ، غير مستحيل ، وبهذا التقدير يتقرر الإشكال ، ولا غرض فى أن يكون ذلك الباقى ، نفس آدمى ، أو جنى ، أو شيطان ، أو ملك ، أو ما شئت من الموجودات ، وهذا لازم على كل مذهب لهم ، إذ أثبتوا دورات لا نهاية لها .
The imagined things [or potentialities] also were some of them causes of others, so a person may suppose that in his imagination, but the discussion is about the existent in external reality, not in minds. Thus nothing remains except the souls of the dead. Some philosophers held that they were one eternal (azali) before attachment to bodies, and upon separation from bodies they unite, so there is no multiplicity in them, let alone being described as infinite. Others said: the soul is dependent on the temperament of the body, and the meaning of death is its non-existence, and it has no subsistence in its essence apart from the body. So then there is no existence for souls except in the case of the living, and the existing living are limited, and finitude is not negated from them, and the non-existent are not described at all, neither with existence of finitude nor with its non-existence except in imagination, if they suppose them existing. The answer: that this difficulty concerning souls we have raised against Ibn Sina and al-Farabi and the verifiers among them, since they ruled that the soul is a self-subsistent substance (jawhar qa'im bi-nafsihi), and that is the choice of the early Aristotle. And whoever deviates from this path, we say to him: Is it conceivable that something comes into existence and then remains (yabqa) or not? If they say: No, that is impossible. If they say: Yes, we say: Then if we suppose each day something coming into existence and remaining, inevitably there would have accumulated up to now existents without end. So even if the cycle is finite, the occurrence of an existent in it that remains and does not cease is not impossible. By this supposition the difficulty is established, and there is no purpose in that which remains being a human soul, or a jinn, or a devil, or an angel, or whatever you wish of existents. And this is necessary on every doctrine of theirs, since they affirm cycles without end.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers regarding the impossibility of infinite souls, and Ghazali's response. The objection is that if souls are many and persist after death, they would be infinite given the philosophers' belief in infinite cycles of world generation and corruption. Some philosophers avoid this by claiming souls were one eternal and unite after death, others by denying the soul's independent existence. Ghazali responds by noting that for those who affirm the soul as a self-subsistent substance, the objection is valid. For those who deny that, he poses a dilemma: either something can come into existence and persist, or not. If not, that is absurd. If yes, then under the assumption of infinite cycles, there would be an infinite number of persisting beings — a consequence the philosophers deny but cannot escape, as their own doctrine of infinite cycles entails it. Thus Ghazali turns their own premises against them.

Key Terms
نفس (soul)
جوهر قائم بنفسه (self-subsistent substance)
دورات لا نهاية لها (infinite cycles)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' objection is about the impossibility of infinite regress in general.
Correction: The objection is specifically about the number of souls given infinite cycles, not about infinite regress in causal chains.

Checkpoint
According to the first group of philosophers mentioned by Ghazali, what happens to souls after death?
A) They each remain separate and multiply infinitely.
B) They unite into one and lose multiplicity.
C) They are annihilated with the body.
D) They continue to exist in a finite number.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers proposed two ways to avoid infinite souls: either deny their multiplicity or deny their persistence after death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were forced to accept that your soul ceases to exist at death, how would the way you live today change? Would you still strive for the same goals? What does your answer reveal about your actual beliefs regarding the soul?
?
The philosophers argue that souls cannot be infinite. Yet you believe in an afterlife with countless souls. How does this passage challenge you to think more deeply about the nature of the soul and its reality? What assumptions do you hold that might be tested by this argument?
?
In this lesson, you read two philosophical positions that avoid an infinite number of souls. Which one seems more plausible to you? What does your preference reveal about your view of the soul's essence—is it a simple, eternal substance or dependent on the body? Are you willing to defend that view against the alternative?

Ghazali's Response: The Dilemma of Persisting Beings
Ghazali refutes the philosophers with a dilemma on persisting beings and infinite cycles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers by showing that their own belief in infinite cycles necessarily entails the existence of infinite persisting beings.

Core Idea
If something can persist, infinite cycles yield infinite beings, contradicting the philosophers.

Ghazali now directs his response to the 'verifiers' among the philosophers, like Ibn Sina and al-Farabi, who affirm the soul as a self-subsistent substance. For those who deny the soul's independence, Ghazali poses a powerful dilemma: either something can come into existence and persist, or it cannot. If it cannot, that's absurd. If it can, then under the philosophers' own assumption of infinite cycles, an infinite number of persisting beings would accumulate. This turns their own argument against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read Ghazali's response from 'والجواب' to the end. Notice he first addresses the verifiers' specific view, then gives a general argument.
Ghazali distinguishes between those who affirm the soul as a self-subsistent substance and others.
2
Understand the dilemma
Focus on the two options: either something can remain (yabqa) after coming into existence, or not. If not, that's impossible. If yes, then with infinite cycles infinite beings accumulate.
The key term is بقاء (remaining/persistence).
3
Apply the dilemma to infinite cycles
The philosophers believe in cycles without end. If each cycle produces something that remains, then the number of such things becomes infinite.
This directly contradicts their denial of infinite souls.
4
Note the generality of the argument
Ghazali points out that this applies to any being that persists—soul, angel, jinn, etc.—not just human souls.
This strengthens the refutation by making it independent of the specific nature of the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانت المقدرات أيضاً بعضها علل لبعض ، فالإنسان قد يفرض ذلك في وهمه ، وإنما الكلام في الموجود في الأعيان ، لا في الأذهان . فلا يبقى إلا نفوس الأموات وقد ذهب بعض الفلاسفة إلى أنها كانت واحدة أزلية قبل التعلق بالأبدان وعند مفارقة الأبدان تتحد ، فلا يكون فيها عدد ، فضلا أن توصف بأنها لا نهاية لها ، وقال آخرون : النفس تابعة لمزاج البدن ، وإنما معنى الموت عدمها ، ولا قوام لها بجوهرها دون الجسم ، فإذن لا وجود للنفوس ، إلا في حق الأحياء ، والأحياء الموجودون محصورون ، ولا تنتفى النهاية عنهم ، والمعدومون لا يوصفون أصلاً ، لا بوجود النهاية ولا بعدمها إلا في الوهم ، إذا فرضوا موجودين . والجواب : أن هذا الإشكال في النفوس ، أوردناه على ( ابن سينا ) و « الفارابي » والمحققين منهم ، إذ حكموا بأن النفس جوهر قائم بنفسه ، وهو اختيار أرسطاليس الأوائل ؛ ومن عدل عن المسلك فنقول له : هل يتصور أن يحدث شيء يبقى أم لا ؟ ! فإن قالوا : لا ، فهو محال ، وإن قالوا : نعم ، قلنا : فإذا قدرنا كل يوم حدوث شيء وبقاءه ، اجتمع إلى الآن لا محالة موجودات لا نهاية لها ، فالدورة وإن كانت منقضية ، فحصول موجود فيها يبقى ولا ينقضى ، غير مستحيل ، وبهذا التقدير يتقرر الإشكال ، ولا غرض فى أن يكون ذلك الباقى ، نفس آدمى ، أو جنى ، أو شيطان ، أو ملك ، أو ما شئت من الموجودات ، وهذا لازم على كل مذهب لهم ، إذ أثبتوا دورات لا نهاية لها .
The imagined things [or potentialities] also were some of them causes of others, so a person may suppose that in his imagination, but the discussion is about the existent in external reality, not in minds. Thus nothing remains except the souls of the dead. Some philosophers held that they were one eternal (azali) before attachment to bodies, and upon separation from bodies they unite, so there is no multiplicity in them, let alone being described as infinite. Others said: the soul is dependent on the temperament of the body, and the meaning of death is its non-existence, and it has no subsistence in its essence apart from the body. So then there is no existence for souls except in the case of the living, and the existing living are limited, and finitude is not negated from them, and the non-existent are not described at all, neither with existence of finitude nor with its non-existence except in imagination, if they suppose them existing. Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers regarding the impossibility of infinite souls, and Ghazali's response. The objection is that if souls are many and persist after death, they would be infinite given the philosophers' belief in infinite cycles of world generation and corruption. Some philosophers avoid this by claiming souls were one eternal and unite after death, others by denying the soul's independent existence. Ghazali responds by noting that for those who affirm the soul as a self-subsistent substance, the objection is valid. For those who deny that, he poses a dilemma: either something can come into existence and persist, or not. If not, that is absurd. If yes, then under the assumption of infinite cycles, there would be an infinite number of persisting beings — a consequence the philosophers deny but cannot escape, as their own doctrine of infinite cycles entails it. Thus Ghazali turns their own premises against them.

Key Terms
بقاء (remaining/persistence)
دورة (cycle)
جوهر قائم بنفسه (self-subsistent substance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is trying to prove that souls are actually infinite.
Correction: Ghazali is not asserting the existence of infinite souls; he is showing that the philosophers' premises (infinite cycles + possibility of persistence) lead to a contradiction with their denial.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's argument, what would happen if we suppose each day something comes into existence and remains, and there are infinite cycles?
A) There would be exactly as many things as there are days.
B) Things would disappear after one cycle.
C) There would accumulate existents without end (infinite).
D) There would be no change in the number of existents.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' own belief in infinite cycles forces them to accept infinite persisting beings, undermining their objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you hold onto a belief that logically leads to an absurd conclusion, are you willing to abandon that belief? What would it take for you to change your mind?
?
Ghazali's argument exposes a contradiction in the philosophers' system. Is there a contradiction in your own worldview that you have been avoiding? How will you confront it today?
?
You affirm that some things in your life persist (your identity, your faith, your relationships). What if that persistence, when extended across infinite time, leads to consequences you cannot accept? How does that make you reconsider what you truly believe about eternity?

The Philosophers' Doctrine of Celestial Souls and the Preserved Tablet
Ghazali presents the philosophers' claim about the Preserved Tablet and celestial souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that the souls of the heavens are the Preserved Tablet and how they analogize it to human memory and the Pen and Tablet.

Core Idea
Celestial souls are the Preserved Tablet, but not a physical tablet.

Ghazali begins by presenting the philosophers' view on how celestial souls know all particulars. They equate these souls with the Preserved Tablet, describing knowledge as an impression similar to memory in the brain, not as writing on a physical object. This sets the stage for their argument and Ghazali's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim
Read the philosophers' statement about the Preserved Tablet being the souls of the heavens. Notice how they describe the impression of particulars.
The philosophers insist it is not a physical tablet but an intellectual impression.
2
Understand the analogy
They compare it to the retentive power in the brain, rejecting a physical tablet. Why? Because infinite particulars would require an infinite body.
3
Identify the Pen and Tablet analogy
They distinguish between abstract intellects (the Pen) and heavenly souls (the Tablet), citing the Quran. Understand the hierarchy of benefactor and beneficiary.
The intellects are nobler as benefactors; souls are beneficiaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ ١٦ ] في إبطال قولهم إن نفوس السموات مطلعة على جميع الجزئيات الحادثة في هذا العالم، وإن المراد باللوح المحفوظ ، نفوس السموات، وإن انتقاش جزئيات العالم فيها يضاهي انتقاش المحفوظات ، في القوة الحافظة ، المودعة في دماغ الإنسان ، لا أنه جسم صلب عريض ، مكتوبة عليه الأشياء ، كما يكتب الصبيان على اللوح ، لأن تلك الكتابة ، تستدعى كثرتها ، اتساع المكتوب عليه ، وإذا لم يكن للمكتوب نهاية ، لم يكن للمكتوب عليه نهاية ، ولا يتصور جسم لا نهاية له ، ولا تمكن خطوط لا نهاية لها ، على جسم، ولا يمكن تعريف أشياء لا نهاية لها على جسم بخطوط معدودة ، وقد زعموا : أن الملائكة السماوية ، هى نفوس السموات ، وأن الملائكة الكروبين المقربين ، هي العقول المجردة ، التي هي جواهر قائمة بأنفسها لا تتحيز ، ولا تتصرف في الأجسام ، وأن هذه الصور الجزئية ، تفيض على النفوس السماوية منها ، وهى (١) أشرف من الملائكة السماوية ، لأنها مفيدة ، وهذه مستفيدة ، والمفيد أشرف من المستفيد ، ولذلك عبر عن الأشرف بـ « القلم ، فقال تعالى : « علم بالقلم » لأنه كالنقاش المفيد ، مثل المعلم ، وشبه المستفيد باللوح ، هذا مذهبهم . والنزاع في هذه المسألة ، يخالف النزاع فيما قبلها ، فإن ما ذكروه من قبل ، ليس محالا ، إذ منهاه كون السماء حيواناً متحركاً لغرض ، وهو ممكن ، أما هذه فترجع إلى إثبات علم المخلوق ، بالجزئيات التى لا نهاية لها ، وهذا ربما تعتقد استحالته ، فنطالبهم بالدليل عليه ، فإنه تحكم في نفسه استدلوا فيه ، بأن قالوا : ثبت أن الحركة الدورية إرادية ، والإرادة تتبع المراد ، والمراد الكلى ، لا تتوجه إليه إلا إرادة كلية ، والإرادة الكلية ، لا يصدر منها شيء ، فإن كل موجود بالفعل ، معين جزئى ، والإرادة الكلية ، نسبتها إلى آحاد الجزئيات ، على وتيرة واحدة ، فلا يصدر عنها شيء جزئى ، بل لابد من إرادة جزئية للحركة المعينة فللفلك فى كل حركة جزئية معينة ، من نقطة إلى نقطة معينة ، إرادة جزئية لتلك الحركة ، فله لا محالة تصور لتلك الحركات الجزئية ، بقوة جسمانية ، إذ الجزئيات لا تدرك إلا بالقوى الجسمانية ، فإن كل إرادة ، فمن ضرورتها تصور لذلك المراد ، أي علم به ، سواء كان جزئيا أو كليا . ومهما كان للفلك ، تصور الجزئيات الحركات وإحاطة بها ، أحاط لا محالة بما يلزم منها ، من اختلاف النسب مع الأرض ، من كون بعض أجزائه طالعة ، وبعضها غاربة ، وبعضها في وسط السماء ، فوق قوم ، وتحت قدم قوم ، وكذلك يعلم ما يلزم من اختلاف النسب ، التي تتجدد بالحركة ، من التثليث والتسديس ، والمقابلة والمقارنة ، إلى غير ذلك من الحوادث السماوية . وسائر الحوادث الأرضية ، تستند إلى الحوادث السماوية ، إما بغير واسطة ، وإما بواسطة واحدة ، وإما بوسائط كثيرة . وعلى الجملة ، فكل حادث ، فله سبب حادث ، إلى أن ينقطع التسلسل ، بالارتقاء إلى الحركة السماوية الأبدية ، التي بعضها سبب للبعض فإذن الأسباب والمسببات ، في سلسلتها ، تنتهى إلى الحركات الجزئية الدورية السماوية ، فالمتصور للحركات ، متصور للوازمها متصور للوازمها ، ولوازم لوازمها إلى آخر السلسلة . فبهذا يطلع على ما يحدث ، فإن كل ما يحدث ، فحدوثه واجب عن علته ، مهما تحققت العلة
Issue [16]: On refuting their statement that the souls of the heavens are informed of all particular events occurring in this world, and that what is meant by the Preserved Tablet is the souls of the heavens, and that the impression of the particulars of the world in them resembles the impression of preserved things in the retentive power deposited in the human brain, not that it is a solid wide body upon which things are written, as children write on a tablet, because that writing, if it becomes abundant, requires expansion of the written-upon, and if what is written has no end, what is written upon has no end, and a body without end is inconceivable, and lines without end cannot be on a body, and it is impossible to define infinite things on a body by limited lines. And they claimed that the heavenly angels are the souls of the heavens, and that the cherubim, the proximate, are the abstract intellects, which are self-subsisting substances without location, and do not act in bodies, and that these particular forms emanate upon the heavenly souls from them, and they (the intellects) are nobler than the heavenly angels, because they are beneficial, and these are beneficiaries, and the benefactor is nobler than the beneficiary. Therefore the nobler was expressed by 'the Pen', and He said exalted: 'He taught by the pen' because it is like a beneficial engraver, like the teacher, and He compared the beneficiary to the Tablet. This is their doctrine. The dispute in this issue differs from the dispute in what precedes it, for what they mentioned before is not impossible, since its conclusion is that the heaven is an animal moving for a purpose, which is possible. As for this, it reduces to establishing the knowledge of the created being of particulars that have no end, and this perhaps one believes impossible. So we demand from them the proof for it, for it is an arbitrary judgment in itself. They argued for it by saying: It is established that circular motion is voluntary, and will follows the intended, and the universal intended is not directed to except by a universal will, and the universal will does not produce anything, for every existent in act is a particular individual, and the universal will's relation to individual particulars is uniform, so it does not produce any particular thing. Rather, there must be a particular will for the specific motion. So for the sphere in each specific particular motion, from a specific point to a specific point, there is a particular will for that motion. Therefore it necessarily has a conception of those particular motions by a bodily power, since particulars are not perceived except by bodily powers. For every will, it is necessary that there be a conception of that intended, i.e., knowledge of it, whether particular or universal. And whenever the sphere has conception of the particular motions and encompasses them, it necessarily encompasses what follows from them, of the varying relations with the earth, such as some of its parts being rising, some setting, some in the midst of the sky above some people, and under the feet of some people. Likewise it knows what follows from the varying relations that are renewed by motion, such as trine, sextile, opposition, conjunction, and other celestial events. And the rest of terrestrial events are dependent on celestial events, either without intermediary, or with one intermediary, or with many intermediaries. In sum, every event has an event-cause, until the chain ends by ascending to the eternal celestial motion, some of which are causes for others. Thus causes and effects in their chain terminate to the particular circular celestial motions. So the one who conceives the motions conceives their concomitants, and the concomitants of their concomitants, to the end of the chain. By this he is informed of what occurs, for everything that occurs, its occurrence is necessary from its cause when the cause is realized.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's presentation and initial refutation of the philosophers' claim regarding the knowledge of celestial souls. He begins by stating the issue: refuting their statement that the souls of the heavens know all particular events, equating the Preserved Tablet to these souls, and describing how particulars are impressed in them like memories in human brains, not as a physical tablet. He then explains their view that heavenly angels are souls of the heavens, while the cherubim (closer angels) are abstract intellects from which particular forms emanate to the souls. The intellects are nobler as benefactors. They analogize the intellect to the Pen and the soul to the Tablet, citing Quran 'taught by the pen'. Ghazali notes this dispute differs from previous ones because here they claim knowledge of infinite particulars, which may be impossible, so he demands proof. Then he presents their proof: circular motion is voluntary; will follows the intended; a universal will cannot produce a particular act; hence the sphere must have particular wills for each specific motion, requiring particular conceptions (knowledge) of those motions via a bodily power. Since the sphere conceives all its particular motions, it necessarily knows all their consequences: celestial relations (rising, setting, aspects) and all terrestrial events dependent on celestial motions through chains of causes. Thus, knowing the motions means knowing all events, as every event is necessary given its cause.

Key Terms
نفوس السموات
اللوح المحفوظ
القوة الحافظة
القلم
اللوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Preserved Tablet is a physical tablet on which infinite lines are written.
Correction: The philosophers argue it is the souls of the heavens, where particulars are impressed like memories in the brain, not as infinite writing on a physical tablet.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers as presented, what does 'the Pen' refer to?
A) The souls of the heavens
B) The abstract intellects
C) The Preserved Tablet
D) The human brain

Key Takeaway
The philosophers view celestial souls as the Preserved Tablet, which receives impressions of particulars not through physical writing but through an intellectual impression akin to memory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah knows every detail of your life, even the smallest thought or action? How does that belief affect the way you live today?
?
The philosophers argued that a physical tablet cannot contain infinite knowledge. Where do you look for security: in physical objects or in your trust in Allah's infinite knowledge?
?
You have just read that the Pen (intellects) is considered nobler than the Tablet (souls) because it gives. In your own life, are you more focused on giving or receiving? What would change if you prioritized being a giver like the Pen?

The Philosophers' Argument for Celestial Souls' Knowledge of All Particulars
Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument from voluntary motion to establish knowledge of all particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' logical argument that celestial souls must know all particular events because their voluntary motion requires particular wills and conceptions, which entail knowledge of all consequences.

Core Idea
The sphere's voluntary motion forces it to have particular will and knowledge of all events.

Having described the celestial souls as the Preserved Tablet, Ghazali now presents the philosophers' proof that they know all particulars. The argument hinges on the nature of the sphere's motion as voluntary and the need for particular wills, which in turn require particular knowledge. This knowledge extends to all events through causal chains.

Guided Path
1
Start from established premise
The philosophers assert that circular motion is voluntary. Why must it be voluntary? Because it is for a purpose.
This premise is taken as established from earlier discussions.
2
Universal vs. particular will
Understand why a universal will cannot produce a particular act; only a particular will can initiate a specific motion.
A universal will relates uniformly to all particulars, so it cannot select one.
3
Particular will implies particular conception
Every will requires knowledge of the intended. So the sphere has conception of each particular motion.
This conception is by a bodily power, as particulars are perceived by bodily powers.
4
Extend knowledge to all events
Knowing all motions leads to knowing all consequences, including terrestrial events, through causal connections.
Every event has a cause leading back to celestial motions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
حركة دورية
إرادة كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A universal will can directly produce a particular act.
Correction: The philosophers argue that a universal will's relation to all particulars is uniform, so it cannot pick out one; a particular will is needed.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why does the sphere need a particular will for each motion?
A) Because it has many motions
B) Because universal will cannot produce a specific particular act
C) Because the sphere is not conscious
D) Because motion is involuntary

Key Takeaway
The philosophers conclude that celestial souls know all particular events because their knowledge of the sphere's motions, which are voluntary and require particular wills, extends through causal chains to every event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the philosophers believed celestial souls know every detail of your life through causal chains, how does that make you feel about your private sins—are they ever truly hidden? And now that you know this is a philosophical argument, not revelation, what moves you to seek forgiveness from Allah who alone knows the unseen?
?
The philosophers argued that knowledge of a cause gives knowledge of its effect. Consider a cause in your life—a habit, a relationship, a decision. What does its existence reveal about you that you might be ignoring? Will you confront that today?
?
This passage forces the sphere to have a particular will for each motion. Reflect on your own 'motions'—your daily actions. Are they driven by a particular will for God's pleasure, or are they drifting? What one action will you intentionally will for Allah tomorrow, starting now?

Ghazali on the Impossibility of Infinite Knowledge in a Created Soul
Ghazali argues that the celestial soul cannot know infinite future particulars in one moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that a created soul cannot simultaneously know an infinite number of future particulars, and how he distinguishes this from God's knowledge.

Core Idea
A created soul cannot contain infinite detailed knowledge at once.

In this passage from Tahafut al-Falasifa, Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim that the celestial soul knows all future particulars. He presents a dilemma: either it knows only the present, which negates knowledge of the unseen, or it knows the infinite future, which is impossible for a finite soul. Ghazali then distinguishes divine knowledge from creaturely knowledge to preempt an objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Notice that Ghazali poses a choice: are the known particulars only present or also future? This sets up the argument.
The dilemma forces a contradiction.
2
Analyze the impossibility of infinite future knowledge
Ghazali argues that if the soul knows future particulars, they are infinite, and a created soul cannot have infinite distinct sciences at once without succession.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Distinguish from God's knowledge
Ghazali preempts the objection that God knows all particulars by clarifying that divine knowledge is different in kind from creaturely knowledge.
This distinction saves the argument from an obvious counter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا نقول : هذه الجزئيات المفصلة ، المعلومة لنفس الفلك ، هي موجودة في الحال ؟ ! أو ينضاف إليها ما يتوقع كونه في الاستقبال ؟ ! فإن قصرتموه على الموجود في الحال ، بطل اطلاعه على الغيب ، واطلاع الأنبياء - صلوات الله عليهم - فى اليقظة ، وسائر الخلق في النوم ، على ما سيكون في الاستقبال ، بواسطته ، ثم بطل مقتضى الدليل ، فإنه تحكم بأن من عرف الشيء عرف لوازمه وتوابعه ، حتى لو عرفنا جميع أسباب الأشياء ، لعرفنا جميع الحوادث المستقبلة ؛ وأسباب جميع الحوادث ، حاضرة في الحال ، فإنها هي الحركة السماوية ، ولكن تقتضى المسبب ، إما بواسطة أو بوسائط كثيرة . وإذا تعدى إلى المستقبل ، لم يكن له آخر ، فكيف يعرف ، تفصيل جميع الجزئيات ، فى الاستقبال ، إلى غير نهاية ؟ ! وكيف يجتمع نفس مخلوق ، في حال واحدة ، من غير تعاقب ، علوم جزئية مفصلة ، لا نهاية لأعدادها ، ولا غاية لآحادها ومن لم يشهد له عقله ، باستحالة ذلك ، فلييأس عقله من فإن قلبوا علينا هذا ، في علم الله تعالى ، فليس تعلق علم الله تعالى بمعلوماته - بالاتفاق - على نحو تعلق العلوم التي هي للمخلوقات . بل مهما دار نفس الفلك ، دورة نفس الإنسان ، كان من قبيل نفس الإنسان؛ فإنه شاركه ، فى كونه مدركاً للجزئيات بواسطة ، فإن لم يلتحق به قطعاً، كان الغالب على الظن أنه من قبيله ، فإن لم يكن غالباً على الظن ، فهو ممكن ، والإمكان يبطل دعواهم القطع بما قطعوا به
But we say: These detailed particulars known to the soul of the sphere—are they present now? Or are added to them those expected to be in the future? For if you restrict it to what is present now, its knowledge of the unseen becomes invalid, and the knowledge of the prophets—peace be upon them—in wakefulness and the rest of creation in sleep of what will be in the future through it becomes invalid; further, the implication of the proof becomes invalid, for it arbitrarily claims that whoever knows a thing knows its necessary concomitants and consequences, such that if we knew all the causes of things we would know all future events. But the causes of all events are present now, for they are the celestial motion, which necessitates the effect either directly or through many intermediaries. And if it extends to the future, it has no end. So how can it know the detail of all particulars in the future to infinity? And how can a created soul gather in one state, without succession, detailed particular sciences whose numbers have no end and whose individuals have no limit? And whoever's intellect does not testify to the impossibility of that, let his intellect despair of [understanding]. For if they turn this against us concerning the knowledge of God Most High, then the relation of God's knowledge to its objects—by agreement—is not like the relation of the sciences that belong to creatures. Rather, whenever the soul of the sphere circulates, it is of the kind of the human soul; for it shares with it in being a knower of particulars through mediation. If it is not definitely attached to it, the predominant presumption is that it is of its kind; and if it is not the predominant presumption, then it is possible, and possibility invalidates their claim of certainty with which they were certain.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's further argument against the philosophers' claim that the celestial soul knows all particulars. Ghazali poses a dilemma: are the known particulars only those present now, or do they include future ones? If restricted to the present, then the soul has no knowledge of the unseen, which undermines the basis for prophetic and dream knowledge of future events. Moreover, the philosophers' own proof—that knowing a thing entails knowing its consequences—would also collapse, since if we knew all present causes (celestial motion), we would supposedly know all future effects, but that is impossible because the chain of intermediaries extends infinitely into the future. Ghazali then asks: how can a single created soul contain an infinite number of detailed knowledges in one moment without succession? He asserts that anyone whose reason does not see the impossibility of this should despair of reason. Then he anticipates an objection: if they compare this to God's knowledge, he says divine knowledge is entirely different, by agreement. The celestial soul, he argues, is analogous to the human soul—it perceives particulars through mediation. If it is not identical to the human soul, at best it is probably similar, and at least possibly so. Mere possibility destroys their claim to certainty.

Key Terms
الجزئيات المفصلة
نفس الفلك
الغيب
علوم جزئية
تعاقب
علم الله تعالى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An immaterial soul can know infinite details at once because it is not limited by matter.
Correction: Ghazali argues that even an immaterial created soul, like a human soul, cannot simultaneously encompass an infinite number of distinct cognitions. Only God's knowledge is infinite and atemporal.

Checkpoint
What reason does Ghazali give for rejecting the idea that the celestial soul knows all future particulars?
A) The causes of future events are not yet known.
B) The future is infinite and cannot be encompassed by a created soul in one moment.
C) The celestial soul is too busy worshipping the First.
D) Prophets have already revealed all future events.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that a created soul's knowledge is finite and temporal, thus it cannot know an infinite future; only God's knowledge transcends these limits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your own intellect or intuition can give you certain knowledge of future events? In what areas of your life do you rely on your own predictions rather than turning to the All-Knowing? Will you consciously admit your limitation and seek guidance from the One who knows the unseen?
?
When you plan your life months or years ahead, do you implicitly believe you know what will happen? How does Ghazali's argument expose the arrogance of thinking we can master the future? Can you repent of that presumption and live with humble reliance on God's knowledge?
?
God's knowledge is infinite and beyond our comprehension, yet we often treat His knowledge as if it were like our own, only bigger. In what ways have you reduced God to your own mental categories? Will you commit to studying His attributes as taught by revelation, acknowledging that He is utterly unlike His creation?

Ghazali Refutes the Claim of Celestial Knowledge Without Distraction
Ghazali refutes the claim that celestial souls know all because they are free from bodily distractions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' argument that celestial souls know all because they lack bodily distractions.

Core Idea
Absence of bodily distraction does not guarantee unrestricted knowledge.

The philosophers argue that celestial souls, free from human bodily distractions, have perfect knowledge of all things. Ghazali responds by questioning the assumption that no other forms of distraction exist. In this passage, he presents a series of rebuttals that challenge the philosophers' certainty and open the door to alternative possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the philosophers' claim carefully. Notice they assume that bodily distractions are the only obstacles to knowledge.
2
Identify Ghazali's first rebuttal
He asks: How do you know there is no other distractor? He proposes that worship and longing for the First could be a distractor.
3
Identify Ghazali's second rebuttal
He asks: What prevents supposing an obstacle other than bodily appetites? This challenges the philosophers' limited view.
4
Consider Ghazali's third point
Distractions are not confined to what we observe in ourselves. Rational people have high ambitions children can't imagine.
This shows the limitation of human experience.
5
Reflect on the method
Ghazali's refutation works by questioning the philosophers' assumption that they can know what is and isn't a distractor for celestial souls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : حق النفس الإنسانية ، في جوهرها ، أن تدرك أيضاً الأشياء ولكن اشتغالها ، بنتائج الشهوة ، والغضب ، والحرص ، والحقد ، والحسد ، والجوع ، والألم ، وبالجملة عوارض البدن ، وما تورده الحواس عليه ، حتى إذا أقبلت النفس الإنسانية على شيء واحد ، شغلها عن غيره . وأما النفوس الفلكية ، فبريئة عن هذه الصفات ، لا يعتريها شاغل ، ولا يستغرقها هم ، وألم ، وإحساس ، فعرفت جميع الأشياء . قلنا : وبم عرفتم ، أنه لا شاغل لها ، وهلا كانت عبادتها ، واشتياقها إلى الأول ، مستغرقاً لها ، وشاغلا لها ، عن تصور الجزئيات المفصلة ! من أو ما الذي يحيل ، تقدير مانع آخر ، سوى الغضب والشهوة وهذه الموانع المحسوسة ؟! ومن أين عرفتم انحصار المانع ، في القدر الذي شاهدناه أنفسنا ؟ ! وفى العقلاء شواغل من علو الهمة ، وطلب الرئاسة ، ما يستحيل تصوره عند الأطفال ، ولا يعتقدونها شاغلا ومانعاً ، فمن أين يعرف استحالة
For if it is said: The right of the human soul, in its essence, is to also perceive things, but its preoccupation with the results of appetite, anger, greed, spite, envy, hunger, pain—in short, the accidents of the body, and what the senses bring to it—so that when the human soul turns to one thing, it distracts it from others. As for the celestial souls, they are free from these attributes; no distractor afflicts them, no worry, pain, or sensation overwhelms them, so they know all things. We say: How do you know that there is no distractor for them? Why is it not the case that their worship and longing for the First are overwhelming for them and distract them from conceiving of detailed particulars? Or what prevents the supposition of another obstacle besides anger, appetite, and these sensible obstacles? And how do you know that the obstacle is confined to what we ourselves have observed? For among rational people there are distractions from high aspiration and seeking leadership—things whose conception is impossible for children, and they do not consider them a distractor or obstacle. So how can one know the impossibility [of such obstacles in celestial souls]?
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. The philosophers object that the human soul in its essence can perceive all things, but it is distracted by bodily appetites, emotions, and sensations; whereas celestial souls are free from these distractions and thus know all things. Ghazali responds with a series of rebuttals. First, he questions how they know celestial souls have no distractor at all—perhaps their worship and longing for the First (the First Principle) could itself be an absorbing occupation that prevents them from attending to detailed particulars. Second, he asks what prevents positing some other obstacle beyond those experienced by humans (like anger and appetite). Third, he argues that the range of possible distractions is not limited to what humans commonly observe; for example, among rational people there are distractions like high ambition and desire for leadership that children cannot even conceive of. Therefore, the philosophers cannot conclusively claim to know that celestial souls have no such obstacles, and thus their absolute certainty is unjustified.

النفوس الفلكية
الشهوة
الغضب
عوارض البدن
عبادتها واشتياقها إلى الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absence of bodily distractions guarantees perfect knowledge for celestial souls.
Correction: Ghazali shows that other forms of preoccupation, like intense devotion, can also limit attention.

Checkpoint
What alternative distraction does Ghazali suggest for celestial souls besides bodily appetites?
A) Fear of punishment.
B) Their worship and longing for the First.
C) Jealousy among themselves.
D) Boredom from monotony.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim that celestial souls know all because they lack bodily distractions is unsustainable since they cannot rule out other possible obstacles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'lofty' pursuits in your life—like seeking knowledge, status, or even worship—might actually be distracting you from a deeper, more comprehensive understanding? How would you know if they are? What would you change today to remove such distractions?
?
Ghazali challenges the assumption that absence of one type of distraction (bodily) guarantees perfection. What assumptions do you hold about your own ability to know truth that might be similarly unfounded? Where do you need to humble yourself before God?
?
Consider your own spiritual life: Could your intense devotion to certain practices or goals be blinding you to other truths you need to see? Ask God to reveal to you any hidden distractions today, and commit to removing them, no matter how spiritual they seem.

The Soul's Perpetual Awareness of Its Body
Ghazali refutes the claim that the soul sometimes forgets its body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Ghazali, the soul is never heedless of the body, and how he uses the principle of inherence and analogies to refute the philosophers' claim.

Core Idea
If the soul inheres in the body, its awareness of the body must be constant.

Ghazali addresses an objection: do we sometimes forget our own body? He argues from the nature of perception and inherence that the soul must always be aware of the body, though not necessarily its detailed shape. This passage uses an analogy from the sense of smell to clarify how we can be generally aware without precise localization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then the translation. Let the argument's flow come through before interpreting.
Notice how Ghazali moves from a general principle to specific examples.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the statement about perception and inherence. Ghazali says that if a perception inheres in a locus, it must always perceive that locus because the only possible relation is inherence.
This is the logical foundation of the entire argument.
3
Apply the principle to the soul
See how Ghazali applies this to the soul's self-awareness: the soul always intellects itself. Then extend to body awareness: as long as man is conscious of himself, he is conscious of his body.
Ghazali distinguishes between general awareness of the body and detailed knowledge of its parts.
4
Analyze the analogy with smell
Consider the analogy of the sense of smell. Everyone knows they smell with their body, but cannot pinpoint the exact seat. Similarly, man feels his identity is centered in the chest/heart area, even without knowing its shape.
The analogy reinforces that detailed knowledge is not required for constant awareness.
5
Evaluate the conclusion
Read the final sentence: the philosophers' claim that we are sometimes heedless of the body is false. Reflect on how Ghazali has refuted it.
The conclusion directly opposes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا تحقيق، وهو أن الإدراك الحال فى محل، إنما يدرك المحل لنسبة له إلى المحل، ولا يتصور أن تكون له نسبة إليه، سوى الحلول فيه، فليدركه أبداً وإن كانت هذه النسبة لا تكفى، فينبغى ألا يدرك أبداً، إذ لا يمكن أن تكون له نسبة أخرى إليه، كما أنه لما كان يعقل نفسه، عقل نفسه أبداً ولم يغفل عنها بحال. فالإنسان ما دام يشعر بنفسه، ولا يغفل عنها، فإنه يشعر بجسده وجسمه، نعم لا يتعين له اسم القلب وصورته وشكله، ولكنه يثبت نفسه جسما، حتى يثبت نفسه فى ثيابه، وفى بيته، والنفس التى ذكروها، لا تناسب البيت والثوب. فإثباته لأصل الجسم ملازم له، وغفلته عن شكله واسمه، محل الشم وأنهما زائدتان فى مقدم الدماغ، شبيهتان بحلمتي الثدى، فإن كل إنسان يعلم أنه يدرك الرائحة بجسمه، ولكن محل الإدراك لا يتشكل له ولا يتعين، وإن كان يدرك أنه إلى الرأس، أقرب منه إلى العقب، ومن جملة الرأس إلى داخل الأنف، أقرب منه إلى داخل الأذن، فكذلك يشعر الإنسان بنفسه، ويعلم أن هويته التى بها قوامه، إلى قلبه وصدره أقرب منها، إلى رجله، فإنه يقدر نفسه باقياً مع عدم الرجل، ولا يقدر على تقدير نفسه، باقياً مع عدم القلب فما ذكروه من أنه يغفل عن الجسم تارة، وتارة لا يغفل عنه، فليس كذلك.
And this is a verification: the perception that inheres in a locus perceives the locus only because of a relation to it, and it is inconceivable that it have any relation to it other than inherence. So it would perceive it always, even if this relation is insufficient; then it should never perceive it, since it cannot have another relation to it, just as when it intellects itself, it intellects itself always and is not heedless of it at all. Man, as long as he is conscious of himself and not heedless of it, is conscious of his body and his frame. Indeed, the name, image, and shape of the heart are not determined for him, but he affirms himself as a body, so that he affirms himself in his clothes and in his house. And the soul that they mention does not suit the house and the clothes. So his affirmation of the basis of the body is inseparable from him, and his heedlessness of its shape and name is like [the case of] the sense of smell—that they are two appendages in the front of the brain, similar to two nipples. For every man knows that he perceives smell with his body, but the seat of perception is not shaped or determined for him, though he perceives that it is closer to the head than to the heel, and from the head to the inside of the nose is closer than to the inside of the ear. Similarly, man is conscious of himself and knows that his identity by which he subsists is closer to his heart and chest than to his leg. For he can conceive himself remaining without the leg, but he cannot conceive himself remaining without the heart. What they mentioned—that he is sometimes heedless of the body and sometimes not—is not so.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that the soul sometimes forgets the body. Ghazali begins by establishing a principle: if a perception inheres in a locus, it perceives that locus by virtue of a relation of inherence. Since perception can only relate to its locus through inherence, if it perceives at all, it must perceive that locus always. He then applies this to the soul's self-awareness: the soul always intellects itself and is never heedless of itself. Likewise, as long as man is conscious of himself, he is conscious of his body. He may not know the exact shape or name of his heart, but he affirms himself as a body, even to the point of locating himself in clothes and a house. Ghazali then uses an analogy with the sense of smell: everyone knows they smell with their body, but they cannot pinpoint the exact seat of smell—only that it is in the head, near the nose. Similarly, man feels that his identity is centered in his chest/heart area, and he can imagine himself without a leg but not without a heart. Thus, the claim that the soul alternates between being heedful and heedless of the body is false; it is always aware of the body as a whole.

Key Terms

حلول
نسبة
قلب
هوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We sometimes forget our body because we do not constantly think of its shape or organs.
Correction: Ghazali argues that general awareness of the body as a whole is constant even if detailed knowledge is absent, just as we know we smell without pinpointing the organ.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali in this passage, why does the soul always perceive the body?
A) Because the soul is itself a body.
B) Because the soul inheres in the body and must always perceive its locus.
C) Because the soul is identical with the body.
D) Because the soul remembers the body from past experiences.

Key Takeaway
The soul's inherence in the body entails perpetual, though not necessarily detailed, awareness of the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is always aware of your body, even when you are not thinking about it, what does that say about the moments you feel distant from yourself? Is that feeling a lie your ego tells you? How will you remind yourself of this constant presence in your daily life?
?
You can imagine yourself without a leg but not without a heart. What does this reveal about what is essential to your existence? Are you living as if your heart is the center of your being, or have you let other things define your identity?
?
Ghazali argues that your soul never forgets your body. If that is true, then how can you claim to forget Allah when your soul is always engaged with something physical? What is the root of your heedlessness, and what concrete step will you take today to turn back to the One who is even closer than your own jugular vein?

The Ninth Proof: Body's Constant Change and Unity of the Self
Ghazali presents the ninth proof that man is not merely the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth proof of Ghazali that the body's constant material change implies that the self is not identical to the body.

Core Idea
The body is completely replaced over time, so the self must be something more.

Ghazali presents a simple yet powerful observation: our bodies are not static but constantly dissolve and rebuild. If we were merely our bodies, we would be different persons at different ages. This proof sets the stage for affirming an enduring self beyond physical matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Ghazali begins with a rhetorical question and then develops the argument through a concrete example.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument continues in the next lesson.
2
Follow the example of the child
Ghazali traces the changes from birth (semen) to age forty. Identify all stages: separation, illness, withering, fattening, growth. Recognize that after forty years none of the original parts remain.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the body at age forty is materially different from the body at birth, if man were identical to his body, he would be a different person. The proof shows that personal identity cannot depend on the physical body alone.
This leads to the conclusion that there is an unchanging self (the soul).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل تاسع : قالوا : كيف يكون الإنسان عبارة عن الجسم مع عوارضه؟! وهذه الأجسام لا تزال تنحل، والغذاء يسد مسد ما ينحل، حتى إذا رأينا صبيا انفصل من أمه، يمرض مرارا ثم يذبل، ثم يسمن وينمو، فيمكننا أن نقول : لم يبق فيه بعد الأربعين شيء من الأجزاء التى كانت موجودة عند الانفصال، بل كان أول وجوده من أجزاء المنى فقط، ولم يبق فيه شيء من أجزاء المنى بل انحل كل ذلك وتبدل بغيره، فيكون هذا الجسم غير ذلك الجسم، ونقول :
Ninth Proof: They say: How can man be expression of the body with its accidents? These bodies are constantly dissolving, and food replaces what dissolves, so that when we see a child separated from his mother, he becomes ill repeatedly, then withers, then becomes fat and grows. So we can say: There remains in him after forty years nothing of the parts that existed at separation. Rather, his first existence was only from the parts of the semen, and nothing of the parts of the semen remains; rather, all of that dissolved and was replaced by something else, so this body is not that body. And we say:
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is the ninth proof against the view that man is merely the body and its accidents. The author argues that the body constantly undergoes dissolution and replacement through nourishment. Using the example of a child from birth to adulthood, he notes that after forty years, none of the original parts from birth remain; even the initial parts from semen are completely replaced. Therefore, the body he has at age forty is materially different from his body at birth. If man were identical to his body, he would be a different person at different times. The proof concludes that there must be an underlying, unchanging principle (the soul) that accounts for personal identity over time. The proof is presented as a reductio ad absurdum of the materialist view.

Key Terms
دلیل تاسع
الجسم
عوارض
انحلال
تبدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that gradual change does not affect identity, so the self can still be the body.
Correction: Ghazali's proof shows that over time the body undergoes complete material replacement, so if the self were the body, personal identity would be lost.

Checkpoint
What does the ninth proof use to show that man cannot be merely the body?
A) The soul's two actions.
B) The recovery of knowledge after illness.
C) The complete replacement of the body's parts over time.
D) The weakness of the senses.

Key Takeaway
The ninth proof demonstrates that the constant material replacement of the body undermines any identification of the self with the physical body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body today is materially different from the one you had as a child. Yet you still feel like the same 'you'. What does that tell you about the nature of your soul? Can you identify one area of your life where you act as if your body is all that matters, and this lesson calls you to shift that focus?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment with a body that has been completely replaced many times over. The part of you that will be held accountable is not this physical shell. In light of this proof, how seriously are you attending to the state of your soul? What is one habit you can change starting today to nourish your soul rather than just your body?
?
Ghazali challenges the idea that you are your body. If your identity does not depend on your physical appearance or age, why do you still invest so much in preserving your body while neglecting the eternal aspect of yourself? Confront that imbalance: what is one concrete step you will take this week to prioritize your soul's well-being over your body's comfort?
