The Cure: Weakening Attachments through Knowledge
Ghazali explains the remedy for soul's attachments and the possibility of weakening them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's teaching that while complete detachment from worldly attachments is impossible, they can be weakened through knowledge and piety, leading to a peaceful death.

Core Idea
Perfect detachment is impossible, but weakening attachments is the key to salvation.

Ghazali now turns from critique to remedy. He explains that while complete escape from worldly attachments is not possible in this life, the soul can weaken them through knowledge and piety. This passage offers both hope and realism, grounding his argument in Quranic verse and a vivid analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: first the remedy, then the limitation, then the Quranic evidence, and finally the analogy.
Pay attention to the word 'تضعيف' – weakening, which is the key concept.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two clear parts: the remedy (restraining desire, turning to knowledge and piety) and the realistic limitation (complete removal is impossible, but weakening is possible). Find where each part begins.
The limitation is introduced by 'ولا يمكن سلب'.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy of a man summoned to a high position: he feels pain leaving his family, but joy overshadows it. How does this illustrate the state of the dying believer?
This analogy shows that minor pain is overcome by greater pleasure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينجى عن التضمخ بهذا الهيئات، إلا كف النفس عن الهوى، والإعراض عن الدنيا، والإقبال بكنه الجد، على العلم والتقوى، تنقطع علائقها، عن الأمور الدنيوية، وهى في الدنيا وتستحكم علاقتها مع الأمور الأخروية، فإذا مات كان كالمتخلص من سجن، والواصل إلى جميع مطالبه، وهو جنة . ولا يمكن سلب جميع هذه الصفات عن النفس، ومحوها بالكلية، فإن الضرورات البدنية، جاذبة إليها، إلا أنه يمكن تضعيف تلك العلاقة، ولذلك قال الله تعالى : [ وإن منكم إلا واردها ، كان على ربك حتما مقضيا ] ، إلا أنه إذا ضعفت العلاقة، لم تشتد نكاية فراقها، وعظم الالتذاذ بما اطلع عليه، عند الموت من الأمور الإلهية، فأماط أثر مفارقة الدنيا، والنزوع إليها، على قرب، كمن يستنهض من وطنه، إلى منصب عظيم، وملك رفيع ترق نفسه حالة الفراق، على أهله ووطنه، فيتأذى أذى ما، ولكن ينمحى بما، من لذة الابتهاج بالملك والرئاسة .
And nothing saves from being sullied by these states except restraining the soul from desire, turning away from the world, and turning with utmost seriousness to knowledge and piety; its attachments to worldly matters are cut off while it is in the world, and its relationship with otherworldly matters becomes strong. So when it dies, it is like one who has escaped from prison and attained all his desires, which is his paradise. And it is not possible to strip the soul of all these attributes entirely nor obliterate them completely, for bodily necessities attract them. But it is possible to weaken that relationship. Therefore God Most High said: 'And there is none of you but will come to it (Hell); this is with your Lord a decree determined' (Quran 19:71). However, when the relationship is weakened, the injury of separation does not become severe, and the pleasure of what is witnessed at death from divine matters becomes great, so it removes the trace of separation from the world and longing for it, though near, like one who is summoned from his homeland to a great position and an exalted kingdom; his soul is pained by separation from his family and homeland, and he suffers some pain, but it is effaced by the pleasure of rejoicing in the kingdom and leadership he is about to assume.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage explains the remedy for the soul's harmful attachments and the realistic possibility of their removal. The author first states that the only salvation is through restraining the soul from desire, turning away from the world, and dedicating oneself earnestly to knowledge and piety. This cuts worldly ties and strengthens otherworldly bonds, so that at death the soul feels like escaping prison and reaching paradise. However, Ghazali then acknowledges that complete removal of all attachments is impossible due to bodily necessities. Instead, these ties can be weakened. He cites the Quranic verse 'There is none of you but will come to it' to show that some connection to the worldly or to Hell remains. But if the attachment is weak, the pain of separation is lessened, and the pleasure of witnessing divine matters at death overwhelms any residual longing. He illustrates with the analogy of a man called to a high position: he feels some pain leaving his home, but it is overshadowed by joy at the honor awaiting him.

Key Terms
العلاقة (attachment)
التقوى (piety)
الالتذاذ (pleasure)
التضعيف (weakening)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can completely remove all worldly attachments through asceticism.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states 'it is not possible to strip the soul of all these attributes entirely... but it is possible to weaken that relationship.' Complete removal is impossible; only weakening is attainable.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say about the possibility of completely removing worldly attachments from the soul?
A) It is possible through strict asceticism
B) It is impossible, but attachments can be weakened
C) It happens automatically at death
D) It requires divine intervention only

Key Takeaway
Though complete detachment is impossible, weakening attachments through knowledge and piety leads to salvation at death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which worldly attachment in your life do you find most difficult to weaken, and what would turning seriously to knowledge and piety actually cost you this week?
?
When you imagine the pain of leaving everything you love behind at death, does the hope of divine pleasure genuinely outweigh that pain in your heart right now? If not, what one step can you take today to make it more real?
?
Ghazali says complete detachment is impossible—so what 'necessary bodily needs' are you using as excuses to hold onto attachments that you could actually weaken? Name one.

Weakening the Soul's Attachment to Bodily Attributes
Ghazali explains the weakening of the soul's bodily attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the soul's attachment to bodily attributes cannot be completely severed but can be weakened, and how this reduces the pain of death.

Core Idea
Bodily ties cannot be cut, but they can be loosened to ease the soul's departure.

Ghazali tackles a practical question: if the soul is attached to bodily attributes, how can it ever leave peacefully? He argues that while complete elimination is impossible, weakening that attachment is achievable through spiritual discipline. This passage explains the mechanism and uses a vivid analogy to illustrate how the pain of separation is overcome by the joy of what follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how Ghazali states his main claim and then supports it with a Quranic verse and an analogy.
The passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where Ghazali says what is possible and what is not possible regarding bodily attributes. Underline the phrases that indicate impossibility versus possibility.
Look for 'لا يمكن' and 'يمكن'.
3
Understand the Quranic support
Ghazali quotes Quran 19:71 ('And there is none of you but will come to it'). Reflect on how this verse supports his point that the soul must pass through something (Hell/body) but can be eased.
The verse is about the obligatory passage through Hell, used as an analogy for the soul's journey.
4
Analyze the analogy
Consider the story of a person called to a high office. How does Ghazali use this to show that the pain of separation can be overcome by greater joy? Write down the elements of the analogy.
Focus on the contrast between initial pain and subsequent joy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يمكن سلب جميع هذه الصفات عن النفس، ومحوها بالكلية، فإن الضرورات البدنية، جاذبة إليها، إلا أنه يمكن تضعيف تلك العلاقة، ولذلك قال الله تعالى: [وإن منكم إلا واردها، كان على ربك حتما مقضيا]، إلا أنه إذا ضعفت العلاقة، لم تشتد نكاية فراقها، وعظم الالتذاذ بما اطلع عليه، عند الموت من الأمور الإلهية، فأماط أثر مفارقة الدنيا، والنزوع إليها، على فقد قرب، كمن يستنهض من وطنه، إلى منصب عظيم، وملك رفيع ترق نفسه حالة الفراق، على أهله ووطنه، فيتأذى أذى ما، ولكن ينمحى بما، من لذة الابتهاج بالملك والرئاسة. يستأنفه
And it is not possible to strip the soul of all these attributes and completely erase them, for bodily necessities pull toward them, except that it is possible to weaken that attachment. Therefore God Most High said: 'And there is none of you but will come to it' (19:71), except that when the attachment is weakened, the pain of separation does not become intense, and the delight in what is witnessed at death of divine matters becomes great, so it removes the effect of parting from the world and longing for it, upon the loss of proximity, like one who is summoned from his homeland to a great office and a lofty kingdom—his soul longs (tarqu nafsuhu) at the time of separation for his family and homeland, so he suffers some pain, but it is effaced by the delight of rejoicing in the kingdom and authority. He begins anew.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the soul's relation to bodily attributes. Ghazali begins by stating that it is impossible to completely strip the soul of all bodily attributes and erase them entirely, because the necessities of the body constantly pull the soul toward them. However, it is possible to weaken that attachment. He supports this with the Quranic verse 'And there is none of you but will come to it' (19:71), referring to the obligatory passage through Hell. He then explains that when the attachment is weakened, the pain of separation from the body at death is not severe, and the soul's delight in witnessing divine matters becomes so great that it removes the effect of parting from the world and longing for it. He gives an analogy: like a person who is called away from his homeland to a high position and a lofty kingdom—his soul yearns for his family and homeland at the moment of separation, so he experiences some pain, but that pain is erased by the joy of attaining the kingdom and authority. The phrase 'yasta'nifuhu' (he begins anew) suggests the soul starts a new life.

صفات (attributes)

تضعيف (weakening)

فراق (separation)

أمور إلهية (divine matters)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ghazali denies any attachment of the soul to the body, claiming it can be completely removed.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that complete elimination is impossible; only weakening is possible.

Key Takeaway
The soul's attachment to bodily attributes cannot be entirely removed, but it can be weakened so that the joy of divine vision outweighs the pain of death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ghazali's Classification of People by Knowledge and Practice
Ghazali classifies people into four categories based on knowledge and practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's fourfold classification of people based on the presence or absence of knowledge (ilm) and practice (amal), and the eternal consequences for each category.

Core Idea
Knowledge without practice leads to temporary punishment; practice without knowledge lacks complete happiness.

Ghazali presents a classification that integrates both intellectual and moral virtues. He distinguishes four types of people based on whether they possess knowledge and righteous practice. This passage clarifies the fate of each type in the afterlife, emphasizing that knowledge perfects the soul even if practice is lacking, while practice without knowledge brings salvation but not ultimate felicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four categories
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali enumerates the four types: the perished, the knower-worshipper, the knowledgeable sinner, and the one with practice only.
The Quranic verse 'He has succeeded who purifies it' sets the stage.
2
Understand the reasoning for temporary punishment
Focus on the knowledgeable sinner. Ghazali argues that because his soul is perfected by knowledge, the bodily stains will be effaced over time, so punishment is temporary.
This contrasts with the view that knowledge alone ensures eternal salvation.
3
Reflect on the implications for your own development
Consider how this classification encourages both seeking knowledge and practicing virtue. Ask: Which category describes me, and what should I strive for?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن عدم هذه الفضيلة في الخلق والعلم جميعاً، فهو الهالك، ولذلك قال الله تعالى: [قد أفلح من زكاها، وقد خاب من دساها]. المطلق ومن جمع الفضيلتين، العلمية والعملية، فهو العارف العابد، وهو السعيد المطلق. ومن له الفضيلة العلمية، دون العملية، فهو العالم الفاسق، ويتعذب مدة، ولكن لا يدوم. لأن نفسه قد كملت بالعلم، ولكن العوارض البدنية لطخته، تلطيخاً عارضاً، على خلاف جوهر النفس، وليس تتجدد الأسباب المجددة، فينمحى على طول الزمان. ومن له الفضيلة العملية دون العلمية، فيسلم وينجو عن الألم، ولكن لا يحظى بالسعادة الكاملة
And whoever lacks this virtue in both character (khuluq) and knowledge (ʿilm) altogether, he is the perished one (al-hālik). Therefore God Most High said: 'He has succeeded who purifies it, and he has failed who corrupts it' (91:9-10). And whoever combines the two virtues, the intellectual (al-ʿilmiyya) and the practical (al-ʿamaliyya), he is the knower-worshipper (al-ʿārif al-ʿābid), and he is the absolutely fortunate one (al-saʿīd al-muṭlaq). And whoever has the intellectual virtue without the practical, he is the knowledgeable sinner (al-ʿālim al-fāsiq); he will be tormented for a period, but it will not last. For his soul has been perfected through knowledge, but bodily accidents (al-ʿawāriḍ al-badaniyya) have stained him with a temporary stain, contrary to the essence of the soul, and the causes that renew it do not arise again, so it is effaced over length of time. And whoever has the practical virtue without the intellectual, he is saved and escapes pain, but does not attain complete happiness (al-saʿāda al-kāmila).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a classification of people based on two virtues: knowledge (ʿilm) and practice (ʿamal). Ghazali begins by stating that anyone who lacks both virtues in character and knowledge is the perished one (al-hālik), citing the Quranic verse 'He has succeeded who purifies it, and he has failed who corrupts it' (91:9-10). He then distinguishes three types: (1) The one who combines both intellectual virtue (al-ʿilmiyya) and practical virtue (al-ʿamaliyya) is the knower-worshipper (al-ʿārif al-ʿābid), who is absolutely fortunate (al-saʿīd al-muṭlaq). (2) The one who has intellectual virtue but lacks practical virtue is the knowledgeable sinner (al-ʿālim al-fāsiq); he will be tormented for a period, but the torment will not last because his soul has been perfected through knowledge, while bodily accidents have only stained it temporarily. These stains are contrary to the essence of the soul, and since no new causes renew them, they are effaced over time. (3) The one who has practical virtue but lacks intellectual virtue is saved from pain but does not attain complete happiness (al-saʿāda al-kāmila).

Key Terms

خلق
علم
عمل
عارف عابد
سعيد مطلق
عالم فاسق
عوارض بدنية
جوهر النفس
سعادة كاملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The knowledgeable sinner is eternally damned without hope of salvation.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that the knowledgeable sinner will be tormented for a period, but not eternally, because his soul is perfected by knowledge and the stains of bodily sins are temporary.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what happens to the knowledgeable sinner (al-ʿālim al-fāsiq) after death?
A) He is eternally tormented
B) He is tormented for a period but then purified because his soul is perfected by knowledge
C) He is immediately saved because of his knowledge
D) He is annihilated permanently

Key Takeaway
Complete happiness requires both knowledge and practice, but knowledge is more fundamental as it perfects the soul permanently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you honestly examine your own life, which category do you currently inhabit: the knower-worshipper, the knowledgeable sinner, the practitioner without knowledge, or the one who lacks both? What specific change will you make today to move toward the first category?
?
Are the 'bodily accidents' staining your soul temporary or are you actively renewing them through habits? What would it take for you to let them be effaced over time rather than replenished?
?
Do you secretly hope that practice without deep knowledge will be enough for you? The source says such a person is saved but does not attain complete happiness. Are you content with mere escape from pain, or will you pursue the knowledge that perfects the soul?

The Philosophers' View on Resurrection and Metaphorical Interpretation
Ghazali reports the philosophers' claim that death is personal resurrection and sensory descriptions are metaphors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that death is personal resurrection and that sensory descriptions in revelation are metaphors due to intellectual limitations.

Core Idea
For the philosophers, resurrection is personal death, and Paradise's descriptions are just metaphors.

This passage from Ghazali's Tahafut reports a key philosophical doctrine about the afterlife. The philosophers reinterpret bodily resurrection as occurring at each person's death, and argue that all sensory descriptions in revelation are merely metaphors for intellectual pleasures, because human minds cannot grasp the truth directly. Understanding this position is crucial for appreciating Ghazali's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style; Ghazali is reporting, not endorsing.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage presents two distinct claims: (1) death is personal resurrection, and (2) sensory descriptions in revelation are metaphors. Find each claim and note how they are connected.
The second claim supports the first by explaining away scriptural descriptions of bodily resurrection.
3
Understand the reasoning for metaphors
The philosophers argue that metaphors are used because human intellects are deficient (قصور الأفهام) in comprehending the true intellectual pleasures. God gives examples people understand, then informs them the reality is greater.
This reasoning is key to the philosophers' allegorical interpretation of scripture.
4
Reflect on Ghazali's framing
Ghazali concludes with 'فهذا مذهبهم' (This is their doctrine). Notice that he presents it as a report, not as refutation. This sets up his later critique.
In the Tahafut, Ghazali will argue against this view, defending bodily resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعموا: أن من مات فقد قامت قيامته. وأما ما ورد في الشرع، من الصور الحسية، فالقصد به ضرب الأمثال، لقصور الأفهام عن درك هذه اللذات فمثل لهم ما يفهمون، ثم ذكر لهم أن تلك اللذات فوق ما وصف لهم، فهذا مذهبهم
And they claimed: that whoever dies, his resurrection has occurred. As for what has come in the Revealed Law of sensory images, the intent with it is to strike metaphors, because of the deficiency of intellects from comprehending these pleasures; so He (God) gave them as an example what they understand, then He informed them that those pleasures are above what He described to them. This is their doctrine.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage reports a key philosophical doctrine regarding the afterlife. The philosophers claim that when a person dies, that is his personal resurrection (qiyāmatuhu qad qāmat), meaning there is no future bodily resurrection. As for the sensory descriptions found in the Revealed Law (shar‘) of Paradise, such as gardens, rivers, and physical pleasures, they say these are intended as metaphors (ḍarb al-amthāl) because human intellects are too deficient to grasp the true intellectual pleasures of the afterlife. So God gives examples that people can understand, and then indicates that the true pleasures are far greater than what was described. Ghazali concludes: 'This is their doctrine.'

Key Terms
قيامة (resurrection)
صور حسية (sensory images)
ضرب الأمثال (striking metaphors)
قصور الأفهام (deficiency of intellects)
لذات (pleasures)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may mistakenly think this philosophical view is compatible with Islamic teachings on bodily resurrection.
Correction: Ghazali clearly presents this as a philosophical reinterpretation that denies bodily resurrection, which is a core Islamic belief. The passage states that death itself is resurrection, so no future bodily return occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers as reported by Ghazali, why does the Revealed Law use sensory images to describe the afterlife?
A) Because those images literally describe Paradise
B) Because human intellects cannot grasp purely intellectual pleasures, so metaphors are used
C) Because the philosophers reject any description of the afterlife
D) Because the Quran is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
The philosophers deny a future bodily resurrection, equating it with death, and reinterpret all sensory afterlife descriptions as metaphors for intellectual pleasures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself sometimes tempted to reinterpret clear verses about the afterlife to fit your own understanding? What does this tell you about the condition of your heart toward divine revelation?
?
The philosophers claimed that intellects are deficient in grasping spiritual realities. Have you ever trusted your intellect over clear revelation? What will you do to ensure your mind submits to the Quran and Sunnah?
?
If you were to face someone who argued that Paradise's descriptions are only metaphors, how would you respond using this very passage? Are you prepared to defend the literal belief in bodily resurrection?

Ghazali's Four Points of Conflict with Sharia
Ghazali enumerates four philosophical denials conflicting with Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four specific philosophical denials that Ghazali identifies as contradicting Islamic teachings on bodily resurrection and the physical afterlife.

Core Idea
Ghazali pinpoints four bodily afterlife denials that conflict with Sharia.

In this passage from the *Tahafut al-Falasifa*, Imam al-Ghazali moves from general critique to specific points of conflict. He lists four denials that he considers irreconcilable with the revealed law. This lesson unpacks each denial and sets the stage for Ghazali's refutation by quoting the philosophers themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the four denials and notice the methodological note at the end.
2
Understand the term 'مخالف للشرع'
Ghazali uses this term to denote doctrines that are contrary to the Revealed Law. Recognize that not all philosophical views are condemned, only these specific ones.
3
Examine each denial
Go through each denial one by one: bodily resurrection, bodily pleasures in Paradise, bodily pains in Hell, and the existence of Paradise and Hell as described in the Quran.
4
Reflect on the methodological note
Notice Ghazali's statement that he must quote the texts of the Islamic philosophers. This indicates his method of refutation: presenting their own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن المخالف للشرع منها: إنكار حشر الأجساد. وإنكار اللذات الجسمانية في الجنة. وإنكار الآلام الجسمانية في النار. وإنكار وجود الجنة والنار، كما وصف في القرآن. أرى الواجب يقتضينى أن أروى نصوص الفلاسفة الإسلاميين، في هذا
But what conflicts with the Revealed Law among them (their doctrines) are: the denial of the gathering (ḥashr) of bodies, and the denial of bodily pleasures (al-ladhdhāt al-jismāniyya) in Paradise, and the denial of bodily pains (al-ālām al-jismāniyya) in Hell, and the denial of the existence of Paradise and Hell as described in the Quran. I see that duty requires me to quote the texts of the Islamic philosophers regarding this.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a list of specific philosophical doctrines that contradict the Sharia. Ghazali identifies four denials: (1) denial of the gathering (ḥashr) of bodies, i.e., bodily resurrection; (2) denial of bodily pleasures in Paradise; (3) denial of bodily pains in Hell; (4) denial of the existence of Paradise and Hell as described in the Quran. He then adds an editorial note: 'I see that duty requires me to quote the texts of the Islamic philosophers regarding this,' indicating his intention to present verbatim quotations from those philosophers, likely to support his refutation. This note may be part of the author's method.

Key Terms
مخالف للشرع (contrary to Revealed Law)
حشر الأجساد (gathering of bodies)
لذات جسمانية (bodily pleasures)
آلام جسمانية (bodily pains)
جنة (Paradise)
نار (Hell)
نصوص (texts)
فلاسفة إسلاميون (Islamic philosophers)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is accusing all philosophers of denying the afterlife entirely.
Correction: Ghazali specifically lists only these four denials regarding the physical and bodily aspects, not a complete denial of any afterlife.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed by Ghazali as a philosophical doctrine conflicting with the Sharia?
A) Denial of bodily resurrection
B) Denial of spiritual rewards
C) Denial of bodily pleasures in Paradise
D) Denial of Paradise and Hell as described in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Ghazali identifies four specific philosophical denials that directly contradict Islamic teachings on bodily resurrection and the physical afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the afterlife, do you truly believe in a physical resurrection and physical rewards and punishments, or have you unconsciously started to spiritualize these realities? Which of the four denials do you find yourself most tempted to downplay?
?
Ghazali says duty requires him to quote the philosophers. Are you willing to confront and examine opposing views on the afterlife, or do you avoid them? What would it cost you to engage with a perspective that denies bodily resurrection?
?
Have you ever heard a fellow Muslim subtly dismiss the physical descriptions of Paradise and Hell as metaphorical? How does this passage strengthen your resolve to affirm the Quranic descriptions literally? What concrete step will you take this week to solidify your belief in the physical afterlife?

The Four Denials Contrary to Revelation
Ghazali enumerates four denials that contradict scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four specific denials that Ghazali identifies as contrary to Islamic revelation.

Core Idea
Four bodily eschatological realities that philosophy cannot deny.

In this passage, Ghazali cuts to the chase: he lists the specific denials that make the philosophers' doctrine unacceptable. These are not abstract points but concrete, bodily realities affirmed by scripture. The passage is a clear enumeration of four non-negotiable elements of Islamic eschatology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noticing the four items enumerated with 'وإنكار'. Let the rhythm of the list sink in.
The word 'إنكار' (denial) is repeated for each point.
2
Identify each denial
List the four denials: bodily resurrection, bodily pleasures in Paradise, bodily pains in Hell, and the physical existence of Paradise and Hell as described in the Qur'an.
These correspond to the key terms: حشر الأجساد, اللذات الجسمانية, الآلام الجسمانية, الجنة والنار.
3

The Possibility of Combining Two Happinesses
A rhetorical question challenging the separation of two types of happiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's rhetorical challenge to the philosophers' separation of spiritual and bodily happiness, and the implication that both are attainable together.

Core Idea
Spiritual and bodily happiness need not be mutually exclusive; both can coexist.

In this lesson, we encounter a single, powerful rhetorical question from Imam al-Ghazali. He asks the philosophers: why can't spiritual and bodily happiness be combined? This question cuts to the heart of the debate about the nature of the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the rhetorical question
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the tone: it's a challenge, not a statement.
The question begins with 'So what prevents...' implying that there is no logical barrier.
2
Identify the dichotomy
The philosophers claimed that happiness in the afterlife is either purely spiritual or purely bodily. Ghazali challenges this by asking why both cannot be combined.
3
Reflect on the implication
If both can be combined, then the philosophers' framework is too narrow. This supports the Islamic view that the afterlife includes both spiritual and bodily experiences.

Source Text

Source pages p. 288

This short sentence is a rhetorical question that challenges the philosophers' dichotomy between spiritual and bodily happiness. Ghazali asks: what prevents the possibility that in the afterlife a person experiences both spiritual and bodily happiness (or misery) together? The question implies that the philosophers' separation is unnecessary; revelation affirms both, and reason does not preclude their combination.

Ibn Sina's Two Kinds of Resurrection
Ibn Sina distinguishes bodily resurrection (revealed) from spiritual (rational).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina distinguishes between bodily resurrection (accepted from revelation) and spiritual resurrection (known by reason), and why he prioritizes spiritual happiness.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection is from revelation; spiritual resurrection is from reason — both are true.

This passage from Ibn Sina's al-Najah presents a key distinction regarding the afterlife. Ibn Sina affirms bodily resurrection as a matter of faith, while also arguing that spiritual resurrection can be known through rational demonstration. He further explains that the divine philosophers seek spiritual happiness above all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Get an overview of Ibn Sina's main points.
Pay attention to the colon after 'فنقول:' — it signals further explanation.
2
Identify the two kinds of resurrection
The passage explicitly divides resurrection into two types. Find where each is introduced and note their names.
3
Note the epistemic status of each
Observe how Ibn Sina states the source of knowledge for each: revelation only for bodily, reason and prophecy for spiritual.
Bodily: 'مقبول من الشرع' (accepted from revelation). Spiritual: 'مدرك بالعقل' (perceived by reason).
4
Observe the priority of spiritual happiness
Ibn Sina says divine philosophers desire spiritual happiness more than bodily, considering it 'approximation to the First Truth'. Reflect on why this prioritization is significant.
This shows a philosophical preference for intellect over physical pleasures.
5
Consider the context of Ghazali's critique
Recall that Imam Ghazali quotes this passage to later critique the philosophers' claims about spiritual resurrection. Think about what might be contentious.
Ghazali accepts bodily resurrection but challenges the rational proof of spiritual resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام : ، وإليك ما يقوله « ابن سينا » فى « النجاة ( ص ٤٧٧ مطبعة السعادة لسنة ١٣٣١ هـ : يجب أن تعلم أن المعاد منه مقبول من الشرع ، ولا طريق إلى إثباته إلا من طريق الشريعة وتصديق خبر النبوة ، وهو الذي للبدن عند البعث ، وخيرات البدن وشر و ره معلومة لا تحتاج إلى أن تعلم وقد بسطت الشريعة الحقة ، التي أتانا بها نبينا المصطفى - محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم - حال السعادة والشقاوة ، التي بحسب البدن . · ومنه ما هو مدرك بالعقل والقياس البرهانى ، وقد صدقته النبوة ، وهو السعادة والشقاوة ، الثابتان بالمقاييس التى للأنفس ، وإن كانت الأوهام منا تقصر عن تصورها الآن ، لما نوضح من العمل . · والحكماء الإلهيون ، رغبتهم في إصابة هذه السعادة ، أعظم من رغبتهم في إصابة السعادة البدنية ، بل كأنهم لا يلتفتون إلى تلك ، وإن أعطوها ، فلا يستعظمونها فى جانب هذه السعادة ، التي هي مقاربة الحق الأول ، فلنعرف حال هذه السعادة ، والشقاوة المضادة لها ، فإن البدنية مفروغ منها في الشرع ، فنقول :
The position: And here is what Ibn Sina says in al-Najah (p. 477, al-Sa'ada Press, 1331 AH): You should know that of the resurrection, part is accepted from revelation, and there is no way to prove it except through the path of the Sharia and belief in the report of prophecy, and it is that which is for the body at the resurrection, and the goods and evils of the body are known and do not need to be learned, and the true Sharia, which our chosen Prophet Muhammad (may God bless him and grant him peace) brought to us, has expounded the state of happiness and misery that pertain to the body. And part of it is that which is perceived by reason and demonstrative syllogism, and prophecy has confirmed it, and it is the happiness and misery that are established by the measures that pertain to souls, even if our imaginations fall short of conceiving them now, due to what we will clarify from action. And the divine philosophers, their desire to attain this happiness is greater than their desire to attain bodily happiness; rather, they pay no attention to that, even if they are given it, they do not consider it great alongside this happiness, which is approximation to the First Truth. So let us know the state of this happiness and the misery opposite to it, for the bodily [happiness] is settled in revelation. So we say:
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a direct quotation from Ibn Sina's al-Najah. It introduces a division of resurrection into two types. The first is bodily resurrection, which is accepted solely from revelation and cannot be proven by reason; its details are given in the Sharia. The second is spiritual resurrection, which is known by reason and confirmed by prophecy; it involves the happiness and misery of souls. Ibn Sina then notes that the divine philosophers prioritize this spiritual happiness over bodily happiness, because it is closeness to the First Truth (God). He proposes to explain this spiritual happiness and its opposite misery, since the bodily aspect is already covered in revelation. The phrase 'فنقول:' indicates the beginning of his detailed explanation.

Key Terms

المعاد البدني
المعاد الروحاني
الشريعة
النبوة
الحكماء الإلهيون
مقاربة الحق الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ibn Sina denies bodily resurrection.
Correction: Ibn Sina affirms bodily resurrection as a matter of revelation, but emphasizes spiritual resurrection as the higher, rational goal.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, how is bodily resurrection known?
A) By reason alone.
B) By revelation only.
C) By both reason and revelation.
D) By introspection.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina affirms bodily resurrection from revelation and spiritual resurrection from reason, prioritizing the latter as closeness to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Prophetic Hadith and the Principle of Combining Pleasures
Ghazali cites the hadith of unseen pleasures and argues for combining both worlds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses a prophetic hadith to argue that the promised unseen pleasures do not exclude other pleasures, and that combining both is more complete.

Core Idea
The promised pleasures are not exclusive; combining both worlds is the most complete.

This lesson examines a key passage from Imam Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa, where he engages with a famous prophetic tradition. Ghazali argues that the unseen pleasures promised to the righteous do not negate other pleasures; rather, the most perfect state is to have both. This refutes the philosophers' claim that only intellectual pleasures exist in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and Ghazali's argument
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the hadith quoted and the logical inference Ghazali draws from it.
The hadith is a 'qudsi' hadith, where God speaks through the Prophet.
2
Identify the two types of pleasures
The passage contrasts the promised hidden pleasures ('what no eye has seen...') with other pleasures (likely intellectual or spiritual). Understand that Ghazali is not denying the latter.
3
Understand the logical structure
Ghazali argues that the existence of one set of pleasures does not negate the other. He then states that combining both is more complete. Trace this reasoning.
This is a refutation of exclusivity claims.
4
Draw the conclusion
The conclusion is that since the promised pleasures are the most complete and possible, we must affirm them in accordance with the Sharia. Reflect on the role of revelation in this argument.
Ghazali emphasizes 'tasdiq' (affirmation) based on shari'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : [ أعددت لعبادي الصالحين، ما لا عين رأت ، ولا أذن سمعت ولا خطر على قلب بشر ] فكذلك وجود هذه الأمور الشريفة ، لا يدل على نفى غيرها ، بل الجمع بين الأمرين أكمل ، والموعود به أكمل الأمور ، وهو ممكن ، فيجب التصديق به على وفق الشرع
And His saying: 'I have prepared for My righteous servants what no eye has seen, nor ear heard, nor has it occurred to the heart of man' – likewise the existence of these noble things does not indicate the negation of others, rather combining both matters is more complete, and the promised is the most complete of matters, and it is possible, so it is necessary to affirm it according to the Sharia.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument based on a prophetic tradition (hadith qudsi). The author begins by quoting the well-known hadith that God has prepared for His righteous servants what no eye has seen, ear heard, or heart conceived. He then makes the point that the existence of these promised noble things (the pleasures of Paradise) does not imply the negation of other pleasures (presumably the intellectual or spiritual pleasures of the afterlife that the philosophers speak of). On the contrary, combining both types of pleasures is more complete. The promised thing (the unseen pleasures) is the most complete of matters, and it is possible (i.e., not impossible). Therefore, one must affirm it in accordance with the revealed law (shar'). The argument is that the philosophers might claim that only intellectual pleasures exist, but the hadith shows that there are also sensory-like pleasures that are far beyond human imagination. The author asserts that these do not exclude other pleasures; rather, having both is superior.

Key Terms
أعددت لعبادي الصالحين
الجمع بين الأمرين أكمل
التصديق على وفق الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The promised unseen pleasures are the only pleasures of the afterlife.
Correction: The author argues they are not exclusive; rather, combining both promised and other pleasures is more complete.

Checkpoint

A) To prove that only sensory pleasures exist in Paradise
B) To argue that the promised pleasures exclude all other pleasures
C) To show that combining both promised and other pleasures is more complete
D) To deny the possibility of intellectual pleasures

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses the hadith to show that the unseen pleasures do not negate other pleasures; affirming both according to Sharia is the proper response.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Ghazali's Numbness Analogy for Afterlife Reality
Ghazali answers the objection that scriptural descriptions are mere examples for common people using the numbness analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the numbness analogy to affirm the real, non-sensory nature of afterlife pleasure and pain, countering the claim that scriptural descriptions are mere metaphors for common people.

Core Idea
Numbness hides true pleasure and pain; the veil of the body removed, we experience the afterlife as real and unimaginable.

Ghazali is responding to a philosopher who argues that scriptural descriptions of Paradise and Hell are simply examples (amthal) tailored for common understanding—analogous to anthropomorphic verses about God. The philosopher implies that the promised pleasures are not real. Ghazali turns this objection on its head by using the analogy of a numb limb: just as numbness can hide intense pain or pleasure, our embodied life veils the true realities of the afterlife. Upon death, the veil is lifted, and we experience them fully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
First, read the objector's claim that scriptural descriptions are merely examples struck for common people, akin to anthropomorphic verses. Note how this challenges the reality of afterlife pleasures and pains.
The objector is essentially accusing Scripture of metaphorical exaggeration.
2
Follow the numbness analogy for punishment
Ghazali first uses the analogy to describe the punishment of the wicked: like a numb limb that does not feel fire until the covering is removed, sinners are oblivious to the painful realities they will face after death.
This part shows that the promised punishment is real, not just a scare tactic.
3
Follow the numbness analogy for pleasure
Then Ghazali applies the same analogy to the righteous: they receive the most delicious spiritual pleasure once the numbness of the body is gone, a pleasure far beyond sensory delight.
This affirms the reality and superiority of spiritual pleasure over physical.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how Ghazali uses the same analogy to validate both punishment and pleasure. The key is that the obstacle (the body) prevents us from experiencing these realities now, but they are real and await us.
Ghazali is not denying literalism; he is explaining our present inability to perceive these realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما ورد في الشرع ، أمثال ضربت ، على حد أفهام الخلق كما أن الوارد من أيات التشبيه وأخباره ، أمثال على حد فهم الخلق ؛ والصفات الإلهية مقدسة" ، عما يتخيله عوام الناس . وكما ينسى المرض الالتذاذ بالحلو اشتهاءه ، وتميل الشهوة بالمريض ، إلى المكروهات في الحقيقة ، عرض لها حينئذ من الألم بفقدانه ، كفء ما يعرض من اللذة التي أوجبنا وجودها ، ودللنا على عظم منزلتها ، فيكون ذلك هو الشقاوة ، والعقوبة التى لا يعدلها تفريق النار للاتصال وتبديلها ، وتبديل الزمهرير للمزاج ، فيكون مثلنا حينئذ مثل الخدر الذى أومأنا إليه فيما سلف ، أو الذي عمل فيه نار أو زمهرير ، فمنعت المادة الملابسة وجه الحس ، من الشعور به فلم يتأذ ، ثم عرض أن زال العائق ، فشعر بالبلاء العظيم وأما إذا كانت القوة العقلية ، بلغت من النفس من الكمال ، يمكنها به إذا فارقت البدن ، أن تستكمل الاستكمال التام ، الذى لها أن تبلغه ، كان مثلها مثل الخدر ، الذي أذيق المطعم الألذ ، وعرض للحال الأشهى ، وكان لا يشعر به ، فزال عنه الخدر ، فطالع اللذة العظيمة دفعة ، وتكون تلك اللذة لا من جنس اللذة الحسية والحيوانية ، بوجه ، بل لذة تشاكل الحال الطيبة ، التي للجواهر الحية المحضة ، وهى أجل من كل لذة وأشرف
If it is said: What has come in the Law are examples struck, according to the understanding of the people, just as what has come of anthropomorphic verses and reports are examples according to the understanding of the people; and the divine attributes are sanctified above what common people imagine. And just as illness forgets the pleasure of sweetness and its craving, and the appetite inclines the patient to things that are actually disliked, then there occurs for it from the pain of its loss a counterpart of the pleasure whose existence we have affirmed and whose great rank we have demonstrated. Then that is the misery and punishment that is not equalled by the scattering of fire on the organism and its transformation, nor the transformation of freezing cold on the temperament. Then our example at that time is like the numb limb we referred to earlier, or that in which fire or freezing cold was applied, and the covering substance prevented the face of sense from feeling it, so it was not pained, then the obstacle was removed, and it felt the great calamity. And as for when the rational faculty has reached such a perfection of the soul that it can, upon separation from the body, complete the full perfection that it is to attain, its example is like the numb limb which was offered the most delicious food and the most desirable state, and it was not feeling it, then the numbness was removed from it, so it beheld the great pleasure all at once. And that pleasure is not of the genus of sensory and animal pleasure at all, but a pleasure similar to the good state of the pure living substances, and it is greater and nobler than every pleasure.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer. The objector claims that scriptural descriptions of Paradise and Hell (like 'what no eye has seen') are merely examples (amthāl) struck for common people's understanding, similar to anthropomorphic verses about God which are not to be taken literally. The objector implies that the promised pleasures are not real sensory pleasures but only metaphors. The author answers using an analogy of a numb limb (khadar). He explains that just as illness can make a person forget the pleasure of sweetness and even crave harmful things, similarly, in this life we are like a numb limb that does not feel fire or extreme cold because of a covering substance. Once the obstacle is removed, the person feels the great pain. Similarly, for the righteous, the rational faculty reaches perfection, and upon death (separation from body), the numbness is removed, and they experience a non-sensory, non-animal pleasure of immense magnitude. This pleasure is of a completely different order – it belongs to the pure living substances (al-jawāhir al-ḥayya al-maḥḍa). The author emphasizes that this pleasure is not sensory at all, thereby reconciling the scriptural descriptions with the philosophical concept of intellectual pleasure: the scriptural descriptions are true, but they point to a reality far beyond what we can conceive in this life.

Key Terms
أمثال ضربت
التشبيه
الخدر
الشقاوة والعقوبة
اللذة العظيمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali is rejecting scriptural literalism and claiming afterlife descriptions are mere metaphors.
Correction: Ghazali actually affirms the reality of afterlife pleasure and pain; he uses the numbness analogy to explain why we cannot conceive them now, not to deny their literal truth.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the numbness analogy in answering the objection?
A) To show that scriptural descriptions are false
B) To illustrate how one can be oblivious to pleasure or pain until the veil is removed
C) To prove that the afterlife is purely spiritual
D) To deny the existence of sensory pleasures in Paradise

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's numbness analogy defends the literal reality of afterlife pleasure and pain by explaining that our bodily existence prevents us from experiencing them now, but they are real and will be fully felt after death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that the numbness of this body is hiding the full reality of your actions, what would you do today to prepare for the moment the numbness is removed?
?
Ghazali says that illness can make a person forget the pleasure of sweetness and even crave harmful things. Where in your life has spiritual 'illness' made you forget the sweetness of faith and turn toward what is actually harmful? What is one step you can take this week to remove that covering?
?
The numbness analogy means you could be feeling great pleasure or great pain right now without realizing it. What if you are actually in a state of misery but are numb to it? How would you find out, and what would you do if you knew?

Ghazali on Happiness, Misery, and the Fate of Seekers
Ghazali defines true happiness and misery and explains who attains each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of true happiness and misery in the afterlife, and who among humanity is destined for each.

Core Idea
True happiness is intellectual perfection; misery is the failure of those who yearned but fell short.

In this passage, Ghazali distinguishes true happiness from mere pleasure and explains that misery in the afterlife is not for all who are deficient, but specifically for those who have acquired a yearning for intellectual perfection but failed to attain it. He also addresses the fate of simple souls and categorizes the miserable into the negligent and the deniers. This lesson unpacks his definitions and classifications.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall argument.
Pay attention to the flow of ideas.
2
Identify the definition of happiness and misery
Note that Ghazali begins by stating 'This is happiness, and that is misery' and then explains the condition for misery: it is for those who have acquired yearning for intellectual perfection.
This sets the stage for the rest of the passage.
3
Understand the role of yearning and opinion
Examine how yearning is acquired through demonstration and that it follows an opinion, which is not innate but acquired. This distinguishes those who are capable of misery from simple souls.
Yearning is a key concept here.
4
Learn the two types of miserable souls
Ghazali categorizes the miserable into: the negligent (who did not strive for perfection) and the deniers (who are bigoted and hold false opinions). Deniers are worse.
Note the distinction between negligence and active denial.
5
Reflect on the approximate measure
Ghazali admits he cannot specify exactly how much conceptualization is needed for salvation, only approximately. This shows humility and the limits of human knowledge.
This concludes the passage.

فهذه هي السعادة ، وتلك هي الشقاوة . وليست تلك الشقاوة تكون لكل واحد من الناقصين ، بل للذين أكسبوا القوة العقلية ، الشوق إلى كمالها ، وذلك عندما يبرهن لهم ، أن من شأن النفس إدراك ماهية الكمال ، بكسب المجهول من المعلوم ، والاستكمال بالفعل ، فإن ذلك ليس فيها بالطبع الأول ، ولا أيضاً في سائر القوى ، بل شعور أكثر القوى بكمالاتها ، إنما يحدث بعد أسباب . وأما النفوس والقوى الساذجة الصرفة ، فكأنها هيولى موضوعة ، لم تكتسب البتة هذا الشوق ، لأن هذا الشوق ، إنما يحدث حدوثاً ، وينطبع في جوهر النفس ، إذا تبرهن للقوى النفسانية ، ههذا أموراً ، يكتسب العلم بها بالحدود الوسطى ، على ما علمت ، وأما قبل ذلك ، فلا يكون ، ن الشوق يتبع رأياً ، وليس هذا الرأى للنفس أولياً ، بل رأياً مكتسباً . لأن هؤلاء إذا اكتسبوا هذا الرأى ، لزم النفس ضرورة هذا الشوق ، فإذا فارقت ولم يحصل معها ، ما تبلغ به بعد الانفصال ، إلى التمام ، وقعت فى هذا النوع من الشقاء الأبدى ، لأن أوائل المملكة العلمية ، إنما كانت تكتسب بالبدن لا غير ، وقد فات . وهؤلاء إما مقصرون عن السعى ، في كسب الكمال الإنسى ، وإما مساندون جاحدون متعصبون لآراء فاسدة مضادة للآراء الحقيقية ، والجاحدون أسوأ حالا ، لما كسبوا من هيئات مضادة للكمال . وأما أنه كم ينبغى ، أن يحصل عنه نفس الإنسان ، من تصور المعقولات ، حتى تجاوز به الحد ، الذي في مثله تقع هذه الشقاوة ، وفى تعديه وجوازه ، ترجى هذه السعادة ؟ ! فليس يمكنى أن أنص عليه نصاً إلا بالتقريب
This is happiness, and that is misery. And that misery is not for every one of the deficient, but for those who have acquired for the rational faculty the yearning for its perfection. And that is when it is demonstrated to them that it is the nature of the soul to perceive the essence of perfection by acquiring the unknown from the known, and to become perfect in actuality. For that is not in it by primary nature, nor in the other powers. Rather, the awareness of most powers of their perfections only occurs after causes. As for the simple, pure souls and powers, they are like passive matter (hayūlā), which have not acquired this yearning at all. Because this yearning only occurs and becomes imprinted in the substance of the soul when it is demonstrated to the psychic powers that there are things whose knowledge is acquired by middle terms, as you know. Before that, it does not occur. For yearning follows an opinion, and this opinion is not a primary (or innate) opinion for the soul, but an acquired opinion. Because when these acquire this opinion, this yearning necessarily follows for the soul. Then when it departs (the body) and does not have with it what it can reach completion after separation, it falls into this type of eternal misery, because the beginnings of the intellectual kingdom are only acquired through the body and no other, and it has passed. These are either deficient in striving to acquire human perfection, or they are opponents, deniers, bigoted to corrupt opinions contrary to true opinions. And the deniers are in a worse state because of the dispositions they have acquired that are contrary to perfection. As for how much conceptualization of intelligibles the human soul must attain so as to cross the threshold beyond which this misery occurs, and by exceeding and passing it one may hope for this happiness – I cannot specify it exactly except approximately.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of true happiness and misery in the afterlife. The author first declares: 'This is happiness, and that is misery.' He then elaborates that this misery is not for everyone who is deficient, but specifically for those who have acquired for their rational faculty (al-quwwa al-ʿaqliyya) a yearning (shawq) for its perfection. This yearning arises when it is demonstrated to the soul that it can perceive the essence of perfection by acquiring knowledge from the known to the unknown (i.e., through syllogistic reasoning). This desire is not innate; it is acquired through learning. Those who do not acquire this yearning are like pure matter (hayūlā) – they have no such desire. The yearning follows an opinion (ra'y) that is not primary but acquired. If a person acquires this opinion and the corresponding yearning, but dies before actually achieving that perfection, they fall into eternal misery because the means of acquiring that knowledge (the body) is gone. He categorizes such people into two groups: those who were negligent in seeking human perfection, and those who were obstinate deniers (jāḥidūn) with bigoted, false opinions. The deniers are worse because they have acquired dispositions contrary to perfection. Finally, he admits he cannot specify exactly how much conceptualization is needed for salvation, only approximately.

الشقاوة
الشوق إلى الكمال
النفوس الساذجة الصرفة
الآراء الفاسدة
الحدود الوسطى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That everyone who is not a philosopher is doomed to eternal misery.
Correction: Ghazali specifies that misery is only for those who have acquired the yearning for intellectual perfection through demonstration and then failed to achieve it. Simple souls who never acquired this yearning are like passive matter and are not subject to this misery.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, who experiences eternal misery?
A) All deficient souls
B) Only those who acquired a yearning for intellectual perfection but did not achieve it
C) Only deniers of truth, not the negligent
D) All souls that are simple and pure

Key Takeaway
Eternal misery, according to Ghazali, is reserved for those who have come to know the path to intellectual perfection but fail to walk it, not for the simple or ignorant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you genuinely acquired the yearning for intellectual perfection, or are you content to remain a passive soul, like hayūlā, with no desire for higher knowledge? What evidence is there in your life of this yearning?
?
When you encounter a truth that challenges your comfortable opinions, do you embrace it with humility, or do you cling to your views out of bigotry like the deniers Ghazali warns about?
?
If your soul were to depart this body tonight, would it have already attained enough conceptualization of the intelligibles to hope for happiness, or would it fall into the misery of unfulfilled yearning? What step will you take today to move closer to perfection?

The Philosopher's Objection: Happiness and Misery as Intellectual States
The philosopher objects that pleasure and pain are intellectual, not physical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that true happiness and misery are intellectual states, not physical, and the analogies used to support this claim.

Core Idea
True happiness and misery are intellectual, experienced after death, not physical.

In this objection, the philosophers challenge the literal interpretation of paradise and hell. They argue that the descriptions in revelation are metaphors for the common people, just as anthropomorphic verses are metaphors. The real happiness and misery are intellectual states of the soul after death, experienced through the removal of barriers that numb the soul. The objector uses vivid analogies of illness and numbness to explain how the soul perceives pleasure and pain.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: opening claim, analogies, and detailed explanation of who suffers misery.
This is a dense passage. Take your time.
2
Identify the two analogies
Identify the analogy of illness (sickness causing distorted desires) and the analogy of numbness (barrier to sensation). Understand how each illustrates the soul's experience after death.
The illness analogy relates to loss of pleasure; the numbness analogy relates to sudden perception of affliction or pleasure.
3
Distinguish the two types of souls
Note the difference between souls that have acquired a longing for intellectual perfection and those that are naive. The objector says misery only applies to those who have the longing but fail to attain perfection.
4
Reflect on the objector's conclusion
Consider the admission that the threshold for escaping misery cannot be specified exactly. This shows the philosophical nature of the argument.
This is a key point: the objector is speaking from a rational perspective, not revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما ورد في الشرع ، أمثال ضربت ، على حد أفهام الخلق كما أن الوارد من آيات التشبيه وأخباره ، أمثال على حد فهم الخلق ؛ والصفات الإلهية مقدسة عما يتخيله عوام الناس .
وكما ينسى المرض الالتذاذ بالحلو اشتهاءه ، وتميل الشهوة بالمريض ، إلى المكروهات في الحقيقة ، عرض لها حينئذ من الألم بفقدانه ، كفء ما يعرض من اللذة التي أوجبنا وجودها ، ودللنا على عظم منزلتها ، فيكون ذلك هو الشقاوة ، والعقوبة التي لا يعدلها تفريق النار للاتصال وتبديلها ، وتبديل الزمهرير للمزاج ، فيكون مثلنا حينئذ مثل الخدر الذي أومأنا إليه فيما سلف ، أو الذي عمل فيه نار أو زمهرير ، فمنعت المادة الملابسة وجه الحس ، من الشعور به فلم يتأذ ، ثم عرض أن زال العائق ، فشعر بالبلاء العظيم وأما إذا كانت القوة العقلية ، بلغت من النفس من الكمال ، يمكنها به إذا فارقت البدن ، أن تستكمل الاستكمال التام ، الذي لها أن تبلغه ، كان مثلها مثل الخدر ، الذي أذيق المطعم الألذ ، وعرض للحال الأشهى ، وكان لا يشعر به ، فزال عنه الخدر ، فطالع اللذة العظيمة دفعة ، وتكون تلك اللذة لا من جنس اللذة الحسية والحيوانية ، بوجه ، بل لذة تشاكل الحال الطيبة ، التي للجواهر الحية المحضة ، وهي أجل من كل لذة وأشرف
فهذه هي السعادة ، وتلك هي الشقاوة .
وليست تلك الشقاوة تكون لكل واحد من الناقصين ، بل للذين أكسبوا القوة العقلية ، الشوق إلى كمالها ، وذلك عندما يبرهن لهم ، أن من شأن النفس إدراك ماهية الكمال ، بكسب المجهول من المعلوم ، والاستكمال بالفعل ، فإن ذلك ليس فيها بالطبع الأول ، ولا أيضاً في سائر القوى ، بل شعور أكثر القوى بكمالاتها ، إنما يحدث بعد أسباب .
وأما النفوس والقوى الساذجة الصرفة ، فكأنها هيولى موضوعة ، لم تكتسب البتة هذا الشوق ، لأن هذا الشوق ، إنما يحدث حدوثاً ، وينطبع في جوهر النفس ، إذا تبرهن للقوى النفسانية ، هذه أموراً [كذا] ، يكتسب العلم بها بالحدود الوسطى ، على ما علمت ، وأما قبل ذلك ، فلا يكون ، فالشوق يتبع رأياً ، وليس هذا الرأي للنفس أولياً ، بل رأياً مكتسباً .
فهؤلاء إذا اكتسبوا هذا الرأي ، لزم النفس ضرورة هذا الشوق ، فإذا فارقت ولم يحصل معها ، ما تبلغ به بعد الانفصال ، إلى التمام ، وقعت في هذا النوع من الشقاء الأبدي ، لأن أوائل المملكة العلمية ، إنما كانت تكتسب بالبدن لا غير ، وقد فات . وهؤلاء إما مقصرون عن السعي ، في كسب الكمال الإنسي ، وإما مساندون جاحدون متعصبون لآراء فاسدة مضادة للآراء الحقيقية ، والجاحدون أسوأ حالا ، لما كسبوا من هيئات مضادة للكمال
وأما أنه كم ينبغي ، أن يحصل عنه نفس الإنسان ، من تصور المعقولات ، حتى تجاوز به الحد ، الذي في مثله تقع هذه الشقاوة ، وفي تعديه وجوازه ، ترجى هذه السعادة ؟ ! فليس يمكني أن أنص عليه نصاً إلا بالتقريب
For it is said: What has come in the law are parables struck on the measure of the understanding of the people, just as what has come of the verses of anthropomorphism and their reports are parables on the measure of the people's understanding; and the divine attributes are sanctified above what the common people imagine. And just as illness forgets the pleasure of sweets despite craving for them, and the appetite of the sick person inclines to things that are in reality detestable, then there occurs to him at that time from the pain of its loss an equivalent of what occurs from the pleasure whose existence we affirmed and on the greatness of whose rank we gave proof; so that becomes misery and punishment which the tearing apart of fire for continuity and its alteration, and the alteration of extreme cold for the temperament, cannot equal. So our example then is like the numbness we pointed to previously, or one on which fire or extreme cold acts, but the material covering prevents the sense organ from feeling it, so he is not harmed; then the obstacle is removed, and he feels the great affliction. And as for when the rational power has reached from the soul such perfection that it can, when it separates from the body, attain the complete perfection that it is able to reach, its example is like the numbness of one who is given to taste the most delicious food and the most desirable state, but he does not feel it; then the numbness leaves him, and he suddenly perceives the great pleasure. And that pleasure is not of the genus of sensory and animal pleasure at all, but a pleasure resembling the good state of the pure living substances, and it is more sublime and noble than every pleasure. So this is happiness, and that is misery. And that misery is not for every one of the deficient, but for those who have acquired for the rational power the longing for its perfection; and that is when it is demonstrated to them that it is of the nature of the soul to perceive the essence of perfection by acquiring the unknown from the known, and to attain perfection in act; for that is not in it by first nature, nor in the other powers, but the awareness of most powers of their perfections only occurs after causes. As for the naive and pure souls and powers, they are like a disposed matter that has not acquired this longing at all, because this longing only occurs and becomes impressed in the substance of the soul when it is demonstrated to the psychic powers that these are things whose knowledge is acquired by middle terms, as you know; but before that, it does not exist, for longing follows an opinion, and this opinion is not primary for the soul but an acquired opinion. So these, when they acquire this opinion, this longing necessarily attaches to the soul; then when it separates and what would enable it to reach completion after separation is not attained, it falls into this type of eternal misery, because the beginnings of the intellectual kingdom were only acquired by the body and nothing else, and that has lapsed. These are either negligent in striving to acquire human perfection or assisting deniers fanatically devoted to corrupt opinions contrary to true opinions; the deniers are in a worse state because of the dispositions they have acquired contrary to perfection. As for how much the human soul needs to obtain of conceptions of intelligibles so that it surpasses the limit at which this misery occurs, and by exceeding and passing it this happiness is hoped for—I cannot specify it exactly except by approximation.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is the objection (فإن قيل) from the philosophers. The objector argues that the descriptions in the Quran and Hadith of paradise and hell are mere metaphors (أمثال) struck for the common people, just as verses of anthropomorphism (تشبيه) are metaphorical; the divine attributes are sanctified beyond what people imagine. The objector then presents an analogy: just as a sick person loses taste for sweets and desires harmful things, and when deprived of pleasure feels pain, similarly the soul after death experiences either pain of loss or pleasure of attainment, but these are intellectual, not physical. The objector describes two cases: the soul that has achieved intellectual perfection experiences immense non-sensual pleasure, like a person whose numbness is removed and suddenly tastes delicious food; this is true happiness (سعادة). The soul that lacks perfection experiences great pain, like a person who is numb to fire but feels it when the barrier is removed; this is misery (شقاوة). The objector then clarifies that this misery only applies to those who have acquired a desire for intellectual perfection but failed to attain it, not to utterly naive souls. These are either negligent or stubborn deniers, and the latter are worse. The objector admits he cannot specify the exact threshold of intellectual perception needed to escape this misery except by approximation.

سعادة
قوة عقلية
لذة عقلية
خدر
أمثال

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is Ghazali's own view.
Correction: The objection is presented as a claim to be refuted; Ghazali will later respond to it.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that true happiness and misery are intellectual states of the soul after death, using analogies of illness and numbness to explain how barriers prevent perception of these states.

Ghazali's Refutation: Metaphorical Verses vs. Detailed Descriptions
Ghazali refutes the objection by distinguishing metaphorical verses from detailed descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first argument for why the detailed descriptions of paradise and hell cannot be interpreted metaphorically like other verses.

Core Idea
Detailed afterlife descriptions are too specific to be metaphors.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to an objection that all scriptural descriptions are metaphorical. He counters by distinguishing between two types of verses: those that admit of metaphorical interpretation (like anthropomorphic descriptions) and those that are so detailed and specific that they cannot be interpreted away. He argues that equating them is arbitrary. This lesson examines his first distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the English translation. Notice how Ghazali begins his answer and the key terms he uses.
Pay attention to the structure: 'الجواب' then 'أحدهما'.
2
Identify the two types of verses
Ghazali distinguishes between 'الألفاظ الواردة في التشبيه' (words occurring in tashbih) and 'ما ورد في وصف الجنة والنار' (what has come in description of paradise and hell). Understand the difference in their nature.
3
Understand why equating them is problematic
Ghazali says equating them is arbitrary. The first type admits of interpretation (تأويل) due to Arab custom of metaphor, but the detailed descriptions do not. To claim they are metaphors would lead to deception (تلبيس), which is beneath prophethood.
This connects to the sanctity of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب ، أن التسوية بينهما تحكم ، بل هما يفترقان من وجهين : أحدهما : أن الألفاظ الواردة في التشبيه ، تحتمل التأويل على عادة العرب في الاستعارة ، وما ورد في وصف الجنة والنار ، وتفصيل تلك الأحوال بلغ مبلغاً لا يحتمل التأويل ، فلا يبقى إلا حمل الكلام على التلبيس ، بتخييل نقيض الحق ، لمصلحة الخلق ، وذلك ما يتقدس عنه منصب النبوة .
And the answer: that equating between them is arbitrary; rather they differ in two respects. One of them: that the words occurring in tashbih admit of interpretation according to the Arab custom of metaphor, whereas what has come in the description of the Garden and the Fire and the detailing of those states has reached a level that does not admit of interpretation; so nothing remains except to take the speech as deception, by imagining the opposite of the truth for the benefit of creation, and that is something from which the station of prophethood is sanctified.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the answer (الجواب) to the objection. Ghazali states that the two cases are not equal; they differ in two aspects. The first aspect: the metaphorical verses (آيات التشبيه) admit of interpretation (تأويل) based on Arab usage of metaphor (استعارة), but the detailed descriptions of paradise and hell (ما ورد في وصف الجنة والنار وتفصيل تلك الأحوال) have reached a level of detail that does not admit of interpretation. To deny their literal sense would be to resort to deception (تلبيس) by creating false imagery for the benefit of the people, and that is far beneath the station of prophethood (منصب النبوة). Thus, the objection's claim that all descriptions are metaphors is refuted.

Key Terms
تأويل
تشبيه
استعارة
تلبيس
منصب النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All scriptural descriptions of the afterlife are metaphorical and can be interpreted away.
Correction: Ghazali argues that detailed descriptions of paradise and hell are too specific to admit of metaphorical interpretation; doing so would imply deception by the Prophet.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Ghazali gives for why the descriptions of paradise and hell cannot be mere metaphors?
A) Because they are found in both Quran and Hadith.
B) Because they do not admit of interpretation due to their detailed nature.
C) Because the Prophet never used metaphors.
D) Because the common people would not understand them.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that detailed descriptions of the afterlife are too specific to be metaphorical, so they must be taken literally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ghazali says that dismissing detailed afterlife descriptions as metaphors would ‘force you to say the Prophet was deceiving for a benefit.’ Do you treat the Prophet's words about the unseen with the same absolute trust as his words about prayer and fasting? If not, what does that reveal about your heart's submission to his prophethood?
?
This passage draws a line: some verses can be interpreted, others cannot. Is there an area of your faith—heaven, hell, angels, judgment—where you prefer reinterpretation over submission because the literal meaning feels uncomfortable? Will you surrender to the literal word, even when it challenges you?
