The Principle of Interpreting Divine Attributes and Afterlife Promises
Ghazali states the rule of ta'wil for divine attributes and literal interpretation for the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intellectual proofs necessitate figurative interpretation (ta'wil) of certain divine attributes, while matters of the afterlife are to be taken literally.

Core Idea
Intellect forces ta'wil for God's attributes, but the afterlife is literal.

Before delving into Ibn Sina's theory of the soul's purification, Ghazali establishes a crucial hermeneutical principle: intellectual proofs necessitate interpreting certain divine attributes (like direction and body parts) figuratively, while the promised matters of the Hereafter, being within God's power, must be taken at face value. This sets the stage for a rational discussion of the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how Ghazali contrasts two categories: what reason necessitates interpreting and what must be taken literally.
Focus on the phrase 'فوجب التأويل' vs 'فيجب إجراؤه على ظاهر الكلام'.
2
Identify the reasoning
Ask yourself: Why can't Allah have direction or a hand? Why must those be interpreted? And why are afterlife matters not subject to the same interpretation?
The key is that intellectual proofs show certain attributes are impossible for God, but the afterlife is within His power.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this principle affects the overall argument. It allows Ibn Sina to discuss the soul's afterlife experiences in a literal sense, even if some divine attributes are metaphorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن أدلة العقول ، دلت على استحالة المكان ، والجهة ، والصورة ، ويد الجارحة ، وعين الجارحة ، وإمكان الانتقال، والاستقرار ، على الله سبحانه وتعالى ، فوجب التأويل بأدلة العقول . وما وعد به من أمور الآخرة ، ليس محالا في قدرة الله تعالى ، فيجب إجراؤه على ظاهر الكلام ، بل على فحواه الذي هو صريح فيه بل عن جهته، فإن التوسط يسلب عنها الطرفين دائماً، ثم جوهر النفس، إنما كان البدن هو الذي يغمره ويلهيه، ويغفله عن الشوق الذى يخصه، وعن طلب الكمال الذى له، وعن الشعور بلذة الكمال، إن حصل له، أو الشعور بألم النقصان، إن قصر عنه، لا بأن النفس منطبعة في البدن، ومنغمسة فيه، ولكن بالعلاقة التى كانت بينهما وهى الشوق الجبلى، إلى تدبيره، والاشتغال بآثاره، و بما يورده عليه من عوارضه، وبما يتقرر فيه، من ملكات مبدؤها البدن . فإذا فارق، وفيه المملكة الحاصلة بسبب الاتصال به، كان قريب الشبه من حاله وهو فيه، فها ينقص من ذلك، تزول غفلته عن حركة الشوق الذي له إلى كماله، و بما يبقى منه معه، يكون محجوباً عن الاتصال الصرف بمحل سعادته، ويحدث هناك من الحركات المشوشة، ما يعظم أذاه ثم إن تلك الهيأة البدنية، مضادة لجوهرها مؤذنة هما، وإنما كان يلهيها عنها أيضاً البدن وتمام انغماسها فيه، فإذا فارقت النفس البدن أحست بتلك المضادة العظيمة، وتأذت بها أذى عظيما، لكن هذا الأذى وهذا الألم، ليس لأمر لازم، بل لأمر عارض غريب، والعارض الغريب لا يدوم ولا يبقى، فيزول ويبطل مع ترك الأفعال، التي كانت تثبت تلك الهيأة بتكرارها، فيلزم إذن أن تكون العقوبة، التي بحسب ذلك، غير خالدة، بل تزول وتنمحى قليلا قليلا، حتى تزكو النفس وتبلغ السعادة التي تخصها .
The second: that intellectual proofs indicate the impossibility of place, direction, form, a bodily hand, a bodily eye, the possibility of movement, and settlement with respect to Allah, Exalted and High, so interpretation (ta'wil) became necessary based on intellectual proofs. What He promised of matters of the Hereafter is not impossible for the power of Allah, Exalted, so it must be taken according to the apparent meaning of the speech, rather according to its purport which is explicit in it. Rather from its direction, for the middle always deprives both ends. Then the substance of the soul: the body is only that which overwhelms it, distracts it, and makes it heedless of the longing specific to it, and of seeking the perfection that belongs to it, and of feeling the pleasure of perfection if it obtains it, or feeling the pain of deficiency if it falls short. Not because the soul is impressed upon the body and immersed in it, but because of the relationship that was between them, which is the innate longing to manage it, and preoccupation with its effects, and with what befalls it from its accidents, and with what becomes established in it of dispositions whose origin is the body. When it separates, while having the acquired disposition due to connection with it, it is close in similarity to its state while in it. So from that, its heedlessness of the movement of longing toward its perfection ceases, and by what remains with it from it, it is veiled from pure connection to the place of its felicity. And there arise there from disturbing movements whose harm is great. Then that bodily state is opposed to its substance and harmful to it, and the body and its complete immersion in it used to distract it from it. When the soul leaves the body, it feels that great opposition and is harmed by it with great harm. But this harm and this pain is not due to an essential thing, but due to an accidental foreign thing. The accidental foreign thing does not last nor remain, so it vanishes and ceases with abandoning the actions that used to establish that state by repetition. It follows then that the punishment according to that is not eternal, but rather vanishes and is obliterated little by little until the soul becomes pure and attains the felicity specific to it.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of Ibn Sina's theory regarding the soul's experience after death, as presented by Ghazali. It begins by establishing that intellectual proofs necessitate interpreting certain divine attributes (like direction, hand, etc.) figuratively (ta'wil), but matters of the afterlife, since they are within God's power, should be taken literally. Then it explains the nature of the soul: the body overwhelms the soul and distracts it from its innate longing for perfection. The soul is not essentially immersed in the body; rather, it has a relationship of innate longing to manage the body. After death, the soul retains acquired dispositions from the body and suffers from the loss of bodily pleasures, but this pain is not essential—it is accidental and arises from the bodily attachments. Because it is accidental, it fades when the soul ceases the actions that reinforce those attachments. Thus, the punishment is not eternal but diminishes until the soul is purified and attains its specific felicity. The key point is that the soul's suffering is temporary and due to accidental causes, not its essence.

Key Terms
أدلة العقول
التأويل
ظاهر الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must certain divine attributes like direction or a bodily hand be interpreted figuratively?
A) Because the Quran uses ambiguous language.
B) Because intellectual proofs show they are impossible for God.
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) interpreted them that way.
D) Because they contradict the afterlife promises.

Key Takeaway
Afterlife promises are to be taken literally because they are within God's power, unlike impossible attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture the pleasures of Paradise—such as rivers of milk or beautiful gardens—do you ever feel a subtle resistance to taking them absolutely literally? What part of your heart hesitates, and what does this lesson demand of you in that moment?
?
Have you ever fallen into a comfortable 'middle path' regarding God's attributes—neither affirming a literal hand nor fully committing to ta'wil? How does Ghazali's warning about tawassut expose the danger in that middle ground?
?
If you truly believed that the afterlife promises are as literal as the ground under your feet, would your priorities, fears, or hopes shift in a tangible way? What concrete change would that make in your life today?

How the Body Veils the Soul from Its Perfection
Ibn Sina's description of the soul's distraction by the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the body overwhelms the soul and distracts it from its innate longing for perfection.

Core Idea
The body distracts the soul from its true longing for perfection.

Having established the literal interpretation of afterlife, Ghazali now explains Ibn Sina's view on the soul's relationship with the body. The body is not a prison but an overwhelming veil that distracts the soul from its natural inclination towards its own perfection. Even after death, traces of bodily attachments remain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the contrast between the body's role and the soul's essential nature.
Key phrases: 'يغمره ويلهيه' (overwhelms and distracts), 'الشوق الجبلى' (innate longing).
2
Understand the relationship
The passage clarifies that the soul is not literally in the body; rather, there is a relationship of innate longing. Identify what this relationship consists of.
It is a longing to manage the body and be preoccupied with its affairs.
3
Consider the after-death state
After death, the soul retains dispositions from the body. Imagine how this causes disturbance and prevents pure connection with felicity.
The phrase 'حركات مشوشة' indicates disturbing movements.

The Temporary Nature of the Soul's Post-Death Suffering
Ibn Sina's explanation of accidental suffering and purification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul's suffering after death is accidental and not eternal, and how it eventually purifies.

Core Idea
Soul's suffering is accidental, not essential, so it fades.

In this final part of the passage, Ghazali presents Ibn Sina's argument that the pain the soul experiences after death is not inherent to its substance but arises from an accidental bodily state. Because it is accidental, it can be removed by ceasing the actions that sustain it, leading to eventual purification and attainment of felicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the distinction between essential (lazim) and accidental (aarid gharib).
Key terms: 'عارض غريب' (accidental foreign thing) and 'غير خالدة' (not eternal).
2
Follow the argument
Trace the logical steps: bodily state is opposed to the soul → after death, the soul feels this opposition → but this is accidental → so it vanishes when actions cease → thus punishment is not eternal.
3
Understand the purification
The process: abandoning actions that reinforce the bodily state leads to gradual obliteration of suffering until the soul becomes pure (tazku) and attains its felicity.
The verb 'تزكو' implies purification and growth.

Source Text

?

?
You believe in the Hereafter, but do you live as if your daily choices are building an accidental state that will cause you temporary punishment? What one action can you change starting now to ensure your soul's felicity, not its prolonged suffering?

The Fate of Dull Souls and Imaginative Afterlife Experiences
Ibn Sina describes the dull souls' afterlife through imaginative experiences and the ultimate spiritual fate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's theory of the afterlife for dull souls, the role of imagination, and the critique that this theory undermines bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
Imaginary experiences in the afterlife can be more real and intense than physical ones.

Ibn Sina presents a detailed account of what happens to souls that did not ascend to intellectual perfection. He explains how their beliefs and habits shape a purely psychic afterlife, using celestial bodies as vehicles for imagination. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the implications for bodily resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of dull souls
Read the first part of the passage and note the two categories of dull souls: those without longing who receive mercy, and those with vile bodily states who suffer intense torment due to unfulfilled longing.
The torment arises from attachment to the body without the possibility of fulfilling those desires.
2
Understand the imaginative afterlife mechanism
Focus on the section describing how souls with ingrained beliefs imagine eschatological states using celestial bodies as tools. Notice that these imaginative experiences are more potent than sensory ones.
The author likens this to dreams but asserts eschatological images are more stable.
3
Compare dream and wakeful perception
Analyze the explanation that both dream and wakeful perceptions are imprints in the soul; external objects are only accidental causes. The essential cause of pleasure or pain is the imprint itself.
This reinforces the reality of imaginative afterlife experiences.
4
Evaluate the critique of bodily resurrection
Read the editorial comment at the end. The commentator claims Ibn Sina's rational part contradicts his acceptance of bodily resurrection by making felicity depend on liberation from the body, implying no return of the body.
Consider whether this critique is valid based on Ibn Sina's own statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفوس البله، التى لم تكتسب الشوق، فإنها إذا ما فارقت البدن، وكانت غير مكتسبة للهيئات البدنية الردية، صارت إلى سعة من رحمة الله، وذوع من الراحة؛ وإن كانت مكتسبة للهيئات البدنية الردية، وليس عندها هيأة غير ذلك، ولا معنى يضاده وينافيه، فتكون لا محالة ممنوة بشوقها إلى مقتضاها، فتتعذب عذاباً شديداً، بفقد البدن، ومقتضيات البدن، من غير أن يحصل المشتاق إليه، لأن آلة ذلك قد بطلت، وخلق التعلق بالبدن قد بقى. ويشبه أيضاً أن يكون ما قاله بعض العلماء حقاً أن هذه الأنفس، إن كانت زكية، وهو وفارقت البدن، وقد رسخ فيها نحو من الاعتقاد فى العاقبة، التى تكون لأمثالهم، على ما يمكن أن يخاطب به العامة، وتصور في أنفسهم من ذلك، فإنهم إذا فارقوا الأبدان، ولم يكن لهم معنى جاذب إلى الجهة، التى فوقهم، لإتمام كمال، فتسعد تلك السعادة، ولا شوق كمال، فتشقى تلك الشقاوة، بل جميع هيئاتهم النفسانية متوجهة نحو الأسفل، منجذبة إلى الأجسام، ولا منع في المواد السماوية، عن أن تكون موضوعة لفعل نفس فيها، قالوا فإنها تتخيل ما كانت اعتقدته، من الأحوال الأخروية، وتكون الآلة التي يمكنها بها التخيل، شيئاً من الأجرام السماوية فتشاهد ما قيل لها في الدنيا، من أحوال القبر، والبعث، والخيرات الأخروية، وتكون الأنفس جميع الردية أيضاً، تشاهد العقاب المصور لهم فى الدنيا، وتقاسيه، فإن الصور الخيالية، ليست تضعف عن الحسية، بل تزداد عليها تأثيراً وصفاء، كما يشاهد ذلك فى المنام، فربما كان المحكوم به أعظم شأناً، في بابه من المحسوس، على أن الأخروى أشد استقراراً من الموجود في المنام، بحسب قلة العوائق، وتجرد النفس، وصفاء القابل، وليست الصورة التى ترى في المنام، والتي تحس في اليقظة، كما علمت، إلا المرتسمة في النفس لا أن إحداهما تبتدئ من باطن، وتنحدر إليها والثانية تبتدى من خارج، وترتفع إليها، فإذا ارتسمت في النفس تم هن ك إدراك المشاهدة، وإنما يلذ ويؤذى بالحقيقة هذا المرتسم في النفس، لا الموجود من خارج، فكل ما ارتسم في النفس، فعل فعله، وإن لم يكن سبب من خارج، فإن السبب الذاتى، هو هذا المرتسم، والخارج بالعرض، أو سبب السبب، سبب. فهاتان هما السعادة والشقاوة الخسيستان، واللتان بالقياس إلى الأنفس الخسيسة. وأما الأنفس المقدسة، فإنها تبعد عن مثل هذه الأحوال، وتتصل بكمالها بالذات، وتنغمس في اللذة الحقيقية، وتتبرأ عن النظر إلى ما خلفها، وإلى المملكة التى كانت لها، كل التبرى، ولو كان بقى فيها أثر من ذلك اعتقادى أو خلق، تأذت وخلفت لأجله، عن درجة عليين، إلى أن ينفسخ عنها [؟] هذا هو رأى « ابن سينا ( فى البعث ، وهو - كما ترى - شطران : (۱) شطر رجع فيه إلى الشريعة المحمدية ، وما جاء فيها عن بعث البدن ونعيمه ، وعذابه وقد أمن بكل ذلك وأذعن له (ب) وشطر رجع فيه إلى العقل ، وما تأدى إليه من بعث الروح ونعيمها وعذابها ، وقد حكى كل ذلك أيضاً حكاية المذعن المؤمن . والذي لا يستطيع المنصف أن يمارى فيه ، أن ما جاء فى الشطر الثاني ، يكاد يودي بما جاء فى الشطر الأول ، إذ قد جعل مناط السعادة والشقاوة فى الخلاص من البدن ، فالنفوس التي توفرت لديها أسباب السعادة ، إنما كان يمنعها من الشعور بها البدن ، فإذا خلعته وتخلصت منه ، استذوقت سعادتها واستكملتها ، والنفوس التى توافرت لديها أسباب الشقاوة ، إنما كان يحول بينها وبين الشعور بها ، البدن وشواغله ، فإذا ألقته جانباً ، تأذت وتألمت ولقد ورد في عبارته ما يفيد أن كلا الصنفين من النفوس سيفارق بدنه إلى غير رجعة ، ومعنى هذا إنكار البعث الجسماني ، وما يترتب عليه من نعيم البدن وعذابه .
As for the dull souls, those that did not acquire longing, when they leave the body, and they have not acquired the vile bodily states, they will go into an expanse of Allah's mercy and a scope of comfort. If they have acquired vile bodily states, and they have no other state nor anything that opposes and contradicts it, then inevitably they are afflicted with longing for what they require, so they suffer intense torment due to losing the body and its requirements, without obtaining what is longed for, because the tool for that has ceased, and the innate attachment to the body remains. It also resembles that what some scholars said is true: that these souls, if they are pure, and they left the body, and there has become ingrained in them some belief about the final outcome that will be for their likes, according to what the common people can be addressed with, and they imagined in themselves from that, then when they leave the bodies, and they have no meaning attracting them to the direction above them to complete perfection, so they would not attain that felicity, and no longing for perfection so as to suffer that misery. Rather all their psychic states are directed downward, pulled toward bodies, and there is no prevention in celestial materials from being subject to the action of a soul in them. They said: then they will imagine what they used to believe of the eschatological states, and the tool through which they can imagine will be something from the celestial bodies. So they will witness what was said to them in the world about the states of the grave, resurrection, and eschatological goods. And all vile souls likewise will witness the punishment depicted for them in the world and endure it. For imaginary images are not weaker than sensory ones; rather they surpass them in effect and clarity, as is witnessed in dreams. Perhaps what is judged is greater in significance in its category than the sensed, moreover the eschatological is more stable than what exists in a dream, due to fewer obstacles, the soul's abstraction, and the purity of the recipient. And the image seen in a dream and that sensed in wakefulness are, as you know, only imprinted in the soul, not that one originates from within and descends to it, and the second originates from outside and rises to it. When it is imprinted in the soul, then the perception of witnessing is complete. In reality, only this imprinted thing gives pleasure or pain, not what exists externally. So whatever is imprinted in the soul, its effect occurs, even if there is no external cause, for the essential cause is this imprint, and what is external is accidental or a cause of the cause. So these two are the lowly felicity and misery, which are in relation to lowly souls. As for the holy souls, they are far from such states, and they connect to their perfection essentially, and they immerse in true pleasure, and they completely dissociate from looking at what is behind them and at the kingdom they had. If any trace of that, be it belief or disposition, remained in them, they would be harmed and would fall from the rank of the highest (Illiyin) until it is dissolved from them. This is the view of Ibn Sina on resurrection, and it is, as you see, two parts: (1) a part in which he returned to the Islamic Sharia and what came in it about bodily resurrection and its bliss and punishment, and he believed in all that and submitted; (2) a part in which he returned to reason and what it led to about the resurrection of the soul and its bliss and punishment, and he narrated all that likewise as a believing submitter narrates. The impartial person cannot dispute that what came in the second part almost destroys what came in the first part, since he made the locus of felicity and misery in liberation from the body. So souls that have abundant causes of felicity, the body used to prevent them from feeling them; when they shed it and become free, they taste their felicity and complete it. Souls that have abundant causes of misery, the body and its preoccupations used to prevent them from feeling them; when they cast it aside, they suffer and are pained. And it came in his statement that implies that both kinds of souls will leave their bodies forever, and the meaning of this is denial of bodily resurrection and what follows from it of bodily bliss and punishment.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage continues Ibn Sina's theory, discussing the dull souls that did not acquire longing for virtue. If they have not acquired vile bodily states, they receive mercy and comfort. If they have acquired such states, they suffer intense longing for bodily pleasures without fulfillment, causing severe torment. Then the passage presents a view attributed to some scholars: souls with ingrained beliefs about the afterlife will imagine those states using celestial bodies as tools. This imaginative experience is more potent than sensory experience, similar to dreams but more stable. The passage explains that in both dreams and wakefulness, perception is ultimately an imprint in the soul; the external object is only an accidental cause. Thus, the afterlife experiences of lowly souls are imaginative but real in effect. Holy souls transcend such states and achieve true spiritual perfection. Finally, the editorial comment summarizes Ibn Sina's view as having two aspects: one accepting bodily resurrection from revelation, and another reducing it to spiritual resurrection based on reason. The comment criticizes this as effectively denying bodily resurrection, since Ibn Sina's rational part makes felicity and misery dependent on liberation from the body, implying no return of the body.

Key Terms
النفوس البله
الصور الخيالية
الأجرام السماوية
النفوس المقدسة
البعث الجسماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imaginative experiences are less real than sensory ones.
Correction: The passage argues that imaginary images can be more potent and stable than sensory ones, as seen in dreams, and that the essential cause of pleasure/pain is the imprint in the soul, not the external object.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the dull souls that have acquired vile bodily states after death?
A) They immediately attain felicity.
B) They suffer intense torment from longing for bodily pleasures without fulfillment.
C) They are annihilated.
D) They are reborn into new bodies.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's vision of an imaginative afterlife for lower souls ultimately raises questions about the necessity of physical resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the afterlife, do you focus more on physical pleasures or spiritual closeness to Allah? Does this lesson reveal where your true attachment lies? What would you sacrifice today to shift that focus?
?
If you died right now, which category of soul would you belong to: one with vile bodily states longing for what is lost, or one with pure belief ready for mercy? Are you actively purifying your soul from attachment to the world, or just hoping for the best?
?
Ibn Sina argues that imaginative experiences can be more potent than physical ones. Have you ever experienced such deep inner conviction or fear that changed your actions? Are you using the power of your imagination to cultivate longing for Allah and the Hereafter, or are you letting it fixate on worldly desires?

Three Divisions of the Return to Bodies
Ghazali enumerates three divisions of the return to bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct classifications of the return to bodies as presented by Imam al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
The return to bodies can be understood in three distinct ways.

Imam al-Ghazali presents the first approach to the return of bodies, which divides possible interpretations into three categories. This classification helps clarify the various positions held by theologians and philosophers on bodily resurrection. Understanding these divisions is essential before examining Ghazali's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the three divisions. Notice how each division is introduced with 'إما أن يقال'.
The passage is the beginning of Ghazali's first approach.
2
Identify each division
Note the three distinct views: (a) person is body only, life is accident; (b) body matter persists as dust; (c) soul persists and body is reassembled.
Use the key terms to help distinguish them.
3
Reflect on the differences
Consider how each view defines the human being and the nature of death and resurrection. Which view do you find most plausible?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الأول : قالوا : تقدير العود إلى الأبدان ثلاثة أقسام . إما أن يقال : الإنسان عبارة عن البدن . والحياة التي هي عرض ، قائم به كما ذهب إليه بعض المتكلمين ، وأما النفس التي هي قائمة بنفسها ، ومدبرة للجسم ، فلا وجود لها ، ومعنى الموت انقطاع الحياة ، أى امتناع الخالق عن خلقها ، فتنعدم ، والبدن أيضاً ينعدم ، ومعنى المعاد إعادة الله تعالى للبدن ، الذي انعدم ، و رده إلى الوجود ، وإعادة الحياة التي انعدمت أو يقال : مادة البدن تبقى تراباً ، ومعنى العاد أن يجمع ويركب على شكل آدمی ، وتخلق فيه الحياة ابتداء . فهذا قسم . و إما أن يقال : النفس موجودة ، وتبقى بعد الموت ، ولكن يرد البدن الأول يجمع تلك الأجزاء بعينها
The first path: They said: The estimation of the return to bodies has three divisions. Either it is said that the human being is the body, and life is an accident subsisting in it, as some theologians held, and the soul that is self-subsisting and governing the body does not exist; the meaning of death is the cessation of life, i.e., the Creator refrains from creating it, so it becomes non-existent, and the body also becomes non-existent; and the meaning of the return is that God Almighty restores the body that became non-existent and returns it to existence, and restores the life that became non-existent. Or it is said that the matter of the body remains as dust, and the meaning of the return is that it is gathered and assembled into a human form, and life is created in it anew. So this is one division. Or it is said that the soul exists and remains after death, but the first body is returned by gathering those very parts.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage classifies the possible views on the return of bodies into three divisions. The first view identifies the human being solely with the body; life is an accident, and at death both body and life vanish completely. The afterlife is a re-creation of the body and life. The second view holds that the body's matter persists as dust, and at resurrection it is re-formed and given new life. The third view posits that the soul is a self-subsisting entity that survives death, and the body is returned by reassembling its original parts. This classification helps frame the debate on bodily resurrection in Islamic theology.

Key Terms
تقدير العود إلى الأبدان
عرض
النفس القائمة بنفسها
انقطاع الحياة
إعادة الله تعالى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that all Muslims hold the same view on bodily resurrection.
Correction: This passage shows three distinct theological positions on how the return to bodies occurs.

Checkpoint
How many divisions of the return to bodies are presented in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The return to bodies is classified into three distinct theological views, each with different implications for the nature of the human being and resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the first division were true and you were only a body with no soul, how would that change the way you view your physical actions and their consequences? Are you preparing your body for a resurrection you believe in?
?
Which of these three views most closely matches your current understanding of yourself? What does that view demand from you today in terms of your spiritual and physical discipline?
?
If the third division is correct and your soul survives death, are you nurturing your soul now as the part of you that will be held accountable? What habit do you need to change to take that responsibility seriously?

Fanari's Note on Bodily Resurrection Advocates
Fanari notes two aspects of affirming both body and soul in resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical position of al-Ghazali and al-Farabi on bodily resurrection and the two aspects of affirming both body and soul.

Core Idea
Even philosophers like al-Farabi affirmed bodily resurrection, though possibly with a new body.

This note from Fanari clarifies a subtle point in the debate on resurrection. Many assume that philosophers who affirmed spiritual resurrection denied bodily resurrection, but here we see that al-Ghazali and al-Farabi are cited as holding the view that both body and soul are involved, albeit with two possible interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is a citation within a citation—Fanari quoting al-Sayyid al-Sharif quoting a commentator.
2
Identify the two aspects
The passage presents two ways to understand the affirmation of both body and soul. Find each one and note the difference: restoration of the first body or attachment to a new body.
3
List the scholars mentioned
The passage explicitly names al-Ghazali and al-Farabi as holding this opinion. Consider why this might be surprising for al-Farabi.
Al-Farabi is often associated with a purely spiritual resurrection, so this note shows a more nuanced view.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this affect our understanding of the Islamic philosophical tradition regarding resurrection? This note suggests that even the falasifa could be interpreted as accepting bodily resurrection in some form.
This connects to broader debates about the compatibility of philosophy with Islamic creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما هو جدير بالذكر أيضاً فى هذا المقام ، ما يرويه الفنارى - - ٨ ص ۲۹۷ من المواقف – تعليقاً على قول السيد الشريف : [ والثالث - أى من الأقوال فى البعث – ثبوتهما معاً - أى الجسم والروح - من قول شارح الصحائف : وهذا على وجهين أحدهما : أن يكون الروح مجرداً عن المادة ، فيعاد الجسم ويتعلق به الررح ، أو يتعلق ، من غير إعادة الجسم الأولى ، وهذا مذهب قليل من أهل العلم ، « كالغزالي » و « الفارابي » . . فعلى هذا يكون « الفارابي » أيضاً قائلا بالبعث الجسماني . بجسم آخر
And what is also worth mentioning in this context is what al-Fanari relates—from volume 8, page 297 of al-Mawaqif—commenting on the saying of al-Sayyid al-Sharif: 'The third—i.e., among the opinions on resurrection—is the affirmation of both together—i.e., the body and the soul—from the statement of the commentator of al-Sahayif: This has two aspects: one is that the soul is immaterial, then the body is restored and the soul attaches to it, or it attaches without restoring the first body. This is the doctrine of a few scholars, such as al-Ghazali and al-Farabi.' On this basis, al-Farabi is also a proponent of bodily resurrection. With another body.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a marginal note citing al-Fanari's commentary on al-Mawaqif. It refers to the third opinion on resurrection, which affirms both body and soul. The commentator of al-Sahayif explains this opinion has two aspects: either the soul is immaterial and the original body is restored, or the soul attaches to a new body without restoring the first one. This view is attributed to a few scholars, notably al-Ghazali and al-Farabi. The note concludes that al-Farabi should also be considered a proponent of bodily resurrection, though possibly with a different body. This helps clarify the historical position of these philosophers.

Key Terms
ثبوتهما معاً (affirmation of both body and soul)
الروح مجرداً (soul immaterial)
البعث الجسماني (bodily resurrection)
الغزالي (al-Ghazali)
الفارابي (al-Farabi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Farabi denied bodily resurrection entirely.
Correction: According to this note, al-Farabi affirmed bodily resurrection in the sense of the soul attaching to a new body, thus he is considered a proponent of bodily resurrection.

Checkpoint
According to this note, which two scholars are mentioned as holding the opinion of affirming both body and soul in resurrection?
A) Ibn Sina and al-Farabi
B) Al-Ghazali and al-Farabi
C) Al-Ghazali and Ibn Sina
D) Ibn Rushd and al-Ghazali

Key Takeaway
This note shows that even philosophers like al-Farabi were considered to affirm some form of bodily resurrection, highlighting the nuance in Islamic theological debates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your body will be raised again, even if it is a different body? How does this belief affect how you treat your current body — the one you will leave behind?
?
If a philosopher like al-Farabi could affirm bodily resurrection despite his rationalist leanings, what excuse do you have for doubting it? Have you ever let intellect override revelation on this matter? What will you change today to submit fully to Allah's promise of resurrection?

The First Two Positions on the Return of the Body
Ghazali states the first two of three philosophical positions on bodily resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two positions that philosophers hold regarding bodily resurrection and the role of the soul in each.

Core Idea
Two views on resurrection: body only, or soul plus exact body.

This lesson introduces the first two philosophical positions on how the body returns on the Day of Resurrection. Ghazali is laying out the views he will later critique. Understanding these positions is crucial for following his arguments about the nature of the soul and the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the structure 'إما أن يقال...' which marks the two divisions.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the first position (body only, life as accident) and its two sub-variants. Then the second position (soul exists, body returns with exact parts).
3
Compare the roles of the soul
Notice how the first position denies an independent soul, while the second affirms it. This difference is central to Ghazali's critique.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why the exact return of parts matters for the second position. The commentary will clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الأول : قالوا : تقدير العود إلى الأبدان ثلاثة أقسام .
إما أن يقال : الإنسان عبارة عن البدن . والحياة التي هي عرض ، قائم به كما ذهب إليه بعض المتكلمين ، وأما النفس التي هي قائمة بنفسها ، ومدبرة للجسم ، فلا وجود لها ، ومعنى الموت انقطاع الحياة ، أى امتناع الخالق عن خلقها ، فتنعدم ، والبدن أيضاً ينعدم ، ومعنى المعاد إعادة الله تعالى للبدن ، الذي انعدم ، و رده إلى الوجود ، وإعادة الحياة التي انعدمت أو يقال : مادة البدن تبقى تراباً ، ومعنى العاد أن يجمع ويركب على شكل آدمی ، وتخلق فيه الحياة ابتداء .
فهذا قسم .
و إما أن يقال : النفس موجودة ، وتبقى بعد الموت ، ولكن يرد البدن الأول يجمع تلك الأجزاء بعينها
First Path: They said: The estimation of the return to bodies is of three divisions. Either it is said: Man is an expression of the body, and life which is an accident subsisting in him, as some theologians held, and the soul which is self-subsisting and governing the body has no existence. The meaning of death is the cessation of life, i.e., the Creator's abstaining from creating it, so it becomes non-existent, and the body also becomes non-existent. The meaning of resurrection is Allah's restoration of the body which became non-existent and returning it to existence, and restoring the life which became non-existent. Or it is said: The matter of the body remains as dust, and the meaning of return is that it is gathered and composed into a human shape, and life is created in it anew. This is a division. And either it is said: The soul exists and remains after death, but the first body returns, assembling those exact parts.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the first two of three positions (aqsam) that the philosophers hold concerning the return (al-'awd) of the body on the Day of Resurrection. Ghazali states that the first position reduces man to the body alone, with life as an accident that ceases at death, and denies an independently existing soul. It has two variants: one where the body and life are recreated ex nihilo, and another where the dust of the body remains and is recomposed into a human form with life created anew. Ghazali notes this is one division. The second position affirms the soul's existence and survival after death, but insists that the first body returns with its exact parts. This sets the stage for Ghazali's subsequent refutation.

Key Terms
العود
البدن
النفس
الحياة
المعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali endorses the first two positions on bodily resurrection.
Correction: Ghazali is laying out these views for refutation; he does not accept either position.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the first and second positions on resurrection?
A) The first denies the soul; the second affirms it.
B) The first affirms the soul; the second denies it.
C) Both deny the soul.
D) Both affirm the exact return of parts.

Key Takeaway
The two positions differ fundamentally in whether an independent soul exists and whether the body returns with its exact parts.

?
The philosophers debated whether your resurrection body would be the exact same particles or a new creation. Does it matter to you? Are you willing to face your Creator with the very same body you use to sin or obey?
?
If you truly believed that your exact body parts will be reassembled on the Day of Judgment, how would you treat your eyes, your tongue, and your limbs right now?

The Third Position and Refutation of the First
Ghazali refutes the first division of the third position on resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the idea that recreating a similar body and life constitutes a return of the same human being.

Core Idea
True return requires persistence of the same soul, not just a replica.

In this passage, Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' theory of bodily resurrection. He presents the third possible division of their view and then systematically refutes the first. The key concept is the meaning of 'return' (عود) and what it entails. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of Ghazali's argument. Notice the clear structure: statement of a division, then refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the third division
Identify what the third division claims: the soul returns to a body, identity by soul, not matter.
3
Follow the refutation of the first division
Ghazali argues that 'return' implies persistence of something through change. A complete recreation is just a like, not the same.
He uses analogies of returning to beneficence or to a town.
4
Examine the objection and response
The supporter says dust persists; life returns to it. Ghazali responds that this is not the human being because identity is by soul, not matter.
Note the point about body change through nutrition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا قسم :
و إما أن يقال : ترد النفس إلى بدن ، سواء كان من تلك الأجزاء بعينها ، أو من غيرها ، ويكون العائد ذلك الإنسان ، من حيث إن النفس تلك النفس ، فأما المادة فلا التفات إليها ، إذ الإنسان ليس إنساناً بها ، بل بالنفس .
وهذه الأقسام الثلاثة باطلة :
أما الأول فظاهر البطلان ، لأنه مهما انعدمت الحياة والبدن ، فاستئناف خلقهما ، إيجاد لمثل ما كان ، لا لعين ما كان ، بل العود المفهوم ، هو الذي يفرض فيه بقاء شيء ، وتجدد شيء ، كما يقال فلان عاد إلى الإنعام ، أي أن المنعم باق ، وترك الإنعام ، ثم عاد إليه ، أى عاد إلى ما هو الأول بالجنس ، ولكنه غيره بالعدد ، فيكون عوداً بالحقيقة إلى مثله ، لا إليه ، ويقال : فلان عاد إلى البلد ، أي بقى موجوداً خارج البلد ، وقد كان له كون" في البلد ، فعاد إلى مثل ذلك ، فإن لم يكن شيء باقياً ، وشيئان متعددان متماثلان ، يتخللهما زمان ، لم يتم اسم العود ، إلا أن يسلك مذهب المعتزلة ، فيقال : المعدوم شيء" ثابت ، والوجود حال يعرض له مرة ، وينقطع تارة ، ويعود أخرى ، فيتحقق معنى العود، باعتبار بقاء الذات ، ولكنه رفع للعدم المطلق ، الذي هو النفى المحض ، وهو إثبات للذات مستمرة الثبات ، إلى أن يعود إليها الوجود ، وهو محال
فإن احتال ناصر هذا القسم ، بأن قال : تراب البدن لا يمنى ، فيكون باقياً ، فتعاد إليه الحياة
•
فنقول : عند ذلك يستقيم أن يقال : عاد التراب حيا ، بعد أن انقطعت الحياة عنه مدة ، ولا يكون ذلك عوداً للإنسان ، ولا رجوع ذلك الإنسان بعينه ، لأن الإنسان إنسان لا بمادته ، والتراب الذي فيه ، إذ تتبدل عليه سائر الأجزاء، أو أكثرها بالغذاء ، وهو ذاك الأول بعينه ، فهو هو ، باعتبار روحه ونفسه
This is a division: And either it is said: The soul returns to a body, whether from those exact parts or from others, and the one returning is that human being, insofar as the soul is that same soul; as for matter, no attention is paid to it, for man is not man by it, but by the soul. And these three divisions are false: As for the first, its falsity is apparent, because whenever life and the body become non-existent, the recommencement of their creation is a bringing into existence of the like of what was, not the very same thing. Rather, the understood 'return' is that which presupposes the persistence of something and the renewal of something, as it is said: 'So-and-so returned to beneficence,' meaning that the benefactor remains, and he abandoned beneficence, then returned to it, i.e., he returned to what is the same in kind, but different in number, so it is a return in reality to its like, not to itself. And it is said: 'So-and-so returned to the town,' meaning he remained existing outside the town, and he had a being in the town, so he returned to the like of that. For if nothing persists, and two multiple similar things are separated by time, the name 'return' is not complete, unless one takes the path of the Mu'tazila, and says: The non-existent is a fixed thing, and existence is a state that occurs to it once, ceases sometimes, and returns another time, so the meaning of return is realized by virtue of the persistence of the essence. But this is the removal of absolute non-existence, which is pure negation, and it is an affirmation of an essence of continuous fixedness until existence returns to it. And this is impossible. If the supporter of this division resorts to saying: The dust of the body does not perish, so it persists, and life is returned to it, We say: Then it is proper to say: The dust became alive after life had been cut off from it for a while, but that is not a return of the human being, nor a return of that same human being, because man is man not by his matter, and the dust that is in him, since the other parts, or most of them, are changed by nutrition, and he is that first one himself? He is the same by virtue of his spirit and soul.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the third division: the soul returns to a body, irrespective of whether the parts are the same or different; identity is by the soul, not matter. Ghazali then declares all three divisions false. He focuses on the first division, which posits the recreation of body and life from nothing. He argues that 'return' requires persistence of something; recreating a like is not a return of the same. He gives analogies: returning to beneficence or to a town implies the person persists. Without persistence, only a replica is produced. He mentions the Mu'tazilite view that non-existence is a fixed thing to allow return, but dismisses it as impossible. Then he addresses an objection: if the dust persists, life can return to it. Ghazali responds that even if dust becomes alive, it is not the same human individual because identity is by the soul, not matter, and the body's matter constantly changes. Thus, the first division fails.

العود
المثل
المعتزلة
المعدوم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is denying bodily resurrection altogether.
Correction: Ghazali is not denying resurrection; he is refuting the philosophers' specific view that a recreated body and life constitute a return of the same individual. He holds a different concept of resurrection that involves the soul.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali consider the first division of the first position false?
A) Because it requires the soul.
B) Because recreating a like is not a return of the same thing.
C) Because the body cannot be recreated.
D) Because the Mu'tazilite view is correct.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that without persistence of the individual soul, what appears to be a return is merely the creation of a similar being, not the same person.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If your true identity is your soul, not your body, then what have you done today to nourish your soul? Think of one specific action that will strengthen your connection to Allah, and commit to doing it before the next day ends.
?
Ghazali argues that a mere replica of a person is not that person. How would you feel if you were told that your loved one who died will be 'replaced' by a perfect copy instead of being raised as themselves? Let that emotion drive you to investigate what Islam truly teaches about resurrection, and ask Allah for certainty.
?
The Mu'tazila tried to make the non-existent 'fixed' to save their theory. Is there something in your life that you are trying to force to be what it is not—like justifying a sin or evading a duty? Write down one area where you might be rationalizing, and resolve to submit to the truth revealed by Allah and His Messenger.

Refutation of the First Division on Bodily Return
Ghazali refutes the first false division on bodily return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali refutes the first division regarding the return of the soul to a body, and why personal identity depends on the soul rather than physical matter.

Core Idea
Return requires persistence; the soul, not the body, defines human identity.

In this passage, Ghazali begins his refutation of the first of three false divisions about bodily resurrection. He argues that the concept of 'return' logically requires some continuous element, and since the body perishes, the soul alone provides continuity. He also rejects the Mu'tazilite notion that non-existent things are fixed entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note how Ghazali uses examples like returning to generosity or to a city.
2
Identify the first division
Locate where Ghazali presents the first division: the soul returns to a body, same or different parts, and the person is the same because the soul is unchanged.
3
Understand the 'return' argument
Ghazali explains that return implies persistence of something. Re-creating an identical body after annihilation is creating a similar being, not the original.
He uses analogies: returning to generosity or to a city.
4
Consider the Mu'tazilite alternative
Ghazali mentions that only the Mu'tazilite view of 'non-existent as a fixed thing' could salvage the idea, but he deems it impossible.
This shows the importance of metaphysics in the discussion.
5
Examine the objection and response
A supporter argues that the dust of the body remains, so life can return. Ghazali counters that returning life to dust makes the dust alive, not the original human; identity is by the soul.
He uses the horse example to illustrate that matter does not determine identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا قسم: وإما أن يقال: ترد النفس إلى بدن، سواء كان من تلك الأجزاء بعينها، أو من غيرها، ويكون العائد ذلك الإنسان، من حيث إن النفس تلك النفس، فأما المادة فلا التفات إليها، إذ الإنسان ليس إنساناً بها، بل بالنفس. وهذه الأقسام الثلاثة باطلة: أما الأول فظاهر البطلان، لأنه مهما انعدمت الحياة والبدن، فاستئناف خلقهما، إيجاد لمثل ما كان، لا لعين ما كان، بل العود المفهوم، هو الذي يفرض فيه بقاء شيء، وتجدد شيء، كما يقال فلان عاد إلى الإنعام، أي أن المنعم باق، وترك الإنعام، ثم عاد إليه، أى عاد إلى ما هو الأول بالجنس، ولكنه غيره بالعدد، فيكون عوداً بالحقيقة إلى مثله، لا إليه، ويقال: فلان عاد إلى البلد، أي بقى موجوداً خارج البلد، وقد كان له كون في البلد، فعاد إلى مثل ذلك، فإن لم يكن شيء باقياً، وشيئان متعددان متماثلان، يتخللهما زمان، لم يتم اسم العود، إلا أن يسلك مذهب المعتزلة، فيقال: المعدوم شيء ثابت، والوجود حال يعرض له مرة، وينقطع تارة، ويعود أخرى، فيتحقق معنى العود، باعتبار بقاء الذات، ولكنه رفع للعدم المطلق، الذي هو النفي المحض، وهو إثبات للذات مستمرة الثبات، إلى أن يعود إليها الوجود، وهو محال. فإن احتال ناصر هذا القسم، بأن قال: تراب البدن لا يمنى، فيكون باقياً، فتعاد إليه الحياة. فنقول: عند ذلك يستقيم أن يقال: عاد التراب حيا، بعد أن انقطعت الحياة عنه مدة، ولا يكون ذلك عوداً للإنسان، ولا رجوع ذلك الإنسان بعينه، لأن الإنسان إنسان لا بمادته، والتراب الذي فيه، إذ تتبدل عليه سائر الأجزاء، أو أكثرها بالغذاء، وهو ذاك الأول بعينه، فهو هو، باعتبار روحه ونفسه، فإذا عدمت الحياة والروح، فما عدم لا يعقل عوده، وإنما يستأنف مثله، أو فرس، ومهما خلق الله تعالى حياة إنسانية في تراب، يحصل من بدن شجر، أو نبات، كان ذلك ابتداء خلق إنسان. قط فالمعدوم لا يعقل عوده، والعائد هو الموجود، أي عاد إلى حالة كانت له منه قبل، أى إلى مثل تلك الحالة، فالعائد هو التراب، إلى صفة الحياة. وليس الإنسان إنساناً ببدنه، إذ قد يصير بدن الفرس غذاء لإنسان، فتتخلق منه نطفة، يحصل منها إنسان، فلا يقال: الفرس انقلب إنساناً، بل الفرس فرس بصورته، لا بمادته، وقد انعدمت الصورة، وما بقى إلا المادة.
And this is a division: It might be said: The soul returns to a body, whether from those same parts or from others, and the one who returns is that human being, insofar as the soul is that soul. As for matter, no attention is paid to it, for a human being is not human by it, but by the soul. And these three divisions are false: As for the first, its falsity is obvious, because whenever life and the body cease to exist, re-creating them is the creation of something similar to what was, not the exact same thing. Rather, the understood 'return' is that in which the persistence of something and the renewal of something else is assumed, as it is said: So-and-so returned to generosity, meaning that the generous one persisted and left generosity, then returned to it — that is, he returned to what is the first in kind, but it is other than it in number, so it is a return in reality to its like, not to it. And it is said: So-and-so returned to the city, meaning he remained existing outside the city and he had a being in the city, so he returned to the like of that. If nothing remained, and two multiple similar things are separated by time, the name 'return' is not valid, unless one follows the school of the Mu'tazila, so it is said: The non-existent is a fixed thing, and existence is an accident that occurs to it at one time, ceases at another, and returns at another. So the meaning of return is realized by considering the persistence of the essence, but it is the removal of absolute non-existence, which is pure negation, and it is an affirmation of an essence continuously fixed until existence returns to it — and that is impossible. And if a supporter of this division tries to evade by saying: The dust of the body does not decay, so it remains, and life is returned to it. We say: In that case, it is valid to say: The dust returned alive after life had been cut off from it for a time, but that is not a return of the human being, nor the return of that specific human being, because a human being is human not by his matter, and the dust that is in him, while all or most of his parts change by nutrition, and he is that same first one, so he is him by virtue of his spirit and soul. So when life and spirit cease to exist, what has ceased cannot be understood to return; rather, its like is created anew, whether a horse or something else. And whenever God Most High creates a human life in dust that comes from the body of a tree or a plant, that is the beginning of the creation of a human being. Never does the non-existent return; rather, the one who returns is the existent, meaning it returned to a state it had before, i.e., to the like of that state; so the one returning is the dust, to the attribute of life. And a human being is not human by his body, for the body of a horse may become food for a human being, and from it a sperm is formed, and from that a human being is produced; so it is not said that the horse turned into a human being; rather, the horse is a horse by its form, not by its matter, and the form has ceased, and only matter remains.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first of three false divisions concerning the return of the soul to a body in the afterlife. Ghazali begins by presenting the division: some say the soul returns to a body, whether composed of the same particles or different ones, and the returning entity is the same human because the soul is identical; matter is irrelevant. He then declares all three divisions false. Focusing on the first, he argues that if life and the body are completely annihilated, re-creating them produces a similar being, not the identical one. The literal meaning of 'return' requires some persistence through change, as in returning to a place or action: the subject persists while the object is replaced. Without any persisting element, the word 'return' cannot apply unless one adopts the Mu'tazilite view that non-existent things (ma'dum) are fixed entities and existence is a transient attribute that comes and goes. Ghazali rejects this as impossible. Next, he addresses an objection: the supporter of the first division argues that the dust of the body does not decay, so it remains and life is returned to it. Ghazali responds that even if dust remains, giving it life makes the dust alive, not the original human being. A human is human by his soul, not by his matter. The dust is merely matter; the soul and life have ceased. What has ceased cannot return; only its like can be created. He gives the example of a horse's body becoming food for a human: the horse's form is lost, and only matter remains; a new human is created from that matter, but it is not the horse returning as a human. Thus, the first division fails because it confuses matter with identity: identity is tied to the soul, not the body, and the soul does not return to the same body after annihilation.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs, the soul)
البدن (al-badan, the body)
العود (al-ʿawd, return)
المعتزلة (al-Muʿtazila)
المعدوم (al-maʿdūm, the non-existent)
الوجود (al-wujūd, existence)
الحياة (al-ḥayāh, life)
الروح (al-rūḥ, spirit)
المادة (al-māddah, matter)
الصورة (al-ṣūrah, form)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The resurrection body must be composed of the identical physical particles as the earthly body.
Correction: Ghazali argues that personal identity depends on the soul, not the matter. The body is re-created similarly, but the same soul makes the person identical.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali in this passage, why is the first division regarding the return of the soul to a body false?
A) Because the soul cannot survive death.
B) Because the re-creation of life and body produces a similar being, not the identical one, unless one adopts the Muʿtazilite view of non-existent entities.
C) Because the dust of the body decays completely and cannot be used.
D) Because the body is irrelevant; only the soul returns.

Key Takeaway
Personal identity is determined by the soul, not by physical matter; resurrection involves the return of the soul to a similar, not identical, body.

?

The Impossibility of One Body for Two Souls
Ghazali states the first impossibility and supports it with the soil cycle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that a single material cannot be restored to two different bodies simultaneously.

Core Idea
One piece of matter cannot serve as the body for two souls.

Ghazali begins his critique of the philosophical view of bodily resurrection by focusing on the problem of material identity. If the same matter has been part of multiple bodies over time, how can it be uniquely assigned to one? This lesson explores the first impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the core problem: one material becoming part of two bodies.
Pay attention to the cycle of soil, plants, and animals.
2
Identify the key claim
Ghazali states that one material was from the eaten and became the eater's body, so it cannot be returned to two souls.
3
Analyze the soil cycle evidence
Ghazali expands the argument to the natural cycle: soil contains remains of many people, becomes plants, then flesh, then human bodies again. No material is unique to one person.
This strengthens the first impossibility.
4
Connect to the lesson objective
Understand why this refutes the philosophical view that resurrection requires exact material reconstruction.
It is not a denial of resurrection itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مادة واحدة كانت بدناً للمأكول ، وصارت بالغذاء بدناً للأكل ولا يمكن رد نفسين إلى بدن واحد .
والثانى: أنه يجب أن يعاد جزء واحد ، كبداً وقلباً ، ويداً ، ورجلا ، فإنه ثبت بالصناعة الطبية ، أن الأجزاء العضوية ، يتغذى بعضها ، بفضلة غذاء البعض ، فيتغذى الكبد بأجزاء القلب ، وكذلك سائر الأعضاء ، فنفرض أجزاء معينة ، قد كانت مادة الجملة من الأعضاء ، فإلى أي عضو تعاد ؟!!
بل لا يحتاج في تقرير الاستحالة الأولى ! إلى أكل الناس الناس فإنك إذا تأملت ظاهر التربة المعمورة ، علمت بعد طول الزمان ، أن ترابها جثث الموتى ، قد تتربت وزرع فيها وغرس ، وصارت حبا وفاكهة ، وتناولتها الدواب ، فصارت لحماً ، وتناولناها فصارت أبداناً لنا ، فما من مادة يشار إليها ، إلا وقد كانت بدناً لأناس كثيرين ، فاستحالت وصارت تراباً ، ثم نباتاً ثم لحماً ، ثم حيواناً .
بل يلزم منه محال ثالث ، وهو أن النفوس المفارقة للأبدان ، غير متناهية ، والأبدان أجسام متناهية ، فلا تفى المواد ، التي كانت مواد الإنسان ، بأنفس الناس كلهم ، بل تضيق عنهم .
One single material was the body of the eaten [person], and through nutrition it became the body of the eater, and it is impossible to return two souls to one body. Second: it would be necessary that one part be restored, such as the liver, the heart, the hand, and the leg; for it is established by medical science that the organic parts are nourished by the surplus of the nourishment of others: the liver is nourished by parts of the heart, and similarly the rest of the organs. So suppose specific parts that were the material of the whole set of organs—to which organ would they be returned?! Moreover, to establish the first impossibility one does not even need [the case of] people eating people; for if you contemplate the apparent soil of the inhabited land, you know after a long time that its soil is the corpses of the dead, which have become dust, and were sown with plants and planted, and became grain and fruit, and animals consumed them, and they became flesh, and we consumed them and they became our bodies. So there is no material pointed to except that it was the body of many people, then it changed and became dust, then plants, then flesh, then animals. Rather, a third impossibility follows from it: namely, that the souls separated from bodies are infinite, while bodies are finite substances, so the materials that were the materials of humans cannot suffice for all human souls—they fall short of them.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments demonstrating the impossibility of re-creating the exact same individual body in resurrection. The author first states the core problem: a single piece of matter may have been part of multiple bodies (the body of the eaten and the body of the eater), so it cannot be restored to two souls simultaneously. The second argument asks which specific organ should be restored when organs are nourished by residues from other organs—the same material could belong to different parts. The author then strengthens the first argument by considering the natural cycle: soil contains remnants of countless deceased people, which become plants, animal flesh, and eventually human bodies again; hence no material can be uniquely assigned to one body. Finally, a third impossibility is derived: human souls are infinite (in number throughout history), while bodies and materials are finite, so there are not enough materials to house all souls. The arguments collectively refute the philosophers' claim that resurrection requires returning to the exact same material body.

Key Terms
مادة
بدن
نفس
إعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies bodily resurrection altogether.
Correction: Ghazali only rejects the philosophical requirement of exact material identity, not resurrection itself.

Key Takeaway
No material can be uniquely assigned to one person because it cycles through many bodies.

The Impossibility of Assigning a Shared Limb to a Specific Organ
Ghazali presents the second impossibility using medical organ feeding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that it is impossible to assign shared organic matter to a specific organ in resurrection.

Core Idea
When organs feed on each other's residues, shared matter cannot be returned to one part only.

Ghazali presents a second impossibility based on medical observation: organs are nourished by the surplus from other organs. This means a piece of matter could have been part of multiple organs. To which organ would it be restored? This lesson examines that problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second impossibility
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the medical premise: organs feed on each other's nutrients.
The liver feeds on parts of the heart, etc.
2
Understand the organ feeding cycle
The surplus nourishment of one organ becomes the material for another. Thus, a given particle may have belonged to several organs.
3
Pose the critical question
Ghazali asks: if we suppose specific parts that were the material of all organs, to which organ would they be returned?
There is no unique assignment.
4
Connect to the lesson objective
Realize that this impossibility reinforces the argument against exact material reconstruction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مادة واحدة كانت بدناً للمأكول ، وصارت بالغذاء بدناً للأكل ولا يمكن رد نفسين إلى بدن واحد .
والثانى: أنه يجب أن يعاد جزء واحد ، كبداً وقلباً ، ويداً ، ورجلا ، فإنه ثبت بالصناعة الطبية ، أن الأجزاء العضوية ، يتغذى بعضها ، بفضلة غذاء البعض ، فيتغذى الكبد بأجزاء القلب ، وكذلك سائر الأعضاء ، فنفرض أجزاء معينة ، قد كانت مادة الجملة من الأعضاء ، فإلى أي عضو تعاد ؟!!
بل لا يحتاج في تقرير الاستحالة الأولى ! إلى أكل الناس الناس فإنك إذا تأملت ظاهر التربة المعمورة ، علمت بعد طول الزمان ، أن ترابها جثث الموتى ، قد تتربت وزرع فيها وغرس ، وصارت حبا وفاكهة ، وتناولتها الدواب ، فصارت لحماً ، وتناولناها فصارت أبداناً لنا ، فما من مادة يشار إليها ، إلا وقد كانت بدناً لأناس كثيرين ، فاستحالت وصارت تراباً ، ثم نباتاً ثم لحماً ، ثم حيواناً .
بل يلزم منه محال ثالث ، وهو أن النفوس المفارقة للأبدان ، غير متناهية ، والأبدان أجسام متناهية ، فلا تفى المواد ، التي كانت مواد الإنسان ، بأنفس الناس كلهم ، بل تضيق عنهم .
One single material was the body of the eaten [person], and through nutrition it became the body of the eater, and it is impossible to return two souls to one body. Second: it would be necessary that one part be restored, such as the liver, the heart, the hand, and the leg; for it is established by medical science that the organic parts are nourished by the surplus of the nourishment of others: the liver is nourished by parts of the heart, and similarly the rest of the organs. So suppose specific parts that were the material of the whole set of organs—to which organ would they be returned?! Moreover, to establish the first impossibility one does not even need [the case of] people eating people; for if you contemplate the apparent soil of the inhabited land, you know after a long time that its soil is the corpses of the dead, which have become dust, and were sown with plants and planted, and became grain and fruit, and animals consumed them, and they became flesh, and we consumed them and they became our bodies. So there is no material pointed to except that it was the body of many people, then it changed and became dust, then plants, then flesh, then animals. Rather, a third impossibility follows from it: namely, that the souls separated from bodies are infinite, while bodies are finite substances, so the materials that were the materials of humans cannot suffice for all human souls—they fall short of them.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments demonstrating the impossibility of re-creating the exact same individual body in resurrection. The author first states the core problem: a single piece of matter may have been part of multiple bodies (the body of the eaten and the body of the eater), so it cannot be restored to two souls simultaneously. The second argument asks which specific organ should be restored when organs are nourished by residues from other organs—the same material could belong to different parts. The author then strengthens the first argument by considering the natural cycle: soil contains remnants of countless deceased people, which become plants, animal flesh, and eventually human bodies again; hence no material can be uniquely assigned to one body. Finally, a third impossibility is derived: human souls are infinite (in number throughout history), while bodies and materials are finite, so there are not enough materials to house all souls. The arguments collectively refute the philosophers' claim that resurrection requires returning to the exact same material body.

جزء
عضو
غذاء
صناعة طبية

D) The soul does not need a body

Key Takeaway
Shared organic matter cannot be uniquely assigned to one organ because of inter-organ feeding.

The Impossibility of Finite Bodies Housing Infinite Souls
Ghazali infers a third impossibility from the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that finite material cannot accommodate the infinite multitude of human souls.

Core Idea
Infinite souls cannot fit into finite bodies and materials.

Ghazali derives a third impossibility from the first: human souls are infinite in number, but bodies and materials are finite. Therefore, the available matter cannot suffice for all souls. This lesson explores this mathematical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the third impossibility
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the premise that souls are infinite and bodies finite.
2
Understand the logical inference
Ghazali says 'it follows from it'—meaning from the first argument about material cycling. Because matter cycles through many bodies, the number of souls throughout history is vast, but the amount of matter is limited.
3
Contrast souls and bodies
Souls are non-physical and not subject to spatial limits, but bodies are physical and require specific matter. The total material available cannot be enough to reconstitute every exact body.
4
Connect to the lesson objective
Understand that this is a quantitative argument against exact material reconstruction.

The Impossibility of the Soul's Return to Any Body
Ghazali refutes the view that the soul can return to any human body, equating it to reincarnation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the philosophers' claim that the soul can return to any human body impossible, and how he identifies this view with reincarnation.

Core Idea
Souls are infinite; bodies are finite – the soul cannot simply return to any body.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a specific philosophical position about the afterlife: the idea that after death, the soul can return to any human body, regardless of its original material. This sounds like a version of reincarnation, and Ghazali argues that it is impossible on two grounds. As you read, pay attention to how he uses the finitude of matter and the specificity of bodily composition to dismantle this view, ultimately equating it with the doctrine of reincarnation that he has already refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of Ghazali's argument. Note the two reasons he gives.
The passage is a single, continuous argument.
2
Analyze the first reason: finite materials, infinite souls
Ghazali says the materials for bodies are finite (limited to the sublunar sphere) but the souls that have left bodies are infinite. Therefore, there are not enough materials for all souls. Reflect on why this is a logical problem.
This shows the quantitative impossibility.
3
Analyze the second reason: dust cannot receive the soul's governance
Even if materials were infinite, dust itself is not suitable for the soul. The body must have a specific mixture similar to the original human composition (flesh, bone, humors). A body of wood or iron cannot become human, and any prepared body already has its own soul.
This shows the qualitative impossibility.
4
Understand the conclusion: it is reincarnation
Ghazali then states that this view is exactly the doctrine of reincarnation, which he has already refuted. The argument against reincarnation applies here: a soul cannot take on a new body after leaving its original one.
This connects the refutation to a broader theological context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث، وهو رد النفس إلى بدن إنسانى من أى مادة كانت، وأى تراب اتفق، فهو محال من وجهين: أحدهما: أن المواد القابلة للكون والفساد، محصورة في مقعر فلك القمر، لا يمكن عليها مزيد، وهى متناهية، والأنفس المفارقة للأبدان، غير متناهية، فلا تفي بها. والثانى: أن التراب لا يقبل تدبير النفس، ما بقى تراباً، بل لا بد أن تمتزج العناصر امتزاجاً، يضاهى امتزاج النطفة، بل الخشب والحديد، لا يقبل هذا التدبير، ولا يمكن إعادة الإنسان، وبدنه من خشب أو حديد، بل لا يكون إنساناً إلا إذا انقسمت أعضاء بدنه إلى اللحم، والعظم، والأخلاط، ومهما استعد البدن والمزاج، لقبول نفس، استحق من المبادئ الواهبة للنفوس، حدوث نفس، فيتوارد على البدن الواحد نفسان. و بهذا بطل مذهب التناسخ، وهذا المذهب هو عين التناسخ، فإنه رجع إلى اشتغال النفس، بعد خلاصها من البدن، بتدبير بدن آخر، غير البدن الأول، فالمسلك الذي يدل على بطلان التناسخ، يدل على بطلان هذا المذهبه
As for the third division, which is the return of the soul to a human body from whatever material and whatever dust it happens to be, it is impossible for two reasons. One: the materials subject to generation and corruption are confined within the concave of the sphere of the moon—they cannot be increased, and they are finite, while the souls separated from bodies are infinite, so they do not suffice for them. The second: dust does not accept the governance of the soul as long as it remains dust; rather, the elements must mix with a mixture resembling the mixture of the sperm-nut; moreover, wood and iron do not accept this governance, and it is not possible to restore a human being whose body is of wood or iron. Rather, a human is only [a human] when the parts of his body are divided into flesh, bone, and humors. And whenever the body and the temperament become prepared to accept a soul, it becomes deserving, from the principles that bestow souls, of the occurrence of a soul [in it]; thus two souls would enter the same body. And with this, the doctrine of reincarnation (tanāsukh) is invalidated, and this [third division of the philosophers] is exactly reincarnation; for it reduces to the soul, after its liberation from the body, engaging in the governance of another body other than the first body. So the argument that indicates the invalidity of reincarnation indicates the invalidity of this doctrine.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage refutes the third philosophical position on resurrection—the idea that the soul can return to any human body regardless of its material origin. Ghazali gives two reasons. First: the materials capable of generating bodies are finite (limited to the sublunar sphere), while souls are infinite in number, so there are not enough materials for all souls. Second: mere dust is not suitable for the soul's governance; the elements must be mixed in a way analogous to the mixture of the male semen, forming flesh, bone, and humors. Even if a body were prepared, it would already have its own soul, so two souls would occupy one body—an impossibility. The author then identifies this view as exactly the doctrine of reincarnation (tanāsukh) that was previously refuted. The argument against reincarnation applies equally here: a soul cannot take on a new body after leaving its original one.

Key Terms
رد النفس (return of the soul)
تناسخ (reincarnation)
مبادئ واهبة للنفوس (principles bestowing souls)
امتزاج (mixture)
مقعر فلك القمر (concave of the sphere of the moon)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some philosophers believed that the soul could return to any human body after death, regardless of the body's material origin, as a form of resurrection.
Correction: Ghazali argues this is impossible because: (1) materials are finite but souls are infinite, so there are not enough bodies; (2) the soul cannot govern any material—it requires a specific biological composition (flesh, bone, humors). Additionally, any prepared body already has its own soul, leading to the absurdity of two souls in one body. This view is exactly reincarnation, which is already refuted.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The soul cannot return to any body because bodies are finite and require specific composition, and this view is identical to the invalid doctrine of reincarnation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
