Ghazali on the Philosophers' Denial of Bodily Resurrection
Ghazali refutes philosophers' denial of bodily resurrection and lists their specific rejections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' denial of bodily resurrection by appealing to Muslim consensus, revelation, and the possibility of combining spiritual and physical rewards.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection is a non-negotiable Islamic belief that reason alone cannot undermine.

In this passage, Ghazali takes on the philosophers who claim that descriptions of the afterlife are merely parables for common people. He firmly declares this view contrary to the consensus of all Muslims. While affirming that spiritual delights exist, he insists that bodily resurrection and physical pleasures and pains are also real. This lesson examines his arguments and his challenge to the philosophers' exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the rhetorical structure: Ghazali states the philosophers' position, then declares it invalid, then presents his own view.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وهذا مخالف لاعتقاد المسلمين كافة'
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Identify what the philosophers say about descriptions of paradise, hell, and bodily rewards. Why do they think these are only parables?
They claim these are 'أمثلة ضربت لعوام الخلق'.
3
Understand Ghazali's counter-arguments
Ghazali lists three specific denials of the philosophers: denial of bodily resurrection, denial of physical pleasures/pains, and denial of the existence of paradise and hell as described in the Quran. Note that he also points out that Muslims know these through revelation, not reason alone.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Ghazali asks: why can't both spiritual and physical happiness coexist? Consider how this question challenges the philosophers' view that only spiritual rewards matter.
This shows Ghazali's method of using logic within the framework of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۸ - مسألة فى إبطال إنكارهم لبعث الأجساد ، ورد الأرواح إلى الأبدان، ووجود النار الجسمانية ، ووجود الجنة والحور العين ، وسائر ما وعد به الناس ؛ وقولهم إن كل ذلك أمثلة ضربت لعوام الخلق لتفهيم ثواب وعقاب روحانيين ، هما أعلى رتبة من الجسمانيين . قال الغزالى : وهذا مخالف لاعتقاد المسلمين كافة ثم قال : فلنقدم تفهيم مذهبهم أولا ، ثم نعترض على ما يخالف الإسلام منه . . . فانظر مذهبهم قال الغزالي : إننا لا ننكر أن في الآخرة أنواعاً من اللذات أعظم من المحسوسات ، ولا ننكر بقاء النفس عند مفارقة البدن ، ولكننا عرفنا ذلك بالشرع ؛ إذ قد ورد بالمعاد ولا يفهم المعاد إلا ببقاء النفس ، وإنما أنكرنا عليهم دعواهم معرفة ذلك بمجرد العقل . قال الغزالي : والمخالف للشرع من مذهبهم : إنكارهم حشر الأجساد . وإنكار اللذات الجسمانية في الجنة وإنكار الآلام الجسمانية في النار . وإنكار وجود الجنة ، والنار ، كما وصف القرآن نص من [ النجاة ] هامش . قال الغزالي : فما المانع من تحقق الجمع بين السعادتين : الروحانية والجسمانية . وكذا الشقاوة ؟
22nd Section: On invalidating their denial of the resurrection of bodies, the return of souls to bodies, the existence of bodily fire, the existence of paradise, houris, and all that is promised to people; and their statement that all of that is parables struck for the common people to make them understand spiritual reward and punishment that are higher in rank than physical ones. Al-Ghazali said: This contradicts the belief of all Muslims. Then he said: Let us first present an understanding of their doctrine, then we will object to what contradicts Islam from it. So consider their doctrine. Al-Ghazali said: We do not deny that in the afterlife there are kinds of delights greater than the sensible ones, and we do not deny the survival of the soul after separation from the body. But we know that by revelation, since the resurrection has been reported, and resurrection is only understood through the survival of the soul. We only object to their claim to know that by mere reason. Al-Ghazali said: What contradicts the revealed law from their doctrine is: their denial of the gathering of bodies, their denial of physical pleasures in paradise, their denial of physical pains in hell, and their denial of the existence of paradise and hell as described in the Qur'an (text from al-Najat marginal note). Al-Ghazali said: What prevents the realization of the combination of the two happinesses: spiritual and physical? And likewise the misery?
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a section heading and Ghazali's introduction to the issue of bodily resurrection. He first states the philosophers' claim that descriptions of the afterlife are merely parables for common people to understand spiritual rewards that are superior. Ghazali declares this view contrary to the consensus of all Muslims. He then clarifies that Muslims do not deny spiritual delights or the soul's immortality; these are also affirmed by revelation. However, he rejects the philosophers' claim to know these matters by reason alone. He then lists the specific aspects of their doctrine that violate the revealed law: denial of bodily resurrection, physical pleasures and pains in paradise and hell, and the existence of paradise and hell as described in the Qur'an. Finally, he poses a rhetorical question: why can't both spiritual and physical happiness (and misery) coexist? This challenges the philosophers' exclusivity.

Correction: Ghazali explicitly affirms spiritual delights and the soul's survival, but insists that bodily resurrection and physical rewards are also real, as per revelation.

Ghazali's Refutation of the Parable Interpretation of Paradise
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that descriptions of paradise are parables, giving two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two arguments against the philosophers' claim that descriptions of paradise and hell are mere parables for the common people.

Core Idea
Paradise descriptions are too detailed to be parables and not rationally impossible to take literally.

The philosophers argued that scriptural descriptions of paradise and hell are parables for the masses, similar to anthropomorphic verses. Ghazali disagrees, offering two key distinctions. This lesson examines his refutation, showing why the afterlife descriptions must be taken literally while anthropomorphic verses require interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim and Ghazali's rebuttal
Start by reading the passage carefully. Notice the philosophers' claim that paradise descriptions are parables like tashbih verses, and Ghazali's immediate response that equating them is arbitrary.
The philosophers use the analogy of 'parables struck for the common people'.
2
Analyze the first reason: difference in interpretability
Ghazali's first argument: Tashbih verses are open to metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) because they are brief and follow Arab poetic convention. But the detailed descriptions of paradise and hell are too elaborate and specific to be interpreted away; dismissing them as parables would make prophecy deceptive.
The key is the level of detail: 'بلغ مبلغاً لا يحتمل التأويل' (reached a degree that cannot be interpreted).
3
Analyze the second reason: role of rational impossibility
Ghazali's second argument: Rational proofs show that God cannot have physical attributes like place or direction, so those verses must be interpreted. But the afterlife descriptions involve nothing impossible for God's power; therefore, they must be taken literally, i.e., on their apparent meaning (zahir) or even their inner explicit meaning (fahwa).
This argument hinges on the principle that scripture is interpreted only when necessary due to rational impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة ما ورد في الشرع بخصوص الجنة والنار ، أمثال ضربت لعوام الخلق على قدر أفهام الخلق ، كما أن الوارد من آيات التشبيه وأخباره أمثال ضربت على قدر أفهام الخلق ، والصفات الإلهية مقدسة عما يتخيله عوام الناس . قال الغزالى : إن التسوية بينهما تحكم ، بل هما يفترقان من وجهين : أحدهما : أن الألفاظ الواردة فى التشبيه ، تحتمل التأويل على عادة العرب في الاستعارة ، وما ورد فى وصف الجنة والنار ، وتفصيل تلك الأحوال ، بلغ مبلغاً لا يحتمل التأويل : فلا يبقى إلا حمل الكلام على التلبيس بتخييل نقيض الحق لمصلحة الخلق وذلك ما يتقدس عنه منصب النبوة . الثاني : أن أدلة العقول دلت على استحالة المكان والجهة والصورة ، ويد الجارحة ، وعين الجارحة ، فوجب التأويل بأدلة العقول وما وعد به من أمور الآخرة ، ليس محالا في قدرة الله تعالى ، فيجب إجراؤه على ظاهر الكلام ، بل على فحواه الذي هو صريح فيه
The philosophers said: What has come in the revealed law concerning paradise and hell are parables struck for the common people according to the capacity of their understanding, just as what has come in the verses of anthropomorphism (tashbih) and its reports are parables struck for the common people according to the capacity of their understanding, and the divine attributes are transcendent above what the common people imagine. Al-Ghazali said: Equating the two is arbitrary; rather, they differ in two aspects: First: The expressions used in anthropomorphism are susceptible to interpretation (ta'wil) according to the Arab custom of metaphor, whereas what has come in the description of paradise and hell and the detailing of those states has reached a degree that cannot be interpreted; so nothing remains but to consider the speech as obfuscation portraying the opposite of truth for the benefit of the people, and that is too exalted for the station of prophethood. Second: The proofs of reason have indicated the impossibility of place, direction, form, the hand as a limb, and the eye as a limb, so interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary based on rational proofs. But what is promised of matters of the afterlife is not impossible in the power of God Almighty, so it must be carried upon the apparent meaning of the speech, nay, upon its inner meaning that is explicit therein.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' claim that the scriptural descriptions of paradise and hell are only parables for common people to grasp spiritual realities, analogous to how verses of anthropomorphism (like God's hand, face) are interpreted metaphorically. Ghazali refutes this by giving two distinctions: First, the anthropomorphic expressions are open to metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) because they are limited, but the detailed descriptions of the afterlife are too elaborate and specific to be merely metaphorical—to dismiss them as parables would make prophetic revelation deceptive and unworthy of prophethood. Second, rational proofs show that God cannot have physical attributes like place or direction, so interpretation is required for those verses. But the afterlife descriptions do not entail any impossibility in God's power; they are within His ability. Therefore, they must be taken literally as true, even if their full reality is beyond our comprehension.

Key Terms
أمثال
تشبيه
تأويل
ظاهر
فحوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali rejects all metaphorical interpretation of scripture.
Correction: Ghazali only rejects metaphorical interpretation where the text is too detailed to be plausibly parabolic and where no rational impossibility forces interpretation. He affirms interpretation for tashbih verses due to rational necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the first reason the descriptions of paradise differ from tashbih verses?
A) Tashbih verses are explicit, but paradise descriptions require interpretation.
B) Tashbih verses can be interpreted metaphorically, but paradise descriptions are too detailed to interpret.
C) Paradise descriptions are impossible in God's power, while tashbih verses are not.
D) Tashbih verses are for common people, but paradise descriptions are for the elite.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali distinguishes between anthropomorphic verses, which require interpretation due to rational impossibility, and afterlife descriptions, which are too detailed to be mere parables and are not impossible for God, so they must be taken literally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Ghazali argued that to dismiss detailed descriptions of paradise as mere parables undermines the dignity of prophecy. When you read such descriptions in the Quran, do you feel the weight of their truth, or do you mentally gloss over them? What does your response reveal about your trust in the Prophet's message?
?
If you were to internalize that this life is truly temporary and the afterlife is literally waiting, what would you do differently tomorrow? What comfort or fear would you embrace that you currently avoid?

Ghazali's First Distinction: Parables vs. Detailed Descriptions
Philosophers analogize resurrection descriptions to anthropomorphic verses; Ghazali replies with first distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first distinction between anthropomorphic verses and eschatological descriptions, and why equating them is arbitrary.

Core Idea
Detailed eschatological descriptions cannot be mere parables—they are too explicit to interpret away.

The philosophers argued that descriptions of Paradise and Hell in revelation are parables for the common people, similar to anthropomorphic verses about God. Ghazali counters that this equation is arbitrary and presents his first key distinction: anthropomorphic expressions can be interpreted metaphorically, but the detailed accounts of the afterlife leave no room for allegorical reinterpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the opening line where the philosophers equate eschatological descriptions with anthropomorphic verses as parables for the common people.
Notice they appeal to the idea that divine attributes are beyond human imagination.
2
Identify Ghazali's response
Read Ghazali's refutation: he calls the equation arbitrary and introduces two distinctions. Focus on the first distinction as presented here.
The first distinction concerns the nature of the texts themselves.
3
Compare the two textual types
Notice how Ghazali contrasts anthropomorphic verses (which can be interpreted metaphorically in Arab usage) with eschatological descriptions (which are too detailed to admit interpretation).
The level of detail is key: the more detail, the less room for figurative reading.
4
Reflect on the implication
If eschatological descriptions were mere parables, it would mean revelation is deliberately misleading—something unbecoming of prophethood.
This connects the first distinction to the sanctity of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة ما ورد في الشرع بخصوص الجنة والنار، أمثال ضربت لعوام الخلق على قدر أفهام الخلق، كما أن الوارد من آيات التشبيه وأخباره أمثال ضربت على قدر أفهام الخلق، والصفات الإلهية مقدسة عما يتخيله عوام الناس. قال الغزالى : إن التسوية بينهما تحكم، بل هما يفترقان من وجهين: أحدهما: أن الألفاظ الواردة فى التشبيه، تحتمل التأويل على عادة العرب في الاستعارة، وما ورد فى وصف الجنة والنار، وتفصيل تلك الأحوال، بلغ مبلغاً لا يحتمل التأويل: فلا يبقى إلا حمل الكلام على التلبيس بتخييل نقيض الحق لمصلحة الخلق وذلك ما يتقدس عنه منصب النبوة
The philosophers said: What has come in the Shari'a regarding Paradise and Hell are parables struck for the common people according to their understanding, just as the anthropomorphic verses and reports are parables struck according to the understanding of the common people, and the divine attributes are sanctified above what the common people imagine. Al-Ghazali said: Equating them is arbitrary; rather they differ in two respects. One of them: that the expressions used in anthropomorphism admit of interpretation according to the Arab custom of metaphor, whereas what has come in the description of Paradise and Hell, and the detailing of those states, has reached a degree that does not admit interpretation. Thus nothing remains but to take the speech as obfuscation by imagining the opposite of truth for the benefit of the people, and that is something from which the station of prophethood is sanctified.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' claim that the descriptions of Paradise and Hell in revelation are merely parables for the common people, just like anthropomorphic verses about God (e.g., hand, face) are interpreted figuratively. Ghazali responds that equating the two is arbitrary (tahakkum). He gives two distinctions. The first: anthropomorphic expressions can be interpreted metaphorically in line with Arab usage, but the detailed descriptions of Paradise and Hell are too explicit and detailed to admit interpretation. To claim they are mere parables would imply that revelation is deliberately misleading (talbis), which is unbecoming of prophethood.

Key Terms
أمثال
آيات التشبيه
التأويل
التلبيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali rejects all figurative interpretation of scripture.
Correction: Ghazali accepts metaphorical interpretation for anthropomorphic verses but not for detailed eschatological descriptions.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's first distinction, what is the difference between anthropomorphic verses and descriptions of Paradise and Hell?
A) Both admit metaphorical interpretation
B) Anthropomorphic verses admit interpretation; Paradise/Hell descriptions do not because they are too detailed
C) Anthropomorphic verses are literal; Paradise/Hell descriptions are metaphorical
D) Both are literal and cannot be interpreted

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that equating detailed eschatological descriptions with anthropomorphic verses is arbitrary because the former are too explicit to be interpreted as mere parables.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the descriptions of Paradise and Hell in the Quran, have you ever mentally reduced them to 'just metaphors' to avoid discomfort? What does Ghazali's argument reveal about the seriousness of that tendency, and what will you do this week to take those descriptions more literally?
?
If you believe the Prophet ﷺ was entrusted with the clearest and truest speech, how does this lesson challenge you to reconsider any doubt you've harbored about the reality of the afterlife? What specific description will you ponder today to make it more real for you?
?
The philosophers claimed the detailed descriptions were for the 'common people'—do you ever look down on 'simple' believers who take these descriptions literally? How does Ghazali's defense of the literal meaning call you to humble yourself and learn from their faith?

Unseen Matters
intermediate
5 min
Ghazali's Refutation of the Instant Resurrection Objection
Ghazali answers the philosophers' objection that instantaneous resurrection is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's response to the philosophical objection that instantaneous resurrection is impossible.

Core Idea
God's power contains wonders beyond human experience; nature is not a limit.

The philosophers argue that a decomposed body cannot be resurrected instantly because it must go through natural stages. Ghazali counters by emphasizing that God's power is not bound by observed natural processes. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the philosophers' argument and Ghazali's response.
Notice the structure: objection then answer.
2
Identify the philosophers' assumption
Identify the key assumption: that resurrection must follow natural stages (aṭwār).
This assumption limits divine power to observed patterns.
3
Analyze Ghazali's counterargument
Understand that Ghazali appeals to God's 'treasury of power' containing wonders beyond human experience.
Ghazali uses this to argue that what is unseen is not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني: أن قالوا: إن جسم الميت المتحلل لا يمكن أن يعود حيا إلا بعد أن يمر بأطوار عدة، فخضوعه لنظرية [كن] فيكون في الحال غير معقول. قال الغزالي: إن في خزانة المقدورات عجائب وغرائب، لم يطلع عليها، ينكرها من يظن أن لا وجود إلا لما شاهده.
The second path: That they said: The decomposed dead body cannot return to life except after it passes through several stages; so its being subject to the theory of 'Be!' (kun) and coming into existence instantaneously is unintelligible. Al-Ghazali said: Indeed in the treasury of the objects of power are wonders and marvels that one has not been informed of; one denies them who thinks that there is no existence except what he has witnessed.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The philosophers present a second argument: a dead, decomposed body cannot be resurrected directly; it must go through natural stages (like the gradual development of an embryo). Therefore, the idea of instantaneous resurrection through God's command ('Be!') is irrational. Ghazali replies by appealing to the infinite power of God. He says that God's treasury of power contains wonders that people have not witnessed. Only those who think that existence is limited to what they have personally observed would deny such possibilities. The lesson highlights Ghazali's reliance on divine omnipotence against naturalistic objections.

Key Terms
جسم الميت
أطوار
خزانة المقدورات
كن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali rejects all natural causality.
Correction: He only asserts that God can bypass natural processes if He wills; he does not deny them entirely.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say is in the 'treasury of the objects of power'?
A) Only what is observed
B) Wonders and marvels unseen by most
C) The laws of nature
D) The philosophers' arguments

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends the possibility of instantaneous resurrection by appealing to God's unlimited power, which is not confined to observed natural stages.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's power can accomplish any miracle, even if it defies everything you know about how the world works? When you think of resurrection, do you imagine it as a process that must follow natural laws, or do you accept that God can bring it about instantly?
?
Has your limited experience ever caused you to doubt a promise of God? Think of one specific area of your life where you struggle to trust that God can act beyond what you see or understand. What would it take for you to submit that area to His absolute power?
?
When you hear Ghazali's words that God's treasury contains wonders you have not witnessed, does your heart feel hope or skepticism? Will you commit to repenting of any secret doubt that God might be limited by your own knowledge?
